THE RED PAGE.
THE WHITE FLOWERS CAME.
v.
’T was in the sweet month tremulous
With dancing joys that none may quell
The white flowers came upon the world:
They taught the old-time Parable.
Life looked so very sweet a thing :
The watchers wept, the lights burned low:
He moved, he felt the long, cold kiss,
And yet it seemed not good to go.
Two damsels, underneath the stars,
Lay listening on the cool green ground
Faint as the falling dew they heard
The song that spins the world around.
Strange bickering rose where Death and Life
Sat quarrelling on the green hillside :
Soft as a blessing spoke the bells,
Blue sky and blossoms for a bride.
Toiling and tired at close of day
A thin white widoiv woman prayed ;
In many a lighted town was told
The parable of man and maid.
’T was in the grey of eventime,
Cool earth and sky delightsome mild,
God’s pity came. In. tears he saw
The blue eyes of a little child.
S.N.
FLOTSAM
From a letter of Chas. Lamb’s :
SJehard. knew my blind side when he pitched upon
brawn. ’Tis of all my hobbies the supreme in the eating
way. He might have sent sops from the pan, skimmings
orumplets, hog s lard, the tender brown judiciously
scalped from a fillet of veal (dexterously replaced by a
salamander), the tops of asparagus, fugitive livers, run-
away gizzards of fowls , the eyes of martyred pigs, tender
effusions of laxative woodcocks, the red spawn of lobsters,
leverets’ ears, and such pretty filchings common to cooks ;
but these had been ordinary presents, the everyday cour-
tesies of dishwashers to their sweethearts. Brawn was a
noble thought.
A. novel by Swinburne is announced, Lbve's
Cross-Currents. “Better not.”
In the long miles of the Brit. Museum, some-
body has found a yard of MS. relative to Omar
K.; date of 1592. The writer is Mahmud al
Husaini (and bother his accents). The discoverer
says a version of the last two lines seems to be :
I said to him, “ What are Khayyam’s writings ? ”
He replied, “ Wrong calculations, and some raptures."
This is plainly a mistranslation. It should be
“Wrong raptures and some calculations.”
July Nineteenth Century (an especially good
number) contains an interesting note on the secret
history of the Berlin Treaty as given by Lord
Rowton, Disraeli’s private secretary. Rowton
mentions that all his life Disraeli was ‘‘ a very
poor man.”
A curious point about him was that, whereas in the
early part of his life he was v.ery fond of jewelry, he died
with hardly any. Lord Rowton said he only leit a single
set of onyx studs, and a watch without a chain ; while at
the time of his death he was not even possessed of an
umbrella.
Another uncommonly good article deals with the
condition of Ireland, which grows worse yearlv.
One alarming social fact was shown in the 1901
census report, where it was stated that
The total number of lunatics and idiots returned in
1851 was equal to a ratio of 1 in 657 of the population ; in
1861 to 1 in 411 ; in 1871 to 1 in 328 ; in 1881 to 1 in 281 •
in 1891 to 1 in 222 ; and in 1901 to 1 in 178.
The writer’s (Lord Dunraven) summary follows :
(1) The wealth of Ireland, as proved by income-tax
returns, by taxed salaries of officials in the employ of
municipalities and public companies, by the wage-earning
capacity of the labouring classes, by the marriage and
birth rate, and by all other tests, is, as compared with
the wealth of Great Britain, out of proportion to the
relative amount of taxation paid by the people of the two
islands. —[ 1.e., Ireland is overtaxed.]
(2) The best of the population is still flowing outward
from Ireland, and seeking a future outside the British
Empire, 89 per cent, of Irish emigrants settling in foreign
countries.
(3) Tiie excess of births over deaths is still so small as
to point to, on the one hand, physical deterioration of a
most alarming character, and, on the other, to an absence
of a due proportion of able-bodied persons remaining in
the country. .
(4) The emigration of the most physically and mentally
fit, and the hopeless life which is led by the largest sec-
tion of the people of Ireland, are resulting in an increase
of lunacy which is proving a scourge to the land.

So that, by the race-test, the “ colonial ” is by 149

H. G. Wells exhibits himself to much better
advantage in Kipps —the story of “a draper’s
soul.” Fragment from the last serial instalment :
“ Here, after all, was the sort of back a woman
can get behind.” That (with corollary) shows a
perfect comprehension of Woman's inmost neces-
sity, whatever her uttermost ideals.

Methuen and Co. send a selection of volumes in
their new . “ Standard Library,” which includes
Jane Austen’s Sense arid Sensibility, M. Aurelius’
Meditations, Shakespear’s works, and T. a
Kempis’ Imitation of Christ. The vols. appear in
paper at 6cZ. net, and in cloth at Is. net; and do
really set a standard. Type and paper are
admirable ; but a fault in all the copies supplied
is that the -type is insufficiently inked. The
Shakespear vol., in particular, is so “grey” that
in places it is difficult to read. If that fault were
mended, one would call the blue-cloth vols. the
best (and to book-lovers the most satisfying) yet
devised by publishers of cheap books.
Hirschfeld Bros., educational publishers, of
Holborn, Lond., forward several instruction-books that
measure the improvement in methods of teaching lan-
guages. An liny.-Herman Grammar (revised edn. 1905;
&>•.) follows Hossfeld’s method of providing four pages for
each lesson : a page of grammar, a page of vocabulary and
exercise, a conversational page and a page of reading.
The system is good, and the lessons well arranged. There
are several appendices, and the book as a whole can be
heartily commended. The features of t.n Advanced
German Header (its. net) are the intrinsic interest
of the extracts in prose and verse, and the
modern vogue of authors represented. The valu-
able biographical notes should be revised for Eng.
style: the present text is inconsequent (Novalis), or
pleonastic (Peter), or ungrammatical (Pfeffel). A New
Eng. and French Vocabulary (its. (id. net) contains
18,000 words, including new phrases, technical words,
and special usages for which one hunts dictionaries in
vain. For reader’s reference and for increasing one’s
knowledge of idiomatic French it is the best book of its
class we fit. /e seen.
The Butler report on Eng. az my corruption and
the plunder of the African war-stores should have
had for epigraph a quotation from Gibbon :
George, from his parents or his education, surnamed
the Cappadocian, was born at Epiphania in Cilicia, in a
fuller’s shop. From this obscure and servile origin he
raised himself by the talents of a parasite: and the
patrons, whom he assiduously flattered, procured for
their worthless dependent, a lucrative commission, or
contract, to supply the army with bacon. His employ-
ment was mean ; he rendered it infamous. He accumu-
lated wealth by the basest arts of fraud and corruption ;
but his malversations werh so notorious, that George was
compelled to escape from the pursuits of justice...
The odious stranger, disguising every circumstance of
time and place, assumed the mask of a martyr, a saint,
and a Christian hero ; ahd the infamous George of Cappa-
docia has been transformed into the renowned St. George
of England, tlie patron of arms, of chivalry, and of the
garter.
Patron of arms and corrupt contractor for armies
—St. George for England ! Hip, hip, hooray !
SAID THE STILL-RUNNER
TIME has hung rather heavy on my hands
lately, the new sergeant and trooper being
raw, and afraid to venture into the gullies :
so, laying aside the faithful old Winchester,
sighted to 200 yards for callers’ benefit, I have
amused myself noting down a few memories
which may be useful to beginners.
Sydney is bounded on the east by the sea, on
the north and south (blow the west) by broken
sandstone country, designed by the Almighty
for but one end—running stills. On the Hawkes-
bury, and in the ranges and gullies from George’s
River to Bulli, the trade has become hereditary ;
though, alas, prices are not what they were. It
is true that the police are as sleepy and timid as
ever ; but this does not make up for the cheapen-
ing of the finished article. A few years ago, one
could readily get 20s. a gallon for rum, represent-
ing 18s. protit ; now one is glad to take 15s. Yet
it is still (excuse the jest) an easy living.
But younger generations do not give the care to
their work that they should do. When I was in
Bellingen, on the N. coast, about twelve months
ago, 1 saw a bullocky drive into a pub. yard
there with a cargo under a load of potatoes. I
made the sign of the trade to him, and sampled
his stuff after dark, when he had unloaded.
Simply vile ! The law should put down such
amateurs. No wonder the North Coast cockys
have such a bad name.
There are three sorts of stillers—the profes-
sionals, like myself; the off-and-ons, who only
work occasionally; an d the amateurs. The first
rarely get caught; the second sometimes; and the
third always. The last-named are a most useful
class : they keep the police from meddling with
us. The professional works either alone, or with
one mate ; the casual with a couple or three ; and
the amateur alone, or with up to a dozen.
Three*conditions we all must have : secresy, a
good supply of cool running water, and a way to
get rid of the fire-smoke. For situation, it is a
matter of taste; personally, 1 prefer always to
work in the gullies. On the other hand, some of
the best of our profession run their stills about
Botany and Redfern. The casuals are generally
selectors who have wild country e:i their back
boundary, and can’t make their places pay other-
wise. The amateurs are broken-down chemists or
doctors, who make it anywhere that is handiest.
Our working plant is simple; a pot with a
(removable) domed top; a “worm,” or many-
twisted spiral of copper-tubing ; a big open pot,
and a few bags of brown sugar, or a cask of
treacle, according to fancy. The process is also
simple :_the domed pot is first filled almost brim--
(.., lof either treacle or sugar-and-water; the dome
is fixed on it; the mouth of the worm is then care-
fully fixed to the point of the dome. The other
worm-mouth stands over the second empty pot.
the domed pot must stand over a tire just
1 , enough to keep it constantly boiling ; and a
stream of cold watei* must runover the worm, to con-
dense the steam. When condensed, the steam runs
out in oily drops into the second pan, cooled in a
similar way. Drawn off, the sturf is as white as
milk, and as strong as rackarock ; the smell from
it will stupefy anyone not used to it. I have
known it to bleach a half-caste’s hair (temporarily,
of course). It is sold to the publican like
this ; he waters it down about 2UO per cent—and
you see where his profit comes in.
Getting rid of tne stuff is supposed to be the
worst part of the job. Rats ! The go-between
takes all the risks; last Tuesdav one from Wol-
longong offered to take 100/. v. orth a week from
me, and drive within half-a-mile of the still to
collect. I think he wants it for cordial-makers’
stuff. Our trouble is getting the raw material
out to the still; to hump a treacle-tin or 701 b. bag
down over rocks and across creeks tries the back
and temper. Humping the finished article up out
of the gully in a little barrel, or preferably a port-
manteau with a tinned inside, is much easier. I
don’t like wearing sandshoes myself, as they
sweat my feet; still, in our business, you must do
it : they leave no tracks.
There are several ways of forwarding. In work-
ing many of the gullies on the George’s River
(salt-water) we find it best to boat the stuff down
to Botany on moonlight nights. If towards Bulli,
send it down the Pass under produce; if near
Appin, ship it as milk from Campbelltown. On
the Hawkesbury, send it round in a ketch under
firewood. All my stuff at present is going to
Cataract dam ; but I won’t say how.
I have had some shaves of being caught. Was
taking in a prime lot once from Appin to Camp-
belltown, under a load of wood ; at the last minute
my brother asked me to deliver five tins of honey
there, too. I shoved them on top of the stingo,
under the wood, to keep the sun off them. Half-
a-mile out of Campbelltown the sergeant stopped
me ; he must have seen the top tin shine through
the wood. “ What have you got there, Falder ? ”
“Only honey,” I told him, looking as inno-
cent as the bullocks. “I’m fond of honey,” he
says—“ let’s taste it.” He tried the five tins- —all
honey. “You’ve saved your bacon this time,” he
says; “and I’ll have you yet.” But he never did !
Another time the trooper rode rigl.: on top of
the rock where I was working. Like all our
shops, it was a sandstone cave blocked up rough
with stone. He sat there looking about till his
horse got a bit lively (I expect lie felt the fire). I
watched him from the gateway at the back ; but
didn’t pull trigger because I saw he was only look-
ing at the view. He rode away a little after. I
never saw him again. When he reads this, he’ll
know how near eternity lie was.'
It is best to shoot strangers only when they’re
meddlesome. (I had to droji one on the Hawkes-
bury about twelve months ago, back of Wiseman’s
Jerry.) people are born without sense,
though, and will come poking about. From the
main road they see a thin line of smoke coming
up out of one of the gullies off the main range.
“ Must be a still!” they say, and come poking
over to look at it. We see them from our
look-out a couple of hundred yards off; douse any
embers, slip into our back gateway and watch.
They poke along till right up to it, walk past and
back half-a-dozen times, then go away grumbling.
Sometimes one stumbles on it: then he drops.
You can walk p'ast a still a hundred times out
here and not find it,-even if you know it’s there.
Ihe waterworn sandstone rocks, oh the gully sides,
run back under forty feet sometimes; and a few
growing' bushes and fallen (?) at/'' ',es in the front
make a natural screen. Then there’s always a
trickle of water soaking through from the hill-top ;
a little passage for a get-away is easily cut out of
the side. Two infallible ways of finding a still
are, the smoke and the scummy water which runs
off the worm after cooling it. I don’t know why
it should be foul like that, running off the hot
copper ; but it is.
Jt
The law about stills is that the informer gets
50/. if the full still is caught. It costs the stiller
from 100/. to 400/. each time he is caught,
‘‘according to circumstances” (previous convic-
tions). He can afford to be caught every twelve
months, too. A still is not a still unless the -worm
is there. Our first care, when we leave by the
back, is to take the worm with us. We generally
keep it in a hollow stump, covered over with dead
leaves, when not in use.
I lost a still once very simply through that.
Had a party of friends out from Liverpool on
Boxing Day, to see the show working. They
borrowed a basin from me to heat some soup in ;
afterwards, not thinking, stuck it, grease and all,
on top of the worm, and filled the stump-hole up
with leaves. The stump had fine green suckers
growing up from it, too. Police came out next
day (someone had informed for spite); they found
the still all right, but couldn’t strike the worm.
The sergeant had a pet retriever with him. Just
as I saw them go to ride away, the brute started
scratching at the stump; he smelt the dish, of
course. Even then they didn’t drop till Carlo got
impatient, and made a dive through the. suckers ;
he fell on top of the dish and worm, and made
them rattle like blazes. Even a policeman knows
that tin-cans aren’t indigenous ; so they got it.
As a set-off, I once worked a still in a swamp
just off the river, towards Como, for about five
years, till it was only fit for scrap iron; then went
and informed on it, and got fifty quid. I had a
lark once with a ’tec, too, at Liverpool. Got up
two fine casks of treacle, and found this brute
watching them ; so I put them on a dray in broad
daylight, and drove out about five miles from
town into a thick patch of scrub. Dumped them
there, and drove back to town, leaving the tec
watching them—and a mate watching him. Left
them there for a couple of day's in the rain, till
the’tec had to quit for food ; and in his absence
carted the casks back to the main road, where I
had another cart waiting to carry them to the still.
The ’tec got home, and died of inflammation of
the lights, or something (I forget the exact details
of his demise).
Another time we shipped a record cargo by train
from Minto, on the Southern line. Got an order
upjn the morning for stuff to go down that night
without fail. Had a big lot of it read}', as it hap-
pened ; packed in treacle tins, honey tins, lager-
bier ‘ ases—in fact, anything that would hold.
Must have had half a ton on the cart when I
dashed out of the bush as the train slowed in.
The outfit smelt like a chemist’s shop as we banged
it into the van. (1 hope none of the guards
sampled it on the way down.) It got there all
right; and we got a big cheque up next day for it.
My advice to those thinking of going stilling is
“Do; by all means.” It keeps tue police from
pestering me ; and the fine for a first offence won’t
hurt you.
FALDER.
JETSAM
Donald Murray, of Sydney, forwards copy of a
very able and interesting paper, read before the
British Institution of Electrical Engineers in
February last. The subject is “Setting Type
by Telegraph.” Mr. Murray has invented a
S y S t e m (claimed to be the simplest extant) by
w bich a message printed on a typewriter at one
end of a wire is automatically duplicated on
another typewriter at the other end. The system
is fuilv explained in the inventor’s paper, which
is admirably illustrated. One agrees with the
commentator who said that “the paper is a
model, and an example of logical and orderly
setting out of a beautiful invention. ’ For long-
distance work, the system, though still in its
infancy of application, seems particularly adapted,
Mr. Murray says, for example, that it is un-
doubtedly practicable with the aid of the Murray
kevboard perforator, the Brown cable relay and
perforator, and the Murray printer, to work a
typewriter in Hew York by playing on a key-
board m London The invention and the descrip-
tion of the invention are things that one is glad to
find springing from an Australian source,
An Amer. writer kindly says that “there is
something provincial in the attitude of a man such
as p ro fessor Jebb, who looks with contempt on
Zealand and the New Zealanders because
they are supposed to speak of ‘Cupid and Sich,’
w hile he himself is doubtless completely ignorant
Q f the Socialistic movement among that people,
w hich in the future history of the world will exert
an influence out of comparison with the pretty
f a b]es of antiquity.”
RECENTLY FUBLISHRD
The Queens Progress and Other Elizabethan
Sketches, by F. E. Schelling (Lond. ; T. W.
Laurie, 10s. net), is a book handsomely printed ;
and the included portraits of Q. Elizabeth, Ben
Jonson, Drummond of Havvthornden, and others
are especially admirable. The sketches represent
the lighter freight of the author’s antiquarian
researches, and may enthral the elect. One meets
curious glimpses and anecdotes:
Women first appeared as actresses on the stage about
the time of the Restoration, and in a few years the hoy
actor was a thing of the past. Francis Kynaston is
reputed to have been the last male actor to appear in
female parts. Kynaston was, famed for his delicate
beauty; and it is of him that Cibber relates the story
that King Charles, coming earlier than was expected to
the playhouse, became impatient that the play did not
begin, until .nformed that the queen was unhappi’- not
yet shaven.
There is a picture of the last sad days o f i obert
Greene, with this pathetic note to the w.io he had
wronged :
“ Sweet Wife: As ever there was any good will or
friendship between thee and me, see this bearer, my host,
satisfied of his debt: I owe him ten pound, and but for
him I had perished in the streets. Forget and forgive
my wrongs done unto thee, and Almighty God have
mercy on my soul. Farewell till we meet in heaven, for
on earth thou shalt never see me more. This 2of
September, 1592. Written by thy dying husband, Robert
Greene.”
The musicianly poets of Elizabeth’s age are the
subject of a chapter. Of the most notable of
them, Thomas Campion, Schelling writos :
The poetry of Campion is saturated with Catullus ; and
it shares in the Roman poet’s sweetness, sensuoueness,
and mellifluous flow of musical words. Campion is not
wholly a poet of love, although he lavishes on Venus’s
altars his richest and loveliest fruits. There is a purity
and simple childlike fervour, a genuine singing quality
and happy mastery of phrase in the more serious of his
Bongs, that raise Campion measurably above the chorus
of amourists and dainty gilded sonneteers who rise and
fall in a singing swarm among the sallows of the Eliza-
bethan garden of Love.
The last essay in the book accompanies Ben
Jonson on his famous journey to Hawthornden.
The volume will be agreeable to local men of
letters.
The National Art Library of the Victoria and
Albert Museum, South Kensington, London,
publishes an interesting catalogue of the Library’s
fine collection of Whistler etchings, lithographs,
and engravings. A useful bibliography is added.
Australian admirers of Whistler, going to London,
will find it worth while to pay a special visit to
this Library.
' The catalogue of Vic. Artists’ Society’s Winter
Exhibition is prefaced by a capital account of “The
Beginnings of Victorian Art,” written by W. Moore. The
society may be congratulated upon the engraving and
printing of the handsome illus. to the catalogue : which
numbers 236 items (including six statuettes, four minia-
tures, and an occasional chair). The S.A. Society of Arts
is strongly represented. Pictures are priced from 10s. 6d.
to 4411., which is asked for Blamire Young’s view of Tenny-
son reading “ Maud ” to his friends, with a dozen por-
traits of pre-Raphaeiites and others.
A very interesting international exhibition of
process engraving was held recently at South
Kensington Museum (Lond.). Australian work
might have- been represented; and local en-
gravers should secure a copy of the cata-
logue (Wymans, Fetter Lane; 9 d.) The in-
troduction to the catalogue is a succinct his-
torical and critical summary of engraving pro-
cesses, by Major Waterhouse: full of valuable
detail. The section on photographic colour-
printing is especially suggestive.

A HARROWING TALE OF SUFFERING AND A
THANKFUL TRIBUTE FROM ULTIMO.
The Case of Mr. V. BQRWICK,
(■By a Sydney Reporter.)
Mr. Victor Borwick is a storekeeper
who has his business premises and re-
sidence at 286 and 288 Crown-road,
Ultimo, and although he could have
been found at this address any time
during the past eleven years,it was not
till lately that a reporter had occasion
to pay his establishment a visit, “the
reason for calling/’ as the scribe ex-
plained, “being to get some informa-
tion respecting a widely-known recov-
ery that happened to come within
hearing.”
“Ah ! That is six years ago now
since I got all right again/’ Air. Bor-
wick remarked, “and I think it was
about up to me to have a change of
luck, considering that I had been suf-
fering for fully three years. The pains
that ran along my gums and reached
up to the top of my head wero some-
thing terrible, and half my time I did
not know what I was doing. My ad-
versities set in when I was ‘running
the plank,’ as they call it, on a boat
discharging blue metal, and I can as-
sure you that J was so broken down
in health that I had to put another man
on to do my work about every other
week. Talk about neuralgia ! I’m sure
nobody could have it worse than I
had, and as I found such a grand cure
for it the least T can do is to let hu-
manity know all about it, for I dare-
say my experience will prove very
helpful. Well, out of all the medicines
I tried to cure neuralgia the only one
that was of any lasting benefit was
Clements Tonic, and when I say that
after keeping going with that remedy
for a few weeks the fearful pains all left
me and have never returned all these
years, it is the honest truth I am tell-
ing you.”
“Is that the only ailment you have
had ?”
“Indeed, no. All the time the neural-
fic tortures were at me I had other
roubles to deal With, for my diges-
tion was just as bad as it could be.
Both from want of appetite, and also
owing to the pains about my jaws,
eating was almost out of the ques-
tion ; but what was still more unfortu-
nate was the fact that the little I did
force myseli to take to keep body and
soul together had the unhappy habit
of disagreeing with me. And you must
remember that I was getting no sleep.
Neither night nor day—for weeks at a
stretch sometimes—could 1 get any
rest, and, as you may well imagine,
the result was disastrous. I got so
terribly weak that ultimately I had to
give up my work altogether, and mv
body was so denuded of flesh that my
clothes hung about me like a sack.
Indeed, 1 was just going to have some
of them altered about the time that I
started on Clements Tonic, and by
some means or other the idea was
shelved for a while, for Which I was
afierwards very glad, as in a few
months I was as stout as ever. Yes i;
it was quite a matter for regular com-
ment how well I was looking, and,
thank God ! I felt well, tbo. For three
years previous to using Clements To-
nic I was nothing but a total wreck,
for every part of my body was ad-
versely affected in somo way or other.
It was my first experience of indiges-
tion, and T sincerely trust it will be
my last, for I would rather be dead
and out of the world altogether than
go through such misery again, t got to
feel as shaky and uncomfortable as if
everything in my inside was going to
pieces, and was so disturbed and un-
settled mentally that the least thing
mad© me irritable. My eyes always
felt heavy and swollen to such an ex-
tent that 1 could scarcely see to read,
and I felt so discontented and restless
that 1 could not stay indoors. Conse-
quently I often wandered about out-
side hardly knowing what to do with
myself, feeling as though death would
be a thousand times more welcome
than the wretched life I was leading,
for I was in agony everywhere. The
pains in my chest were really cruel,,
and I thought I had strained myself
or broken something between my
shoulders owing to the pain that was
there. It was like getting hold of a
piece ot meat, and tearing it in two —
that was the kind of feeling 1 had be-
tween the shoulders, and my loins
ached badly into the bargain."
“And have you never had a return
of any oi thoso symptoms during the
past six years 1”
“Mo, upon my honour ; and I have
not had that nasty, bulged-out feeling
in the stomach either after eating my
food. Mo ; Clements Tonic removed
everything, even to the bitter taste of
a morning and the fur that used to
cover my tongue, besides which it gave
me permanent relief from the many at-
tacks of giddiness that I had pre-
viously been subjected to. How much
my nerves accounted for the misery I
had endured is hard to say definitely,
but they undoubtedly had their fair
share of responsibility, for I must tell
you that 1 was so highly nervous that
I could not stand the least excitement
without getting all of a tremble, and I
had also contracted an uncontrollable
practice of measuring the future witn
nothing but trouble and worry in
store. All I could see before me was a
continuance of my suffering only to be
added to by misfortunes of other de-
scriptions, and now that you under-
stand the gloomy frame of mind I wa»
in you will readily conceive why 1
speak so highly of Clements Tonic for
lifting me from that sorry state to
the happy and healthy condition. I ami
now blessed with. It was through
reading somebody's testimonial that 1
decided to give Clements Tonic a trial,
and my earnest hope is that other
sufferers, for their sake, will be so in-
fluenced when they read mine, and
then l shall consider I hav© performed
a valuable service to the community.
1 can give Clements Tonic a special
word in respect to its invigorating in-
fluence over my nervous system. It
cleared every trace of nervousness
right away, and as an appetiser it
was simply marvellous, bat ! Why,
when once I got going I hprdly knew
when to stop, so no wonder I soon
Idled my clothes out again. But it
was all good, sound flesh I put on,
plenty of strength and vigour behind
it, and after using several bottles of
Clements Tonic, there was hardly an
ache anywhere to mar the wonderful
cure I had made. What little discom-
fort there was left after using my first
lot of Clements Tonic was soon dis-
persed by my continuance of the same
remedy, and if anybody doubts the
completeness of my recovery all they
hav© to do is to drop in and see. me,
and I will give them my experience
verbally from end to end." .
“And in the meantime may I circu-
late your story ?”
“You are at liberty to publish it m
any conceivable style you may wish a -
STATUTORY DECLARATION'.
I, VICTOR BORWICK, of 286-288 Crown
ftoad. Ultimo, in the State of New South
Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,’ do
soleihnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my lull permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment: and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of tho Oaths Act, 1960.
07 .
Subscribed and declared at Ultimo this
twenty-third day of August, one thousand
nine hundred and four, before me,
W. J. KYLE, J.P,

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I am Old.
When I am old,
When all the springs of life run dry,
And joy no longer wings my feet;
When laughter breaks off with a sigh,
And shadows fill the darkened street,
When I am old.
When I am old,
And Love no longer comes my way,
Nor Friendships claim me as their own;
When life grows greyer day by day,
And pulse and heart are cold as stone,
When I am old.
When I am old,
And locks are white instead of gold,
Wrinkled and brown the skin of snow,
The face is as a tale long told,
The lissome form bent like a bow,
When I am old.
When I am old
Then all the tides of feeling ebb,
No longer passion, sorrow, pain
Await me in Fate’s future web;
The curse is as the curse of Cain,
When I am old.
When I am old
An alien in the homely throng,
A changeling child to guard with care,
A thing remote from right or wrong,
A worn-out frame no longer fair,
When I am old.
When I am old,
And dull to hear and slow of speech,
A mark for Youth’s impatient speed ;
A thought didactic, given to preach
The fragments of a bygone creed,
When I am old.
When I am old
The great emotions are no more,
Nor love nor sorrow, joy nor pain.
Food, and a fire by which to pore
Back to the animal again,
When I am old.
When I am old
And laggard Death arrives at last
Some decent tears, to hide relief,
Fall on a relic of the past,
A derelict beyond all grief,
When I am old.
N.S.W.
C.E.S.
The Time That’s Here To-Day.
Now, I’m sick of all the story that I’ve heard the
bush-bards humming,
As they sit, like Patience, smiling on a monument
at Grief,
’Bout the days of want and worry, and the good
times that are coming,
And the clouds with silver linings all trimmed up
for our relief.
All this “cheer up, brother,” gospel, and the cant
of “bright to-morrows”
Seems to set my dander rising when I meet them
on my way,
For I reckon that the future, given all its balm of
sorrows,
Isn’t worth a simple fraction of the Time we’ve
got to-day!
What’s it matter if we’re chasing pennyweights
for clothes and tucker
While the other fellow’s tearing nuggets from a
ten-ounce ditch ?
It’s his luck —without the miners there would be
no room for truckers,
And there’d be no miners either s’posing no one
struck it rich.
All this chat about the harvest that some season’s
going to bring us
Seems to me a sort of funking of the tail-end of
the plough;
And if good things come by waiting, well, I guess
the gods will fling us
Just a few of them —to kid us—if we hustle for
them now !
When I tumbled out this morning I’ll admit the
world looked gloomy
(Always seems to when you’re starting on the day
by candle-light);
And my dungarees were clammy, and the sky was
grey and spumey,
And I overturned the billy—seemed like nothing
would go right.
But the bell-birds started piping in the wet bush
all around me,
And my blood rushed quicker than it will a hun-
dred long years hence,
And the very pride of living burst the puny cords
that bound me;
My dreams may be the future’s, but my life’s the'
present tense!
I suppose to-day we’re reaping what we sowed in
bygone ages.
If we gloried in the tilling need we grumble at the
crop ?
I suppose the future, somehow, has been writ in
earlier pages,
We shall sin, and live and linger, and will ripen,
fret and drop.
So I’m sick of all this story that I hear the bush-
bards humming,
Of the days of want and worry, as they moon
along their way;
For I reckon that the future, given all its “ good
times coming,”
Isn’t worth a glowing fraction of the Time that’s
here to-day 1
WESTKALIA.
T. H. WILSON.

** Ruberoid Roofing is admirable/' There are a number

THURSDAY, AUGUST 10, 1905.
Pkick 6d.
A DUPLICATE PARLIAMENT.
After looking at the notice paper of N.S.W. Assembly “The Bulletin” is convinced that what is most required is two State Parliaments.
The grave-looking gathering in the lower left-hand corner passes laws. The other one attends to the bad language, the sectarian rows and the
motions for adjournment, does any drinking that is necessary, asks questions, makes personal explanations, and demands select committees to
inquire into the reason why railway ganger Jones spoke harshly to Smith in 1902.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 10, 1905.
ASSISTED IMMIGRATION.
A few days ago Dr. Aetiiue, M.L.A., pounded
the gong of warning and blew fiercely on the
trumpet of admonition in a Sydney hall under the
lamentable auspices of the “ British Empire
League.” His text was that Australia is in
danger, and on that text many bitter sermons can
fitly be preached. Australia is in deadly danger.
It is the most solitary outpost of the white man.
It has a mere shred of population, and it is an
object of envy to at least one powerful State, and
that an Asiatic State. It has no navy to defend
it and not much of an army. For want of a navy
and at least two transcontinental railways, fully
half its coast can be more easily attacked from
.Japan than defended from Australia; Port Darwin
is three weeks sail from Nippon, and from three
to six months’ tramp from the end of the nearest
railway that can bring up men from the two
strongest States—Victoria and N.S.W. And on
top of all this, Australia is a country almost
without arms and ammunition, without iron and
steel works, without means to make its own rifles,
artillery and cartridges, without mills to supply
its people with clothing when outside supplies are
cut off, without a hundred different kinds of
manufactures which a country that is so liable to
be cut off should have. It is even a land that
doesn’t make its own railway rolling-stock except
to a very small extent, and what it does is largely
a mere fitting together of imported parts. It
is so overwhelmed with debt that it could
hardly raise a war-loan at 10 per cent, interest
to meet an emergency. And its dependence
is on Britain 16,000 miles away Britain
where the army is admittedly one great dry-rot,
and where the navy is being called in from all
parts for the security of the imperial capital, just
as the legions were called in when Rome began to
lose confidence in its destiny as the mistress of the
world.
* * » *
Under these circumstances, it is no wonder that
a call begins to arise for assisted immigration.
Australia wants men and women and children of
the white race—for preference English, Irish,
Scots, Scandinavians, Danes, Hollanders, Ger-
mans, and Frenchmen—the races that lead the
world in courage and intelligence, and that have
filled history with the records of what they can do
in time of trouble. Australia is the remotest
white man’s country that these Europeans
can resort to,, and in many cases the
cost of coming here is prohibitive. Therefore
it is quite possible that Australia will have to take
to assisted immigration again on a large scale—a
1 .rger scale than ever before. There is no reason
why Australia should not go in for assisted immi-
gration on a large scale, provided always that it
pays the assistance out of revenue, and that it
provides abundance of work for the immigrants.
When the country practically buys immigrants it
gains the right to select its immigrants— to pick
those who are sound in mind and body, indus-
trious, of good character, and acquainted with
some useful trade. Assuming that such immi-
grants are brought put here at £l2 per head, each
one of them—always provided that there is pro-
fitable work waiting for him on arrival—at once
shoulders some £57 of our famous and wonderful
f r> lC That means a profit to Australia
of £45 in one act—provided it has profitable
work for the immigrant to do. The same new
arrival at once begins to be a taxpayer at the
rate, even allowing for the fact that there are
many taxes from which the poor are exempt, of,
say, £2 per head-provided the country has
profitable work for him to do so that he can pay
taxes. That represents a dividend of fully 16 per
cent, on the money invested in him. The profit
is magnificent—again provided that Australia has
profitable work awaiting its immigrant.
* * * *
But these results cannot be achieved by the
well-worn joke of “ putting them on the land.”
ihe immigrant who has hardly anything save his
muscle, his grit, and a certain amount of intelli-
gence and that is the kind of man who mostly
wants to leave his own country—is quite unable to
go on the land unless the State finds him
transportation, seed, tools, material for fencing
and house-building, and food and clothes for hi m-
self and his family for a year, or, perhaps, two
years, if the land has to be cleared. His taxes
would also have to be remitted for a time, and
there is always a chance that he would fail at first
through ignorance of our droughty conditions, and
need more assistance. To bring out immigrants
to build up the manufactures that we so badly need
might cost £l2 per head ; to bring out these same
immigrants and put them on the land would pro-
bably cost £5O per head. Moreover, to cry out for
putting men on the land because our country is in
danger is the mere habit of the parrot that has got
its meaningless sentence by rote. If Australia
falls before some foreign invader, want of food
will never be the primary cause of its fall.
It will fall for the want of a navy and an
army, for want of shipbuilding yards, steel and
iron works, arms and ammunition factories, for
want of works where it can make its own rails and
railvvay .material so as to keep its lines of com-
mumcation open, for want of its two transconti-
nental railways so that it can convey troops to
outlying points of danger, for want of factories
where they make clothing and the material for
clothing—for want, in short, of almost everything
except food. It is a sorry joke, because Australia
is unarmed, and unprepared, to propose to merely
rush in men to grow more wheat, which can only
be sold at a low price in a precarious market
16,000 miles away, and which, if the command of
the sea is lost, as it must be before Australia will
be in serious danger, cannot be sold at all. Yet
Dr. Arthur, M.L.A., when he delivered liis stir-
ring address last week, had nothing more than
this to propose. And after talking about bringing
m some five or six million people in the next 20
years, the only suggestion he had to make as
regards finding employment for all this host was
the unutterable bathos of setting aside “ alternate
W' ™ n scru h country of
* * * *
The fact that Australia wants many more strong
white citizens of a fighting stock is taking hold of
this country. It is so obvious a fact that it must
take hold. And the first day that Britain is in
trouble, and the first day that Asia begins to
knock hard at our door and demand admittance,
what is now a calm, indifferent sort of belief will
become a panic-stricken conviction. It is better
to move now and not wait for the panic. In any
case, it is almost certain that a great immigration
movement will come. The question is whether it
is to be a Democratic movement or a Tory
movement. If the progressive party takes it up,
the first duty is to suppress the bulk of our imports
by a 50 per i cent, tariff; to create, by that and
every other possible means, every kind of industry
that the country is capable of, and as our own
unemployed won’t one-tenth fill the demand thus
created, to bring out, on wholesale lines, white men
of good character—British preferred, but only as a
matter of preference—to fill the want, paying their
passages, when necessary, out of revenue. Then
to feed these, and maintain a surplus of food for
export, it is necessary to open up' State lands
where the State has land in accessible and decently
watered country, and by a heavy graduated land-
tax and a compulsory resumption system to force
private estates on the market at a reasonable
price. It is not necessary to give away
the land, nor even to lend very much aid to
except by a State Land Bank system.
Where good, accessible, well-watered country
is available on fair terms, and there is a
market for the produce, the independent settler
has never yet been wanting, and until he is
wanting, the question of giving away the land
doesn’t arise. Such an immigration policy might
double Australia’s population in a very few years.
It would enable Australia to show the world that
it is possible, with progressive legislation, to have
manufactures without having slums, paupers, and
deformities. It would reduce our unemployed
instead of increasing them. It would, in a
sense, halve the awful burden of the Public
Debt; by doubling the population without
adding to the aggregate liability the debt
per head is diminished 50 per cent. It would
vastly increase the revenue bv doubling the num-
ber of taxpayers, arid that “should provide the
means for creating a really serviceable navy, for
training the whole adult male population to arms,
and for building the two transcontinental railways
one of which, by making the Northern Territory
a splendid asset instead of a bankrupt dead
weight, would more than pay for itself, provided
Australia had people to settle in the Territory.
And Australia, under these conditions, wouid
have men to form an army and man a fleet, and
would manufacture within its own borders all the
essentials of defence.
* * * *
That is the prospect of a great assisted immi-
gration scheme run on Democratic principles, and
with the supply of abundant and profitable work
for the people who are here already, and for the
people who are to come, set down as the first
essential Unfortunately, the Labor party in
politics shows no sign of moving, and the Demo-
cratic party in politics which is outside the Labor
ranks is not strong enough to force any movement
by itself, bo the immigration theory is falling
into the hands of Tories, Freetraders, big landl-
ords, importers, Jingoes, flag-flappers, nigger-
labor men, sweaters, borrowers and Empire
League people. They are pasting their advertise-
me,nt,on a i tl( l using it as their especial property
and how valuable a property it-may be to them
sh ? w - . If a day of real panic
comes, and the immigration movement is by
that time firmly associated with Toryism
it may be sufficient of an advertisement
to bring a very bad kind of Toryism into
power all over Australia. The idea of the
class which is now running the immigration theory
is, first of all, to resort to the old, bad, ruinous
dodge of borrowing money with which to pay the
passage-money of the new arrivals. The idea of
even putting up such a trifle as £250,000 a year to
help in making Australia stronger for defence is
beyond its petty, unpatriotic comprehension, and
its first inspiration is to load this incredibly mort-
gaged community with more debt. Most of it
wouldn’t do with one bangle less at dinner-time,
or expose one less knob of its spine in evening
dress, or pull through with one less flunkey, or one
less bottle of champagne, if its country were sink-
ing to perdition. Then it doesn’t contemplate stop-
ping imports and creating great manufactures. A
large section of it lives by imports, and a still larger
section recognises that the artisan class, which
a manufacturing policy would greatly increase,
is the strength and support of Labor in politics.
It hasn t the remotest dream of cutting up its
large estates to promote closer settlement. Its
idea is just to bring people in wholesale and turn
them loose. Some of them, in the struggle for a
crust, would cultivate its land cheaply. Some of
them would take up the rich landowner’s farms
at almost any rent which held out the prospect of
a margin, however small, for subsistence. And
when population was greatly increased by artificial
means, with no attempt to provide a corresponding
increase of employment, the rest of the new
population would provide cheap and servile
employees of all kinds. Incidentally the in-
creased population would do something to
supply increased means of defence, but
there is hardly a mention, in any utterance on the
subject, of serious military or naval preparation,
or of any attempt to put the country in a position
to manufacture its own means of defence. The
talk is all of more population—population that is
to hit the invader with a hoe, or throw a clod or a
turnip at him.
* * * *
■ ™ a “ att< ? r . of urgency that the Labor men
m f ederal politics should combine with the Demo-
cratic Protectionists and make the immigration
question, with the questions which hang on to it—
the creation of industries, the breaking up of
great estates, and the establishment of ade-
quate defences—their special weapon. If they
fail to do so the immigration question will
become their enemy’s special weapon. It is an
instrument which may be used in the interests of
land monopoly and low wages, or it may be used
to make Australia a country where there is a more
general diffusion of wealth and a higher standard
of comfort than anywhere else on earth. It is a
business that the Empire League push,
the Tories, importers, borrowers, land monopolists,
and the Stinking Fish party should have been
allowed to take up the cry in the first place, and
use it for their special ends. It is not much of a
cry now, but it may be a deafening one a very few
years hence.
THE LABOR PARTY ORGANISATION.
.The Queensland Labor members have issued a
formal plea for common sense in the methods of
Labor in politics, which will be cordially endorsed
by every Labor party in the Commonwealth. It
has been met with a savage denunciation by the
Queensland Labor paper, but that was to have
been expected. Queensland Worker, if it had
an y perception, would have long ago recognised
that it is on the wrong side on most democratic
questions from the frequency with which the Tory
papers throughout Australia quote its articles
to help their cause. But it either lacks
perception or is s'o vain of its shriek-
power that it must shriek even when every
yell means confusion to its friends and help
to its enemies. The essence of the Queensland
Labor party’s complaint is that its effectiveness
at the polls and in the House is hampered by the
intransigent and reckless attitude of the Political
Labor League conferences. The Queensland Labor
party has to stagger under the burden of an
“objective” which declares for complete Socialism,
though every man outside of a lunatic asylum
fqels that complete Socialism is not within the
range of this century’s practical politics; and
pleads for the modification of that “ objective ” to
the form adopted by the Inter-State Conference
for the Federal Labor party. Further, the Queens-
land party stresses the point that all the great
achievements of Labor in politics have been
effected by prudent alliances, which the Labor
League officials discredit and denounce.
» * * *
This matter of alliances is really the chief point
of difference now between the Labor parties in the
various Parliaments and the Political Labor
League officials. Truly all that has been attained
by Labor of any value has been by alliances.
Watson held office on alliance terms. Price
in S.A. and Daglish in W.A. are now in
power as Labor Premiers with the help of out-
side men. Queensland is being pulled out of the
bog in which a long course of Tory misrule left it
by an alliance between the Labor Party and the
Morgan following. Yet the inter-State Political
Labor Conference at its last session decreed that
no alliances must be entered into except under
obviously absurd and impossible conditions. This
decree, if it is persisted in, and if it is obeyed,
hamstrings Labor in politics! The men in Parlia-
ment cannot co-operate with any friendly section.
The tendency of sympathisers to cOme towards
them is penalised, for to be their friend is to
incur the hostility of Toryism, and to gain
none of Labor’s goodwill. But it is hard
to believe that it will be either persisted in or
obeyed. The great mass of the Labor voters is
with the Labor members in a desire to make the
Labor political movement a practically progressive
one. There are no extreme Socialists to speak of
among the Labor voters ; in N.S. Wales, for three
elections now, the extreme Socialists have run can-
didates of their own and each time scored only a
few dozen votes. Equally is the mass of Labor
voters opposed to any wild-cat Ishmael tactics in
Parliament by which the Labor party will put
itself in a pariah position and condemn itself to
perpetual opposition and perpetual friendlessness.
Practical progress is what the Labor in politics
movement calls for—not shadowy objectives and
hopeless tactics.
* * * *
Why is it, then, that, though the Labor members
and their constituents are the Labor political
movement and think practically the same way on
the points at issue, Labor League Conferences
decide the other way ? The reason is to be found
partly in bad organisation. In N.S.Wales, for
instance, the Labor party has a lien on, roughly,
30 electorates out of 90. Of the remaining 60
there are at least 40 which, to all appear-
ances, will never return a Labor member
while this generation of voters lives. Yet in
each of these 40 a Labor League can be
formed and can secure representation at the
League Conference. Such leagues can do little
t(5 help the movement. But they can'do much to
hinder by forcing on it wil'd -or- impracticable
views. _ Similarly, at the Inter-State Conference
Tasmania, which returns one Labor renreseui-mf, ’
to the federal Parliament, was represented by six
delegates-most of them not Tasmamansbut
Melbourne citizens, who represented nothing but
their own notions. Their votes were generallv
cast to thwart and hamper the Parliament-n-^
party. Additional to the faults due to an'mpeV
feet organisation there is of course, something
to be counted to the fact that the Labor
leaguer is only human— representing a higher
type than most of the rank and file of other
political organisations who, as a rule, to roll
up only when the beer begins to flow but still
only human And he very often cherishes an
ambition to be m Parliament himself, and that
sometimes affects Ins vote. An alliance involving
JLaboi support to a group of outside friends might
deprive a labor league official of a run for the very
seat he has an eye on. Insensibly he finds his view
against alliances strong. There is, too, the man
who isn t in Parliament, and doesn’t hope to be
in, but has a very exaggerated notion of the privi-
leges he confers on the man he helps to put in
and cannot see why that man shouldn’t regenerate
society at the rate of GO reform Acts a year His
want of knowledge makes it impossible for him to
understand the necessities of Parliamentary work
and he is ready to vote for any extreme proposal!
*** * '
The upshot is that, standing between the Labor
members find the Labor constituents, is a small
body ot political managers whose present attitude
threatens the whole' movement with disaster
Ihey insist—or a majority of them—on making
the platform impracticable and refuse to allow to
the Labor parties in Parliament even the
elementary right of deciding on their own tactics
in the Legislature. Clearly that state of things
cannot last. The men in Parliament owe it
to the movement as well as to themselves
to see that their work is effective. In
the last resort they should appeal to their con-
stituents—the Labor voters—over the heads
ot the Labor League’s officials. But that would
be an extreme and in most respects a regrettable
step, for those officials include many sterling
unselfish workers in the cause. Perhaps some
middle cou rse is possible in the direction of
making the Labor League Conferences more
truly representative—reflecting the strength of
the Labor vote rather than the tally of the States
or ot the electorates.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Swinburne’s Water Bill.
Thus a Victorian pressman :
A tall, thin, endless sort of man has been sitting- at the
table of the Victorian Assembly of late, with a huge docu-
rnent spread before him. The man is George Swinburne,
Minister of Water Supply, and the document is his Water
Bill. It is a toss-up whether the Victorian Parliament or
the Victorian community knows least about the said Bill.
It is a legislative anaconda, and contains over 300 clauses.
Probably no one apart from Swinburne has read it right
through ; but the long, thin, slotlike man has got every
clause off by heart. This Bill is so big and so valuable that
the intellect of the ordinary brand of State politician refuses
to button round it. Even Premier Bent admits that he
knows less about it than he knows of the inscription on
Noah’s tomb ; and Murray and Gillott and Cameron
and the other Ministerial cyphers admit that they only
know what Swinburne tells them.
To put it shortly, the Bill is one of the greatest
blows at the joss of Vested Interests that has ever
*« ***" v US J'- ralia '\ p° litics - It goes point blank
for the nationalisation of the water supply of Victoria.
It does it so honestly that the Vic. State House has
reared up and fallen back on itself in a confused heap,
and the Conservative doesn’t know' now' whether he is a
Tory or a Radical. It destroy s all private riparian rights
in one emphatic act. It says that the State shall own all
all, P on fch ? Premises, and shall apportion it as it
thinks fit, and that no Rich Person shall annex
any river or creek without payment merely because it
has the misfortune to meander through his proputty.
AH rights to water are to be vested in the “Crown,” and
. ro 'Y n 18 n °t to be separated from its moisture bv
anything short of dynamite. Persons who can prove that
they have used water for irrigation purposes for twenty
continuous years will be given 10 years’ further free use
as a bonus for their pertinacity, but the bonus of free
damp ends exactly there. The Victorian Water Trusts
whose financial histories read like an extra-bad balance
sheet of the Bank of Gomorrah, are informed that a large
hearse shall be provided on a certain date, and that the
trusts shall then arise as one corpse and be buried with-
out further argument. The language used by the Trust
fakirs has been simply shocking, and infuriated Shire
presidents have been bounding through the lobbies in
pursuit of Swinburne’s blood; but the long, spiderv
Minister sticks to his Bill. s> p y
It was absolutely necessary that this clean sweep
should be made. Knowing that all water dealt with from
this out is its own exclusive chattel, the State can erect
works with impunity ; and with the knowledge that its
carefullj’-matured plans are not to be burst up and
trampled upon by an ignorant potato-mayor. The State
is going to take over every cent of liability, and will get
square by charging the irrigated lands with a tax. The
Bill tries to get down to the unimproved land value, and
will be a great machine for the breaking up of bloated
estates. The State will peddle moisture to all and sundry ;
and the land-owner can either irrigate with the State
water and pay the tax, or else he can pay the tax and do
a perish. He has got to pay the tax any way. The value
of the land will increase with the population, and in time
the water supply will become a huge national asset.
Swinburne has equipped the “ Crown ” with a perfect
armada of powers and rights. The “ Crown ” may declare
any district an irrig-ation area without consulting the
land-owner, and if the land octopus doesn’t like it
he can make remarks in the background. Lands
are to be divided into three classes. The best are to pav
an irrigation tax of one-fifth of their net annual value
and the worst lands are to pay one-tenth. Intermediate
grades are to pay such rates as the Water Commission
may determine, but the rates shall not be more
or less than the figures mentioned. A permanent
Water Commission is to be appointed, and is
to have supreme control. Local management ceases
automatically with the proclamation of the Act. Under
Turner’s last Water Bill loans were advanced to Trusts
which were to pay 4 per cent, on the balance owing!
with a-half per cent, for a redemption fund. There being
J',° compound interest in Turner’s scheme, this meant
that the State would not get its money back for two
centuries. Swinburne proposes to provide for 44 per
cent, interest and redemption money—to be paid right
through. This will repay the State inside fifty-six years.
Turner’s finance leaked occasionally, but the man with
the long legs and the large, square mind is plugging up
the orifices in a thorough fashion.
The State has had awful trouble in connection with
conipensation for lands taken over for the purposes of
the Irrigation Department. Rapacious land-sharks have
dashed down upon the Department with demands for
thousands of pounds, and have finally taken a few
hundreds. One howled for £2013, and took £950.
Another wanted £5500, and took £667. Another de-
manded £3OO, and was overpaid with £5 ! But the shark
gets very little change out of Geoiioe Swinburne. Not
more than 10 per cent, above the market value is to be
paid for any lands resumed in, the future. The landowner,
who has sucked away the Government’s reservoirs and
has paid nothing, is to be dragged before the mag-istrate
and informed that he is a common defaulter.
There are some estates amounting to 30,000 acres in
extent which will come shortly under the operation of
Swinburne’s measure if it passes, and the irrigation tax
will, it is most probable, automatically sever them into
smaller areas. The only pity is that Swinburne’s Bill is a
State and not a Federal' affair. Federal legislation, on
the lines laid down by that thin, persistent man, would
be one of the greatest b-ws that Australia is likely to
receive this side of the .year 20-JO. " '


B. Smith’s New Idea.
Apparently Spruce Myth’s idea of himself and
his fellow members of the Australian Parliament is
that they are a sordid crowd, who only struggle
for office because of the extra money that comes a
Minister’s way. The result is a motion, to come
on next month, setting out that the House of
RSps. is of opinion that it should get £5OO a
year instead of £4OO, that Ministers should on y
get £lOOO instead of £1650, while the Prime
Minister’s £2lOO should come down to £1250.
Spruce Myth argues in his motion that it s a bad
thing for Australia to have these frequent changes
of Ministry, and that there wouldn t be so
many if the salaries were levelled off in this
fashion. And this is the Spruce Myth who,
for all these years, has been holding up
Socialism as a shameful, ruinous, unscientific, in-
equitable and generally blastiferous thing because
it tended towards a general dead-level m the
matter of pay ! This is he who has talked in w'oids
of seven syllables about the reward of industry,
and the fit and proper encouragement of enter-
prise, and who has pictured civilisation as coming
to a dead-stop if the levellers had their way. Now
he thinks that all industry isn’t good, and that
enterprise of some sorts may be a national cala-
mi tv ; and he will risk hanging up the march of
civilisation for a while if they will knock £lOO off
Deakin’s salary and tack it on to his own. But,
as a secondary consideration, what about that pre-
posterous extravagance whereof Spruce Myth’s
friends, the Sydney State I l ights party, which is
mostly the old Freetrade party working under an
alias, make such a mouthful ! What Mr. Smith
proposes, in short, is to cut £5400 off Ministers
salaries and put £ll,lOO on to members’ salaries,
which will make the Federal machine cost £5700
a year more than it has been doing hitherto. Ihe
Bulletin cordially approves of adding £ll,lOO to
members’ salaries; but there is this difference
between it and the State I rights crowd Ihe
Bulletin wants to give the Australian Parlia-
ment enough work to do, and enough power
and responsibility, to earn its higher salary,
while the State Frights crowd is busy lopping
off its powers whenever it sees a chance,
and reducing its authority, and blocking
any increase of power. The increase of members
pay should, in short, be justified, not by cutting
down the salaries of the men who have to do the
administrative work for Australia, but by cutting
down the powers (and number) of the State autho-
rities who have to attend to the bridge and the
culvert. And, anyhow, is there any sort of proof
at all that (apart from his own party) the struggle
for office is a mere struggle for pay ? As to his
own party, Mr. Smith no doubt knows best; but
as to the Labor party, at any rate, it gave proof
that it didn’t consider pay only, by throwing up
office when it might easily have carried on, and
when it certainly would have carried on if it
hadn’t had a much more tender regard
for principle than Mr. Smith’s leader has
ever shown. Mr. Smith’s party is, in fact,
the only Federal party which has ever had to
be thrown out of office by the scruff of the neck
—it is the only party which, therefore, lies under
suspicion of regarding office as merely a matter of
pay, instead of principle. And Australia s remedy
against that kind of party is simply not to elect
it. To punish some other party (and incidentally
Australia) for Mr. Smith’s party’s lack of prin-
ciple is too grotesque a piece of injustice to be
seriously considered.

The N.S.Willis “Reform” Governjnent.
The people of N.S.Wales, who were pen-jostled
into putting a “Reform” Government into power
at last elections, deserve now a cynical congratula-
tion on their bargain. The very least to be
expected from a Reform Government is a jealous
care for the purity of public administration, and
this Reform Government—in addition to going
back on its pledges in regard to economy, the
partial stoppage of borrowing, and Local Govern-
ment—is now recognised by nine citizens out of
ten as responsible for the fact that the inquiry into
the Lands Department scandals is water-logged
and drifting. The indictment against the
Government made last week by the Labor party
loft no loophole of escape from that conclusion.
The position is that there are certain facts known,
and certain conclusions which seem to be the
inevitable outcome of the said facts. These conclu-
sions are in most cases only suspicions, and it
would not be fair to condemn any man or body of
men on any single circumstance of suspicion. But
When there are several sets of facts all pointing
irresistibly to the same suspicion, it attains prac-
tically the standing of a proved truth. Half-a-
dozen incidents in regard to the paralysed Lands
inquiry are mysterious and unexplainable until
the explanation is hazarded that the Government
wished to hamper and smother the proceedings.
Allowing that explanation to be “the unknown
quantity,” and every problem is clear as daylight.
When the Sims-Browne facts were disclosed, there
was prompting to drastic action which would have
either broken up a nest of robbers or shown that
the Lands Administration was pure, and that the
magnetic land agent was able to get enormous
fees from clients purely because of their folly.
The Government took no such action. Why ?
After much delay and pressure the Government
was flogged into appointing a Commission of
Inquiry, with very limited scope and powers.
The counsel representing the Crown was a
man with no training to push home an inquiry,
and he treated witnesses as gingerly as if
they were precious china. Why ? When the
demand for a more searching inquiry grew
strenuous, the Government delayed action
until Mr. Willis, the one witness who could
throw light on some of the matters under inquiry,
was out of the Commonwealth. Why ? Mr.
Willis, when the Lands inquiry was getting
warm, made arrangements to leave Australia, and
drew up, in the office of Messrs. Carruthers and
Wilson, a power of attorney in favor of his wife,
making Mr. Carruthers’ partner his trustee.
Mr. Carruthers was in complete ignorance (he
affirms) of this document. Why, knowing that
Mr. Willis was a client of his office and that Mr.
Willis had employed him personally in at least
one land matter, did Mr. Carruthers allow
his business to be run in such a way as
to permit of that astonishing ignorance? Half-
a-dozen more such queries may be pro-
pounded, and to all of them a reply
is forthcoming if it is presumed that the
Government is not sincere in the task of inquiring
into the Lands scandals. Without that presump-
tion it is difficult to find any adequate explanation
for a whole series of happenings. That explana-
tion, 'therefore, must inevitably be accepted.
Ministers may explain. Those in Parliament who
have had money out of this Lands business may
protest. Parliament may accept explanations and
protests. But public opinion refuses to be misled.
If there were a general poll to-morrow on this
issup, probably not one man who voted with the
Government last week would be returned again.
With the assurance of public approval, and with
the knowledge that, as a consequence of its action,
the N.S.W. Government seems at last to be
galvanised into some activity, the Labor party
must, for the present, be satisfied. Its plea
for honesty has been blackguarded m Parliament
and in the daily press, which takes up the curious
attitude that though the Government is gravely
blameable it must still be supported. As Mr.
Holman neatly put it, the press has
laid down the new doctrine that it is better to have
Class Government and dishonesty than a Labor Govern-
ment and clean politics.
But the public gratitude is keen. Last week in
Sydney the most virulent Tories could be heard
praising the Labor party as the champions of
clean politics. On that foundation the party can
build up a solid platform for the next appeal to
the people if it has the sense to put forward, sin-
cerely, a genuine reform platform.

The Pay of the Politician.
“He had sacrificed his private and home life
trying to serve the country.” That was one of
the reasons given by N.S.W- Attorney-General
Wade last week in condonation of the action of
his Government in hampering the inquiry into the
Lands scandals. The same sort of plea is put
forward by Premier Carruthers, who speaks
with a sob of the immense personal sacrifices he
has to make to be N.S. Wales Premier, and by
many others. There is sometimes a good deal of
truth in these statements. The spectacle of the aged
politician turned out to die on the poorest of pas-
tures is common enough in Australia, and exceed-
ingly pitiful. Some day a strong Government, of
such honest reputation as to be safefrom the imputa-
tion of sordid motives, will set itself to remove the
necessity for such scandals. The pay of the
politician should be sufficient to keep him in
decent comfort, should be rigorously protected
against cadgers, and should be supplemented by a
pension system for all those who have given up a
material portion of their lives to the public service.
Then there would be no occasion for the politician
to whine that the country is sweating
him, and that he can’t afford to be too
honest, as the money doesn’t run to it. In
very many cases at present the whine is not
justified, and a man makes more at politics
than at any other calling he might adopt. But
that does not alter the fact that the pay of the
politician is not sufficient to command capacity
and honesty, and to give those qualities an
assurance of respectable comfort. The pay, there-
fore, should be raised, and accompanied by a well-
considered pension scheme. With the introduc-
tion of a system of elective Ministries this would
not be so difficult as at present, since that system
would do away, to a great extent, with the occa-
sional and inefficient Minister.
Holman M.L.A. Once More.
The Bulletin receives still another letter from
Holman M.L.A. (it has already published two),
but the document is, in the main, so careful an
evasion of the points at issue as to be hardly worth
space. It contains three more screams about
Pierpont Morgan, who seems to be such an
obsession of Mr. Holman’s that that gentleman
really thinks Pierpont Morgan is the only
person and the United States the only place on
earth. The letter also wants to know why The
Bulletin accuses people of Mr. Holman’s way of
thinking—the people who object to private
industries and are “prepared to wait’
indefinitely, in a weary sort of way, before
starting State industries—of callousness to-
wards the unemployed, seeing they backed up
O’Sullivan in temporarily staving off the
unemployed difficulty by loading posterity with
the cruel burden of still more debt so that the
present generation might be pauperised on loan
works. Also he points out that his party is pre-
pared to tackle the unemployed problem by having
a few railway engines built locally (presumably
out of loan money), which is about equal to one
drop of water in a desert. As for the questions
really at issue, it seems impossible to extract an
answer from the evasive Holman. Some of these
questions are as follows ; —-
(1) Seeing that Mr. Holman claims to be an unbiassed
person, with an equal scorn for Freetrade and Protection,
why did he tell that glaring lie that the aliens in England
were mostly Germans fleeing to a Freetrade country from
their own High Tariff one ?
(2) Why does he reserve all his scoffs for the Australian
manufacturer, even when the manufacturer pays decent
wages for fair hours of work and is not himself a rich man ,
and why does he save none of his scorn for the rich im-
porters—and the richest man in Sydne3% if not in Austra-
lia, is mainly an importer—who pay far worse wages?
(3) Why does he always strive to scare the public away
from creating Australian manufactures by yelling exclu-
sively about the United States, where private railway
ownership and the want of industrial legislation but
most of ail private railway ownership—has enabled great
and tyrannical Trusts to arise. France and Maoriland are
two examples to prove that with State ownership, or careful
State control of railways, great industries can be built up
without the Trust evil, but wild horses won’t drag Mr.
Holman into even mentioning these places or recognising
tll (4) Why all this Pierpont Morgan ? Rockefeller is a
much bigger and richer man than P. Morgan. Is the
reason whv he is never held up as Mr. Holman’s awful
example that he represents the Harvester combine, and is
doing the dirty work of Freetrade in trying to break up
an Australian industry ?
(5) When Mr. Holman professes to think that the estab-
lishment by Protection of privately-owned industries—in
default of those State-owned industries that are so slow to
arr i V e—will necessarily, despite Labor legislation and
State railway control, iead to exactly the same evils as in
the United States, with its private railways and with
hardly any industrial legislation, what does he mean ?
The most apparent meaning is that Mr. Holman has no
faith in his party’s industrial legislation, and is only fool-
ing the people when he advocates it.
(6) Mr. Holman has distinctly put forth his scheme of
State-owned industries as something that will do away
with both Freetrade and Protection —as a third alterna-
tive, in fact. When State-owned industries get beyond
building a few locomotives out of loan moneys, and take
to manufacturing things on a large scale for the supply of
the public, is it proposed to compete against the importer
by employing cheap labor for long hours and so under-
selling him, or by levying Protective duties against him?
And how came it, after 15 years or so of alleged thinking-
over the matter, that Mr. Holman didn’t see that even
State-owned industries must be run either under Free-
trade or Protection, and that State industry isn’t a third
alternative? . '
(7) Does Mr. Holman regard the pawning of our State
lands and our State railways to private money-lenders
compatible with any true devotion to the principle of
collective ownership? Also does he consider it the part
of a man to live by selling his own children into financial
bondage, and does he think there is anything .democratic
in a system whereby the son of Labor is born to toil all
his life to pav interest to Pierpont Morgan, or some such
bad character, and to do this simply because his fathers
were too lazy or too incapable to help themselves ? For
the interest that has to he made good out of taxation in
N.S.W. is getting to be a fearsome item.
(8) How long is Mr. Holman going to be “content to
wait” before he even attempts to start State-owned
industries—apart from building a handful of locomotives
for the State railways out of loan money ? When is he or
his party going to start a real crusade for the creation of
a single State-owned industry for supplying the people
with any of the necessaries of life? And when did Mr.
Holman "seriously urge the starting of any such industries ?
These are questions that can’t be answered by any
vague eloquence about Pierpont Morgan, and if
Mr. Holman proposes to answer them, he had
better leave that familiar obsession out of the
case.
Minister Ltne’s Bill for the establishment of an
iron industry provides for the payment of £304,000
in bonuses, spread over five years, for the manu-
facture of iron, steel, galvanised iron, wire net-
ting, tubes, and reapers and binders. Wages are
to be decided by the Federal Arbitration Court;
a fair price, which must not be exceeded, is to be
fixed for the sale of the goods; and the State
within which the works are situated may buy
them after the bounty period expires, at a price
to be fixed by the Federal Arbitration Court. Itisa
question whether, taking everything into account,
the bonuses are large enough. To take only one
example, the bonus which hI.S.W. paid secretly,
by reduced railway rates, to the hay, straw and chaff
industry alone, for the year to 30th June, 1901, was
£313,000; that is to say, if hay, straw, and chaff
had been charged at the same rate as most manufac-
tured goods they would have paid that much more
in railway freight. This was for one year in one
State, not for five years in the whole Common-
wealth ; and the producers who get this large
assistance are not bound to pay decent wages—in
fact, they represent a very bad form of Sweating
in Private Enterprise. Private Enterprise steam-
ship companies don’t do this sort of thing. 1 hey
rather charge extra for the hay, straw, and chaff
kind of cargo, which occupies so much extra space
for its weight. In the same year, and in the same
way, N.S. W. aided the grain and flour business
with an unobtrusive bonus or rebate of about
£1,500,000, and the coal and coke industry to the
extent of £1,260,000. These figures make the iron
bonus look a very humble affair.
