Vic. Premier Bent’s move in the direction of
settling unemployed on the land on the Danish
principle is being hotly opposed in Right-Thinking
circles on the ground that it will, if successful,
“ attract unemployed from all over Australia, if
not from other countries.’’ The very same people
who are now putting forward this argument are
those who have been agitating for immigration for
years, and denouncing Federal legislation for pre-
venting the admission of Asiatic scum. In their
last utterance, they give their case away. Under
the Danish scheme safeguards are provided suffi-
cient to prevent wasters fooling with allotments
for a few months, only to become State burdens in
the end. Only strong, intelligent men with proofs
of at least five years’ experience in agriculture are
taken into consideration. Anyhow, it is only the
success of the idea which will attract anybody to
Victoria. The agitation for increased immigra-
tion, on the principle that if the people are here
the work will come, stands revealed as a cruel and
heartless endeavor to exploit the desperate poverty
of men and women in older world centres, beside
which the tactics of longshore crimps are benevo-
lence itself. The Right-Thinking folk, by their
last admission, show that at no time did they want
the immigration of people who had work to come
to, but only wanted men to swarm in at random
and, by becoming stranded, reduce wages. As for
Bent’s scheme, The Bulletin’s opinion of it is
that, if he makes it a success, Bent should have a
statue, assuming there is a block of marble broad
enough.
O
Book-Arcade Cole, of Melbourne, issues to his
customers brass medals inscribed thus: “Acquaint-
ance only required. If all men throughout the
world were acquainted with each other there
would be no war.” This bright saying doubtless
explains why families never quarrel, why there
are never any civil wars, and why you always like
a man better the more you know him. In fact,
Cole’s theory is that everybody improves on
acquaintance however dirty he may be, and how-
ever much he may attempt to make love to your
wife as the acquaintanceship progresses. Perhaps
the sentiment would read better if it were reversed.
For instance, the Chilian is regarded, by some
folks who are acquainted witli him, as the fire-
brand of South America, but he gets on well
enough with the Thibetans and other people who
never heard of him. It would be interesting to
know if Cole never dislikes people when he be-
comes acquainted with them, and if he only hates
people that he doesn’t know at all. If these things
are not so, then he is a living evidence that his
own tin medal is an ass.
Senator Pulsford has taken up the cause of the
yellow races, and pleads in the press for a repeal
of the law against Japanese immigration. Apart
from the point that there is no law against
Japanese immigration, but that the Jap comes
under the same provisions as all other folk—this
pro-Asiatic falls in badly when he cites the
ex-Consul for Japan in Sydney
An impression appears to exist in some quarters, and
to find voice in certain sections of the Australian press,
that Australia is in danger of an influx of Japanese immi-
grants. I have always endeavored to show that this im-
pression is altogether erroneous, and in support of this
position I have been requested by the Minister for Foreign
Affairs in Tokio to bring under your notice some clauses
of the Japanese Act (passed in 1.896, and amended
in 1901) for the regulation and control of emigration
and the protection of Japanese emigrants.
Under this Act it is provided that no Japanese, may go
abroad without first applying to the Government (in
writing) for permission to do so, and his application must
be accompanied by a guarantee, signed by two or more
responsible sureties, for the good conduct of the emigrant
while abroad.
Now the Jap. immigrant best known in Australia
is the girl of gay brown habits. Have all the
Jap. women who haunt the dens of Northern
Australia guarantees of good conduct? If so,
what is good conduct within the meaning of the
Act in Japan ?
Kelly, M.H.R., has a notice on the business
paper of the Representatives:—
(1) That all naval expenditure by the Commonwealth
of Australia should be towards an Imperial Navy, on the
efficiency and adequacy of which in time of war will
depend her security from serious danger ; and
(2) That the Commonwealth of Australia’s contribution
to the Imperial Navy should be doubled.
Yet it has just been explained to Australia for
the dozenth time —this time by Commander
Brownlowe —that the British fleet will almost
certainly be withdrawn from Australian waters in
war time, and our coasts left without defence. Mr.
Kelly’s idea is that Australia, instead of defend-
ing itself with a fleet, should defend itself with
£4oo,ooo—as if a small enemy with one cruiser
couldn’t disperse £400,000 with a single shot. A
great deal of the Kelly variety of Jingoism, how-
ever, isn’t real Jingoism. It is rather the laziness
and incapacity of beautifully-dressed young men
with swift motor-cars, who haven’t an idea how to
set about a defence policy, and find it easier to
say that we don’t want one. It takes brains to
put a country in a state of defence, but anybody
can vote £400,000.
Carruthers has now gone back on Local Govern-
ment for N.S.W. He has behind him a party
large enough to push his present measure through,
but he has gone out of his way to inform the
Assembly that he “is not making this a party
measure.” That is the plainest possible intima-
tion that his majority need not vote for the Bill
unless it likes ; that it certainly won’t be rounded
up by the Government Whip to vote for it; and
that it need not be deterred from voting against
the Bill by any fear of throwing out the Ministry,
for the Ministry, not having made the Local
Government Bill a party question, will not resign
if it is lost. In so many words Carru-
THERS says: “Don’t vote for the Bill if
you feel the slightest inclination the other way ;
the blessed Bill doesn’t matter.” Yet at last
election Local Government was almost the first
item on Joey’s programme. It was a party ques-
tion then—a question of vital importance, and in
these days Joey was so madly enthusiastic about
it that he would have climbed over the dead body
of a profitable land agent to get at the Bill. His
assurances that Wise wasn’t in dead earnest about
his Local Government Bill, and that he (J oe) was
the only man who would perish in the last ditch
for the cause, were pathetic. And now J oe is the
same treacherous wet rag as usual, the same wisp
of sodden blotting-paper, the same moist collar
hanging on a line.
♦-
Brisbane Courier and other people who are
barking against Arbitration are gleefully pointing
out that, in N.S. W., the average number of hands
employed in factories from 1900 to 1904 was
60,779, 66,230, 65,633, and 68,036, while the aver-
age wages were £Bl 16s. 10d., £74 13s. 4d.,
£73 ,14s. 9d., and £73 13s. Therefore, of course,
Arbitration has been powerless even to prevent a
shrinkage in wages. Well? All this simply goes
to prove two things. First, it helps to knock out
the Fatman’s stupid notion that Arbitration is a
sort of brigand which makes the employer pay
whether the.industry can afford it or not. Five
thousand times or so it has been pointed out that
Arbitration is a device for fixing wages in a
reasonable way. The Fatman’s claqueurs shrieked,
in reply, that it was an infamous dodge to increase
wages; and when the figures knock the bottom
out of that foolish argument, the claqueurs don’t
go and hide their heads—instead, they say that
they declared all along that Arbitration was a base
device for cutting down pay. And, in the second
place, the figures give a hint to the Labor people
that Arbitration without Protection is like a pint
pot without beer. It’s a beautiful pot, no doubt,
and when the beer is made available it guarantees
that he will get a full, fair pint for his money ; but
until the beer is made available, it' is just so
much waste metal. While the cheap foreigner,
who isn’t under any Arbitration Court,
can compete with the local workmen, then either
the Arbitration Court has to fix wages lovv enough
for the local man to successfully compete With the
foreigner, or the foreigner gets the job. Protection
alone can guarantee the job for the local man;
Arbitration merely comes in then to guarantee
the local man fair pay for the job that Protection
has got for him.
♦
Said Bruce Smith recently
Good seasons are not wholly responsible for a nation’s
prosperity. A country requires something- more than
seasons. * It requires a good name 'with the people outside
—the people who will pour their money in to develop its
industries.
Always that gag about the people outside who will
pour their money in. Australia has, for 50 years
and more, been digging up money in huge quan-
tities. For years it supplied most of the golden
money, and much of the silver, in use in the
British empire. If it would only send away less
to pay for imports—in other words, if it would
have less of Bruce Smith’s Freetrade—it could
develop all the industries the country is Capable
of with its own money. The childish Spruce
Myth idea is that no country has any money
except what some absentee or foreigner “pours
in.” He is always wanting to borrow, or
to “ pour,” or to do something that means
dependence on the foreigner. The world, as
Spruce Myth sees it, is a sort of table round
which all the nations sit passing the same old
shilling from hand to hand—each pouring in the
same old “bob” on its neighbor to develop its
industries, and not one Of them developing its own
industries with its own “bob.” And when the
“ bob ” has gone all round they all rise and say
how prosperous they are. while Spruce Myth
waves the Union Jack in the middle distance and
the gaud mayor of London gives his, blessing from
the background.

That blatant Foreign-trader, Conroy, M.P.,
budded better than he knew when he urged Cus-
toms Minister Lyne to produce the official papers
showing why he had fixed the duty value .of
imported harvesters at £65 instead of the previous
figure, £3B 10s. Lyne promptly handed out what
turned out to be another exposure of the methods
of the International Harvester Combine, and the
treachery to Australia of the Foreign-trade advo-
cates in Parliament. Briefly, the Customs officers
had all along had suspicions about the exceedingly
low invoice prices of these machines, and, on 11th
October, ’O4, after making some small in-
quiries in Argentina and Canada, the invoice
value was fixed at £3B 10s. The American sup-
pliers, needless to say, wanted it fixed at much less.
Nothing further happened till McKay; of the Aus-
tralian Sunshine Harvester Works, got a letter from
his Italian agent, W. de Haan, of Milan. Haan
forwarded a letter, dated 3/4/’O5, from the Massey-
Harris Co.—part of the Combine—offering him
its Italian agency, and quoting the wholesale price
of its machines—the same machines it had hereto-
fore been trying to pass through the Australian
Customs at £29, and had actually passed at
£3B 10s.—at £65; and also repeating the old lie
that “McKay had closed down.” Seeing
that these machines are sold retail at from
£BO to £B5, the £65 figure is probably about
correct. Even as things stand now, the blessed
importer has been shown far more mercy than
justice. He is still given a very fair chance
against the Australian manufacturer.
♦
S. J. Mitchell, one of the Northern Territory
members in the S. Aus. Assembly, intends to
move that, in the opinion of Parliament, the over-
land line should be pushed on north from Oodna-
datta. The idea that needs to be rooted out first
is that the line must be laid in a lump. That
plan means either wholesale borrowing for a work
that certainly won’t pay interest for years, or
wholesale grants of country ; and every land-grant
railway in Australia has been a howling
failure. The line will have to be built
some day, if only for defence purposes; and
it looks at least as much of an Australian as
a South Australian business. If it’s good enough
for S. Australia to offer a foreign syndicate a vast
territory in return for building the line, it dught
to be better business for the State to offer the
Commonwealth the same terms for the gradual
construction of the line; and ;if S. Aus-
tralia can’t conveniently raise the money by
increased taxation, the Commonwealth can. The
best way of all is for the Commonwealth to take
the whole Territory, debts and all, buy up the
existing line from Adelaide to Oodnadatta, finish
the work, and thus own the entire route. And,
while it is about the business, the Commonwealth
can’t do better than acquire all the other Australian
railways and all the other Australian debts.

Andrew Mack’s “ Arrah-na-Pogue, ” at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney), is a trifle more cheerful than
some others. Of course, it can’t be made a uni-
formly cheerful affair. The most distressful coun-
try that ever had a foreign landlord is not a subject
that lends itself to a joke in four acts. But still it
lends itself in a peculiar manner to the four-act
melodrama. You cannot please either the average
patriotic Irishman or the average man in the
gallery better than by laying on the coat of trouble
inches thick in the third act so long as you take it
all off in the fourth. So Ireland abroad loves the
Irish play with a love that runs to a full house at
every revival. To him it’s an allegory ending just
as he wants it to end. Ireland has lived through the
promise of Act 1, the machinations of Act 2, the
woes of Act 3. All it wants now is the happy
ending of Act 4, when all the Michael Feeneys
are kicked out into space, and all the Arrahs and
V 1 be happy till the cows come home.
And Uoucicault gives Ireland just what it wants.
* * * *
But times for Ireland are changed a bit:
and the play has to change with them. Already
a part of the fourth act has been played, in fact:
wherefore, Ireland isn’t quite so boiling-hot as it
was, and a more subdued Feeney seems, nowa-
days, truer to nature; and the Feeney in Mack’s
performance is that sort of Feenev. Shaun, of
course, doesn’t alter much—he is still the same
Drotti of a postboy, and Arrah is a colleen that
needn t alter. Andrew Mack is, as hitherto, easy,
confident and melodious, but not particularly con-
vmcing; on the other hand, Miss Lovett’s Arrah
is her best performance by a good deal. In the
opening scenes, when no great depth is called for,
she is excellent. The other members of the co!
are from middling to poor—there is nothing that
will be remembered. The setting is, however,
something extra. Two charming stage pictures
are shown and some others that fall not far short
°u ii rir . e most notable of Mack’s songs is the
old Wearing of the Green ”to a humming ac-
companiment; Saturday night’s crowded house
got nearest to real enthusiasm at this'point.
** * *
At Sydney Palace the Great—not to say
Tremendous Thurston’s shadow is not growing
less. By all the laws of fairy tale he ought to have
no shadow. The Evil One should have bought it,
and put it in his strong box in Sheol till the owner
came, for it. But in these days of land agents
there s no knowing how Old Scratch and Thurston
manage their affairs. Anyhow, the magician has
caught the town, and has promised new mysteries
for next Saturday.
** * *
. Recently, in a spasmodic effort to improve
its mind, The Bulletin took up a book of Schop-
enhauer’s essays, and read that “ people who do
not go to theatres are those who perform their
toilet without the aid of a mirror, ” whereupon it
dropped the book and hustled off to have another
look at “The Earl and the Girl,” at Sydney
Gntenon. And it came away vowing that,
if it looks like Sheridan when he dances the
minuet in peer’s robes, it will not only
give up dancing the minuet altogether, but will
take to dressing opposite a full-length mirror and
then carry the same glass about with it, so that
it can notice any further tendency to look like
Sheridan, and retire into a quiet spot till the
calamity blows over. “The Earl and the Girl”
show no signs of going off—off the stage that is—
at present.
&& * &
Those ten boys picked from the Sydney
choristers, and presented to a delighted audience
by Mr. Arthur Mason, on Saturday night, at
Sydney Town Hall, must be real mothers’ joys
and fathers prides. That is, if the parents
appreciate their sweet soprano voices. Their
ages range from 10 to 16 ; they are public school
boys, and their careful training in sacred music,
combined with their habit of singing in church,
has given them just a film of the holy choir boy
suggestion so loved by the public that exists to be
deluded. At the same time the stained window
expression is not so marked as to cause their non-
singing school chums to give them the name of
Pie Faces. Sydney public flocked in thousands to
hear Branscombe’s Westminster Abbey concert
group, probably because it was under the delusion
that the pretty boys used to warble in the big
church where lie the Mighty Dead. When the
Sydney public recognises that the Sydney chor-
isters have as good voices as the visiting choristers,
and that with a little more training and practice
in public they will acquit themselves as well or
better, then the public will roll up to support local
productions and the electric light will wink at
Manager Southwell as he rakes in coin at the front
door.

“The Midnight Mail” will steam into
Sydney Royal for the last time on Friday, and,
aßer gloomily putting out the fires and blowing
off steam, it will be side-tracked among the
decayed locomotives of the past. On Saturday
Her Second Time on Earth” will be staged.
I his is a robust melodrama, familiar to Sydney,
and in it Miss Eugenie Duggan breaks things with
a bottle.
*# * *
Sydney Lyceum still totters on the verge
between Mammon and Righteousness. The old
signboard which said that liquid comforts could
be had by turning to the left has been hauled
down; in its place comes a calico poster which
says that spiritual consolation can be had within
every Sabbath. Between times the Theatrescope
effects a sort of composition between sin and the
other thing, and serves up a programme of “ani-
mated pictures,” running from a scrap of the
boat race, to the story of the wicked young
squire who has stolen away the blacksmith’s
daughter, and left her only a tarnished name
and a baby.

bell and Robert Young are among the assisting

The most conspicuous new-comer at Sydney
Tivoli is a talkative gentleman named Stilwell,
who would seem even more conspicuous if Sydney
hadn’t just been watching Thurston in much the
same line of business. Stilwell’s specialty, how-
ever, is flag-flapping ; instead of pushing the
atmosphere and extracting coins or beautiful
ladies out of it, he extracts flags and handker-
chiefs. Drvden i 3 now offering a lyrical version
of Marcus Superbus, and swearing to be true to
Mercia and Christianity as long as the limelight
lasts; never since the days of Rignold has Sydney
seen such a flood of limelight as Dryden calls for.
And Milo is still being beautiful in eight atti-
tudes.
** * *
“The Gondoliers,” introducing the Gilbert
and Sullivan Repertoire Co. to Melbourne, at
Her Majesty’s, last Saturday, is a gay and
sprightly performance from the opening chorus,
-the sixteen or so girls who never had a winning
chance in the game of blind man’s buff with
Marco and Guiseppe are good to see and hear;
the chorus men are almost as satisfactory, the
principals in each case have something, if not
everything, to recommend them, and the manage-
ment of the music and minor by-play strikes one as
being more artistic than in previous productions
of the opera. Where the principals fall short of
the Gilbertian intention the fault is not theirs
They can’t help it. The Duke of Plaza-Toro (H.*
H. Wallace) has the appearance and nimbleness
of a mere boy disguised as an elderly gentleman—
an escapee from a juvenile opera company. He is
amusing ; he has an effective voice, but his per-
sonality breaks up the illusion. Under the cir-
cumstances, a large, heavy, real contralto Duchess
would be more out of place than Miss Ghiloni
nevertheless the unfitness of Miss G. for these
Gilbertian old-woman parts is a painful reminder
of the period when the “ Firm ” used, to cast them
suitably. A Duchess so obviously young and
handsome to-day makes it hard for a boyish
Duke to persuade an audience that she was
a plain woman more than 20 years ago, as their
dialogue insinuates. Casilda (Miss Deloitte) has
the family failing in not looking the part, but she 1
plays and sings it very well. The other characters
are truthfully presented. Howard Vernon and
Kenningham have never been happier in their old
parts, John Ralston makes an admirable Luiz, and
he even perspires freely, as a menial drummer in
an embarrassing position should perspire. Miss
Aggie Thorn is a sweet Italianistic little Tessa,
and Miss Dolly Castles a winsome Gianetta of the
Dutch type. But the much-talked-about European
training of Miss Castles has not served to make
her sing or speak in quite the unaffected clever
style of the girl who had to make her own way to
the front. “ The Gondoliers ’’ runs for a week, and
on Saturday come the “Pirates of Penzance a
brief revival. The season will last only three
weeks.

At the Princess’s they were to take down
The Walls of Jericho” after the Wednesday
matinee this week, and give “ Niobe ” at night
for the one occasion only, wit,h Mrs. Brough as
the animated statue. A revival of “ Beauty and
lhe Barge” is advertised for to-night (Thursday)
and to-morrow, and “ Quality Street ” will happen
on Saturday. The success of “Jericho,” an
unquestionably powerful comedy, might have
been greater if some of the members of
the Smart Set had carried more weight.
The noble marquis was almost as un-
impressive as the silent _ guests who hovered
round the outskirts of his drawing-room, and
the epigrammatical Hannaford came short of
requirements, like one or two other young men
representatives of a gilded but degenerate civilisa-
tion. Moreover, “Jericho” had two or three
lines which were too strong for ordinary public
taste. They added nothing to the moral force of
the play, and they caused many persons to miss
seeing it* The girls gave one another warning
that these lines would be expected to make them
blush if Augustus were present, so—for fear that
they might not succeed in blushing up to expecta-
tions they told Gustus to keep them away from
“ Jericho.”

A Russo-Japanese war drama will shed an
aroma of gunpowder throughout Melbourne Royal
° n Saturday night. Bland Holt has never neg-
lected his duty where a war was concerned.
Sooner or later, he stages a drama on the subject,
and, m the present case, he has probably made
arrangements by which either side can be defeated
with great slaughter, to suit the cable news.
Meanwhile, some crushing defeats of the Russians
are in active rehearsal. On Saturday night, thev
will surrender Port Arthur and bite the dust at
Mellow Peril is to be presented
with the most appalling attention to detail. Two
more nights of Woman and Wine 99 will prepare
an expectant populace for “ Besieged in Port
Arthur, or Allies and Enemies.”

gmeered by admiring musical craftsmen, marked
the hnal appearance of Hugo Heermann at Mel-
bourne Town Hall last Saturday afternoon. Heer-
mann is a serenely beautiful violinist from the
mechanic s point of view, and in any of the verv
seriously intellectual concertos for violin and
orchestra he may be the most satisfactory soloist
under Heaven. Given a chance to play “ some-
thing resembling an air ” in the middle of an im-
pressive work (the bits of sugar are almost invari-
ably dropped into the middle piece) Heermann’s
tone is as pure as the proverbial ice, and as cold.

these t hi ngs described in non-Australian language?)
reminds one of the prayer of a sanctified We -

Kara, the juggler, who took ill whilst he
was taking well in Sydney some weeks ago, made
his convalescent appearance at the Opera House,
last Saturday. When he has recovered his lost
practice Kara will no doubt compare favorably
with Cinquevalli as a j uggler of light goods, but
he doesn’t introduce any of the feats of strength
and agility that made the veteran wonderful.
Mdlle. Guerra, a lady in expensive clothes,
described as a French and Spanish soubrette, is
another newcomer at the Opera House, and the
old-comers are still headed by Diamond and
Beatrice.
** * *
On Friday night, last week, the Players
made another effort—at Her Majesty’s, Mel-
bourne —to better their first treatment of “ The
French Maid.” With the assistance of a more
harmonious orchestra than they had at the Bijou,
the amateurs showed a very upward tendency.
All the performers, bar one, were the same people
as before, improved by further rehearsal. The
one new arrival, Miss Florrie Green, proved to be a
charming amateur actress in the name part. Mel-
bourne already knew of Miss Green as a dancer—
the “ star ” pupil of her mother’s school, and a
brilliant star at that—but her admirers had never
seen her as a musical comedienne. She needs a
little stage experience to give her the value of a
Letty Lind, the tall Gaiety danseuse who became
an accomplished all-round artist in light pieces.
Miss Florrie Green sings prettily enough, and has
more than enough grace and chic. The super-
abundant decorative style of the limelight dancer
only wants judicious pruning.

Edward Lauri and May Beaity bustled
through a week of “The Chinese Honeymoon”
and “ Bill Adams ” at Adelaide Royal.' Bill is a
cheery and welcome acquaintance, but he has
about as much coherent thought and purpose as a
kitten playing with a marble. Two of the most
successful items in the show were simply dropped
in from other pieces, and they fitted just as well as
if built for Bill. Anything except a hymn would
fit Bill. The brief season suffered slightly from
the fact that the Williamson Opera Co. was, so to
speak, waiting on the mat; and now “The
Cingalee ” has begun its course with great
splendor. Watkin Mills and Co. have come and
gone, and the Westminster chorister has been
exhibiting his large white collar in great strength
at the Town Hall.
The Wild Cat Column.
Dr. Culpin, M.11.R., has a proposal before the
House of Representatives for the issue of Commonwealth
Treasury notes, after the fashion of the Queensland
Treasury notes, with a gold reserve behind them. He
suggests that 4 per cent, of the notes should be with-
drawn annually, and wants the paper money used
to pay the States for properties taken over ‘by the
Commonwealth. This last part of the idea is utterly,
rotten finance, and should be rejected by every honest
man. The properties taken over from the States by the
Commonwealth were almost all built with loan money
The only way they should be paid for is by the Common-
wealth taking over as much of each State’s debt as will
represent their value, and then the Commonwealth
should set to work to pay off that debt. To pay the
States cash in any form for assets which don’t really
belong to them but to their creditors, and allow them to
spend the cash, and leave the debt standing after the
assets which represent the debt are gone, would be sheer
dishonesty.
* * * *
N.S.W. starts off the new financial year with a
month’s revenue of £756,599, or £36,020 less than for
July, 1904, and £121,366 less than for July, 1903.
The land revenue alone shrank £64,496 from last year’s
figures. Evidently the Treasury did some violent whip-
ping to get in revenue at the tail end of last year, in
order to allow Joseph the Reformer to mention that he
had a surplus. Expenditure was £922,382, or £165,783
more than the revenue—which is a pretty good start on
the way to a deficit. Loan expenditure was £152,195, or
at the rate of £1,826,340. a year; and only £18,844 of it
was on account of harbor resumptions. Waddell, in the
same month of last year, spent £138,351 of borrowed
money on works ; and Carruthers, who was so shocked at
Waddell’s extravagance and riot that he stumped the
country as a Reformer and got Waddell’s job, has spent
£138,197, or £4846 more.
Victoria’s revenue for July was £604,986, or
£12,806 ahead of that for July, 1904 ; so Bent starts off
the year with enough to encourage him to do better than
he did last year. Now that Bent is bracketed with Kid-
ston as the man who is keeping the finances straight, why
shouldn’t he publish a monthly statement of expenditure?
Kidston does it; ditto Carruthers and the Westralian
Premier, and Bent has a good lot less to hide than either
of the two last.
Westralia’s revenue for July was £196,428, the
lowest for a month for many years, and £20,231 less than
the expenditure. What is going to happen to Westralia
Heaven only knows. It still talks in a lordly way about
building new railways, and making harbors, and dropping-
a casual wharf here and there ; yet it only mentions extra
taxation vaguely, and then not above a whisper. If it
doesn’t tax, it will have to borrow, and when it lately
tried to borrow it could only get the public to lend a few
thousands, and the one thing that saved its face was that
Cohen, the financier, before he knew how badly things
were going to turn out, had guaranteed the loan for a
stiff commission. Cohen has most of the loan left on his
hands now, and Westralia is thus in the remarkable posi-
tion of having borrowed more than it can borrow.
Queensland collected £263,807 last month and
spent £178,448, leaving £84,639 to go towards the
appalling interest bill of £129,000 per month. Compared
with July, 1904, it collected £17,254 more and spent
£18,027 more. Though Kidston pulled through with a
speck of a surplus last year, he will have a hard struggle
to do the same this year. It is so very lon- since
Queensland had anything in the shape of a surplus on the
premises that the occurrence of the recent small stranger
has sept crowds of people pounding at the Treasury door
and Remanding concessions, and requiring that things be
restored to the basis of the drunk days ; and there is no
mention of taxation to do it with. That small speck of a
surplus looks as though it were going to cause its parent
a lot of trouble. 1
Most notable feature of Tasmania’s revenue
returns for July is the further slump in railway receipts.
Tasmania has the most deplorably unprofitable railway
system in Australasia ; in 1904 it yielded only I'9 per-
cent., and that was above the average. The lines dra-
thiough country which carries sheep instead of men for
want of a stiff land-tax on honestly assessed values : and
still, though the young men drift away and deficit is
chronic, it is not yet known whether fresh taxation will
be lequired. When does Tasmania think that taxation
is required ?

The amount shown as added to Renewal Reserve (£1247)
** v n aid 0f „ this reserve profits were charged with
£7314, but £6067 was written off, as against £2976
in the previous year. But for this extra writing-off, the
profits this year would be £1067 more than they were the
previous year, instead of, as shown in the table, £2024
less. On the other hand, £1570 is written off leaseholds
this year, against £lB6O last year. The published ac-
counts puts the capital at £480,000, on which the div
works out at 12£ per cent. ; but £230,000 of this capital
has been provided out of profits, and in the table the div.
is calculated upon the £250,000 which the shareholders
actually subscribed. The assets, apart from freeholds,
rolling stock, &c., total £586,495 (municipal and other de-
bentures account for £496,941) ; and the surplus of liquid
assets over liabilities, after paying present div., is
£490,752. The freeholds are in at book values, and the
debentures at cost; to the latter .holding- nearly £70,000
has been added during the year. The interest receipts
are set down at £18,090. On the amount held in deben-
tures, fixed deposits, and mortgage at the beginning of
the year, less bank overdraft (£6648), this interest would
mean £3 17s. per cent., so that, if there has been any
depreciation of these securities, it is apparently not
extensive.
The co.’s tramway undertaking, which is leased
from the Municipal Tramways Trust, reverts to the trust
without any payment on July 1, 1916. The co.’s expenses
in the past year included £74,250 on the Trust’s loans
(£1,650,000), and £45,000 sinking fund as against £33,000
the previous year; the total sinking fund is now about
£850,000. The position, then, is something like this.
The co. put £250,000 into the business, and, after paying
interest on the loans which the municipalities floated to
build the lines with, and also sinking fund it
made in the year a profit of 41 per cent, on its capi.
tal. Certainly, part of the profit went into a fund to
provide for renewals ; but it is very unlikely that the
renewals will cost £270,545 during the few years that are
left of the co.’s lease. It has been paying superb divi-
dends all the time ; as shown above, it has a surplus of
£490,752 of liquid assets, in addition to its own rolling
stock, freeholds, &c. ; so that, if the municipalities
accepted the latter to counterbalance any depreciation of
the lines (and the co. would probably smile at any such
proposal), the co., which put in £250,000, would walk out
with £490,000, after having pocketed an average div. of
about 17 per cent, a year. ' And yet it is a mournful co.
which pleads hard times and has a shocking reputation
for the niggardly treatment of its employees. Its record
is a warning against (he folly of leasing city tramways

1904 13,889 10 ~ „ ~ 11,250 2639 4,393
WOo 6 ’ 6,502 10 „ „ „ 5,625 - 877 5,270
The co. shows reserves of £20,270, but the assets include
“ Parliamentary rights, £15,000,” which being a paper item
only, is disregarded in the foregoing. The reserves are,
moreover, of the nature of provision for actual deprecia-
tion in some cases and probable depreciation in others.
All assets are in at cost—even the Waterloo lease, which
is obviously a wasting security. Such reserves as remain
sifter scoring out the paper item are therefore to some
extent nominal—even though the Parliamentary rights
have a market value at present and so long as they are
the basis on which the co. can work profitably. The co.’s
profits are not growing markedly. Its position is some-
what peculiar. If it pays more than 10 per cent., it has
to share with the city council; and it probably prefers, in
view of electrical competition, to give its customers the
benefit of anything it can make over an easy-10 per cent.
On the other hand, there seems no reason why, if it could
make larger profits, it shouldn’t use some of them to write
down its properties. Certainly the city has the right to
take over the undertaking at certain periods; but the. city
council isn’t likely to follow the balance-sheet valuations,
and it is less likely to do that the longer the co. lasts
without writing off anything. Co. has a profitable busi-
ness ; but under the circumstances the chances of larger
profits being available for distribution look pretty small.
The capital expenditure on the works is still mounting
up ; at the end of 1902 (the first year after the engineering
business was floated off) the surplus of liquid assets over
liabilities amounted to £6037 ; it is now only £1996. In
other words, plant, &c., has absorbed the current undi-
vided profit and £4041 besides, which is all the more
reason why writing-down should have attention, since
more and more pf the co.’s resources are going into
things that decay.

The figures show a very striking increase, allowing a
larger dividend and something extensive in the way of
writing off. Further details may throw some light on
matters :
1899. 1904. 1905.
Net Traffic Receipts .. .. £8,878 £13,710 £17,534
Management, Insurance, &c.. 3,233 5,53 S 6,276
Liabilities to Public .. ~ 32,691 41,766 38,495
Steamers .. .. .. 39,227 41,085 36,250
Properties .. .. .. 12,040 11,261 9,932
Cash and Deposits .. .. 5,693 16,394 15,455

The net traffic receipts have thus very nearly doubled in
six years. Liabilities to the public include £28,000 of
debentures due in 1910, and £8745 interminable 5 per
cents, issued to the old Manly Co. The steamer account
is being steadily written down ; including the cost of
special repairs made to one of the vessels, and paid for out
of revenue, £14,465 has come off during the past two
years ; and landed properties have also been written down.
As no large additions have been made to the floating and
other property, the writings-down have greatly strength-
ened the financial position. The co. is, however, now
launching a new steamer, the payments on account of
which (£10,047) are included above in “other assets.”
Co. deserves an extra stroke of good luck for having
had this steamer built in Australia. What the real
profits of the co. are it is impossible to tell;
but if the writings-off in the past half-year are no more
and no less than enough to cover the half-year’s deprecia-
tion, then the return on capital (£25,000) and reserves
was at the rate of £l4 13s. 9d. per cent, per annum. And
that is large enough to suggest that the assets are not
over-valued, and that the reserves are at least as big as
they are set down. On the other hand, note that the
Jan.-June half-year is usually the most profitable.

This half-year the form of the published accounts is some-
what altered, and, as usual, not in the direction of giving
shareholders more information about their property.
Previouslv, nothing was shown as charged in profit and
loss account for depreciation; now “ manufacture, de-
preciation, &c.,” are bunched. When the amount charged
for wear and tear is not disclosed, directors are, of course,
able to make the net profits look big or little as they please.
The “ plant, land and building” item in the balance-sheet
doesn’t help at all, because additions are constantly being
made, or, again, plant may be sold. The properties appear,
for book-keeping purposes, to have been kept fairly well
within their real value ; therefore, though the co. has
£15,000 of borrowed money (debentures, 4 per cent.,
repayable 1915), the whole assets show for the past year a
profit of £7 12s. 6d. per cent. ; or on shareholders’ funds
alone, a net £9 15s. per cent. The co. owes practically
nothing outside the debentures, but all its resources are
in the business.

The position is none too certain. There was formerly a
“ suspense ” item of £2890, which included premiums on
new shares ; that has now disappeared, and the formal
reserve fund has sprung' £3309 without explanation.
Apparently the two movements are connected. Of the
reserves £28,675 is described as “ Renewal plant, mams,
&c.” This is a very doubtful reserve. It is pro-
bably a provision against depreciation which has
already taken place. In that case the reserves are tq
that extent nominal: also in that case the true profits
are for the half-year £5OO less than shown, that being the;
amount added to this depreciation fund. On the other
hand, part of the unexplained addition to reserve fund
may have come out of undisclosed profits. As remarked
before, the accounts were apparently made up during a
heavy fog, and none of these things are too clear. The
co.’s position is, nevertheless, and for certain, much
stronger than that of most gas co.’s. It has no deben-
tures or deposits, and its debts to the public total only
£6667 ; while in addition to plant, works, &c., it has
£l6 575 of Government stock and £12,828 of book debts.
Also it has a remarkably profitable business. Its whole
asset’s (even though some of them should be written down
out of renewal reserve) are earning, net, £7 ss. per cent,
per annum, while on its own funds (capital £45,000 and
reserves as shown in the table above) the return is
£8 ss. 6d. per cent.

The Brighton Gas Co. (Vic.)
Written To Re- Re-

June, 1904.. 1831 6„ „ 1340 343 148 3228

June, 1905.. 2172 6,, „ 1340 275 557 4190
There is practically always some suspicion about gas co.s’
reserves; the funds are in plant, buildings, mains, &c.,
and even when repairs and renewals are well kept up, the
value of the patched concern isn’t the same as the value
of the original, brand-new concern. The Brighton Co.,
at any rate, has the merit of showing what it does write
off ; also it has practically no public creditors. On the
other hand, very nearly all it has is in the business. The
undertaking is yielding a solid £7 17s. per cent, net on
shareholders’ funds ; and on the whole assets £7 10s. per
cent. ; and when money can earn that much in the busi-
ness,'there isn’t much temptation to put it into stocks or
deposits that yield 3* per cent. Yet the latter are a handy
thing to have on hand when a stroke of bad luck comes
along, and something extraordinary in the way of re-
newals has to be done ; for the most philanthropic of con-
tractors or coal-suppliers won’t take payment in a mile or
so of partially worn-out pipes and a load of meters.

This vast concern continues to spread ; through the issue
of new stock (capital is now £1,220,100) it has obtained
control of the United Distilleries, Ltd., Belfast, in respect
of which £120,000 goes into the balance-sheet as an asset.
The co. has £974,912 stock on hand and maturing ; so the
whisky-drinker who calls for D.C.L. isn’t likely to call in
vain yet awhile. * * *
Mt. Boppy (N.S.W.), whose south-western
leases are still under an indefinite option to the Anglo-
Australian Exploration Co., hasn’t so far opened up there
any deposit that’s likely to lead to business, though the
country has been systematically tested by more than
1000ft.'of east and west crosscutting at a depth of 100 ft.
At south end of mine’s workings reef is known to dip,
but as it has been picked up in a shallow crosscut 1000 ft.
from the main shaft, and shows a south-easterly trend,
assumption is that co.’s western leases, described from
time to time as auriferous, aren’t quite so auriferous as
was thought. Experts Dunstan, Hocking and M'Donald
may thus prove right in stating that Boppy South carries
the continuation of Boppy lode. On account of the dis-
appointing result in Boppy’s crosscuts, the Bakeweil
diamond drill, whose mission is to pick up the lode about
3000 ft. still further south from last discovered point,
seems to have a hard, lonely road ahead. And there is
enough pluck in that venture to deserve something
better. #
North Cobar (N.S.W.), lately acquired by
English newspaper man Newnes and the electric ore-
finders, doesn’t seem to have much chance of getting on
to the profitable end of the Cobar Mine’s shoots. Extreme
point of ore body opened up in Cobar Mine is 750 ft. from
North Cobar’s boundary, and at this it is 600 ft. deep and
of low grade (about 2 per cent.). On present calculation
North Cobar will have to sink 2000 ft. to reach it if it lives,
which is some explanation why “ the S3 r ndicate has
never madly rushed after North Cobar, and why North
Cobar old shareholders never got anything for their out-
lay in upper levels.
* * * *
Chesney, Cobar, which could not be handled at
a profit by the Malcolm M‘Eacliarn crowd, through want
of a basic ore, now has in sight 200,000 tons of 31 per
cent, copper ore, carrying 26 grains of gold to the ton.
Stuff is going regularly into furnaces of Cobar copper
mine ; and as Chesney stuff smelts something like 18s.
per ton more than the Cobar sulphides, the syndicate’s
deal pans out profitably.
* -K * *
It will be two months at least before things are
at all straight at the Sulphide Corporation. Two levels
are apparently unaffected by the recent crush, but lack
of means to get ore to the mill from there will block pro-
duction for a time. As expected, Block 10 is more
affected by the crush than it cared to own, but the South
seems solid.
* * * *
The Broken Hill mines feel the strain for weeks
before the crash comes—they “talk,” as the miners
call it. The Sulphide mine had been “ talking ” for more
than a week ; and on Monday there was movement
enough to upset the working of the shaft. Barrier
Miner published a report on Tuesday that things were
unsettled ; but the management pooh-poohed the rumors,
and outsiders in the cities heard jipthuig■ of them. But
people who had been bitten before proaiptly g shaft

was two years ago recognised as doomed, so ° p ’
and a new main shaft, now almost begun. For

ping Kintore shaft to last another week*

B.H. Prop, is thinking hard over a proposal to
put up a pig-iron furnace at Port Pirie (S. Aus.), drawing
its ore from the Iron Knob, on the other side of Spencer s
Gulf, and its coke from Bellambi (N.S. W.). The manage-
ment naturally isn’t very enthusiastic about it, rattle
absence of tariff or bonus help, but is almost persuaded
to risk the experiment, being assured anyway of one large
customer—itself.
* * * *
Junction, Wyalong (N.S.W.), long under a
cloud, is showing rich mineral stone in the deep levels.
Co. has also 500 tons average quality ore ready for treat-
ment. The shaft is down 800 ft., and expense of d velop-
ing the reef in deep country has to be borne by some
half-dozen shareholders. The property is on the main
line of reef (The Mallee Bull), and adjoins the True Blue.

Development here will be the salvation of the co., for the

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boileis as
the standard for ‘the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decav, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discretion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Last year Australia imported £106,758 worth of
motor-cars ; this year the figures will probably be trebled.
And every penny of all those thousands should have been
spent in the country. The Victorian Government does
well by making all its own motor-buses—all but the
machinery, and it ought to make that likewise.

Not long since a N S.W. railway engine-
i Ver r a veteran Wlt}l a pood record and afirst-
dass character faded to pass the periodical eve-
sight test, and was degraded in his declining
years to be a cleaner. That was fair enough • a
riwer with failing eyesight is too serious a danger
to be tolerated. But when a Chief Justice grows
deaf he is just as useless and just as dangerous as
the near-sighted driver. And if the law were no
respecter of persons it would reduce him to dust-
lng out what used to be his court, with his old
judicial wig fpr a duster. If it isn’t right to do
that sort of thing to the old Chief Justice, then the
old engine-driver isn't getting a fair deal.

The voice of China begins to be heard on
the Rand. A Chinese interpreter in that region
has written to London Daily Chronicle to say
that his countrymen were beguiled to Africa by
false prefences, and that they are insufficiently
fed, cruelly flogged, and paid less than the Kaffirs.
Y el J’ an y san e individual expect anything
else ? Ihe war was a war for cheap labor, and
cheap labor has come. The mine-owners, who
deliberately brought about the death of thousands
ot men, women and children to increase their
blood-stained dividends, are not likely to consider
flogging and starvation as anything out of the
way—they are small things compared with mur-
der. Besides, cheap labor is always supposed to
be flogged—if it is cheap enough.
A Victorian town has been having some
amusement in a small way. There was a
prospective vacancy for a beadle or some-
thing, and an energetic councillor is said to
have got up a requisition to himself and gone
round getting ratepayers to sign it—the purport
of the document being that the ratepayers had an
enthusiastic desire to see him decorating the post.
Then he is alleged to have advertised it at his
own expense, and later, to have advertised that
the warm-hearted ratepayers were going to pre-
sent him with the document, but as it had never
been out of his possession the presumption is that
he presented it to himself, and urged himself
strongly to overcome his natural diffidence, and
come forward, and accept a post which he was so
well fitted to adorn. At last advices the gentle-
man was said to be still struggling with his
natural diffidence.
Mr. M‘Anulty is still an issue in Victoria.
A Public Service Board met last week to consider
a charge, laid against Paul Anthony M‘Anulty by
M. Byrne, secretary of the Crown Law Depart-
ment, of not complying with an order to give over
to his successor the documents relating to his job
as taxing officer of the Supreme Court. Briefly
M'Anulty’s suggestion is that he doesn’t come
under the jurisdiction of the Public Service Board
but of the Supreme Court. A person bearing an
exact resemblance to P. A. M‘Anulty attended,
but explained that he was not there as
1. A. M'A unity, but as the taxing-master of the
Supreme Court. (Reminded that the charge was
against P. A. M‘Anulty, the adumbration, or
whatever it was, affirmed “P. A. M‘Anulty is not
here,’ and stated that, as taxing-master of the
Supreme Court, it intended to retire from the
room ; and did so.
Ihe proposal to shift a number of dead
men’s bones from the northern end of Old Mel-
bourne (West) Cemetery, in order to enlarge the
inconveniently situated Victoria Market, has
recalled attention to the fact that Melbourne’s
earliest corpses have received no sentimental con-
sideration from posterity. Graves and tombstones
at the V est Melbourne burv big-ground have, in
most cases, been utterly neglected for years. The
state of the enclosure is an uglv satire on the
occasional professions of respect for Old Pioneers
which are uttered at gatherings of modern feeders.
As a matter of fact, the inmates of the first
local cemetery went early to bed, as a rule. They
were young pioneers when they died for want
ot careful housing, or proper medical attention, or
because the early Melbourne liquor was of a
wicked character. But they pioneered. They
were adventurous spirits—among the fittest,
maybe, although they didn’t happen to survive.
Judging from some existing specimens, and sons
ot specimens, of Melbourne s ancient pioneerage,
one would say that the unfittest survived, whilst
the good fellows took up the only allotments of
local land that didn’t enormously increase in value.
The decision of Camperdown to munici-
pally join the city of Sydney gives the Greater
Sydney movement some kind of a start. The
Sydney municipality covers 2180 acres, and the
new arrangement will add 435 acres to this. Cam-
perdown is a ridiculous little borough, yet
it is large compared to some around Syd-
ney. For instance, Darlington keeps up all
the pomp of local self-government for an
area of 38 acres, and Erskineville for 166
acres. Camperdown muddled along on its
own for 43 years before bankruptcy drove it to
incorporation. The dirtiest part of the muni-
cipality voted solidly against union, being bitterly
afraid that it would be washed, but the cleaner
regions gave an overwhelming vote the other way,
and left the offensive locality in a bad minority. '

* * * *
London County Council has determined
to put down what it calls the “ bogus
agency system ” ; and a man who ad-
vertises a situation and charges ap-
plicants an entrance fee is regarded
as a bogus agent. Under these cir-
cumstances N.S.W. Public Service
Board is lucky that it doesn’t live in
London. For it regularly advertises
vacant posts, or posts that may be
vacant some day, and as regularly
notifies that applicants must pay ss.
or 10s., or more, to have their fitness
tested. _ A London agent who really
had a girl’s billet for disposal set up a
half-crown entrance fee, and raked in
800 half-crowns. It was admitted that
one girl received the appointment, but
the London County Council, never-
theless, treated him as a swindler.
A barrister or doctor candidate cer-
tamly pays a fee for examination ; but
as many as pass receive qualifications.
It is different when the number of billets
is fixed, and any number of fee-paying can-
didates are invited to contest.
* * » T
The German Kaiser’s proposal that the
entrance to the Baltic Sea should be closed against
warships by treaty, just as the Black Sea is closed,
has a streak of brilliance about it. The entrance
which it is proposed to close is, of course, the
natural one by the Skagerßaek and the Cattegat.
But Germany has its own well-fortified private
entrance to the Baltic by the Kiel Canal—an
entrance which probably half the navies of
Europe would be insufficient to force. Therefore
the German fleet could come out of the Baltic
when it pleased, and if it lost the fight, or if it
found the enemy too strong, it could scurry back
again like a cat going up a tree ; and its enemy
would be in the position of the dog that can only
bark down by the tree-root. It would be eternal
Sunday on the Baltic, with the pub closed all the
time, but Germany vvould be perpetually drinking
at the side door, while the other nations waited on
the front step and parched. Of course the closure
may come about some day, when Germany has
borrowed the remainder of Denmark ; but, just at
present, the bounding Kaiser seems to have been
touching the paint before it was dry—as usual.

Music hath charms. The last appeal of a

There won’t be nobody to play the pianner on

A while ago the Australian post office got
tired of tracking up at considerable expense the
lost letter that was never lost, but was left hang-
ing up at home in the other coat, and it caused no
end of a row by asking for 2Ad. along with each
complaint. Everybody who had ever lost a letter
thereupon threw offensive language at the depart-
ment for finingpeople2id. for mislaying their corres-
pondence. Pumpkin Smith made a minute just
before he left office that the 2*d. hadn’t had the
desired effect of reducing the number of com-
pkunts ; wherefore he suggested that it should he
aboashed as leading to more worry and wicked
language than any 2Jd. is worth. The matter
really takes some hard thinking over. There are
not any satisfactory Australian figures on the
subject, but Maoriland has some which answer
just as well. Last year, in that State, 116,000,000
separate letters, packets, newspapers, etc., were
handled ; and only 3586 complaints of non-delivery
were made one for every 30,000 articles. In
1883 cases the article were traced ; no less than
887 had been delivered ; and in most of the other
cases they hadn’t been posted at all, or had been
posted without being addressed. What happened
to those that couidn t be traced nobody knows—•
except, perhaps, the husbands who forgot and
didn t post them, and dropped them silently at
the back of the grate after the row started.
* * * *
The Sydney law firm of Carruthers and
Wilson (patronised by Willis) was evidently of
the sort that never let its right hand know what
its left hand was doing. Premier Carruthers—
confronted in the New South Willis Assembly
with a copy of a power of attorney signed on May
20, by which W. N. Willis appointed his wife as
his attorney, with Wilson (Carruthers’ law partner)
as trustee, and which set forth that he (Willis)
intended to leave Australia—explained that he
knew nothing of the document. His junior part-
ner micln t mentionod it to him. I'he power of
attorney was signed on May 20, just when the
Lands Inquiry was getting into its stride,
and was registered and made public property
on June 27. But though the deed was exe-
cuted in his office and appointed his partner a
trustee, and though it was rumored as a wild joke
throughout Sydney, Premier Carruthers knew
nothing about it! It was an awful state of ignor-
ance for a man to be in after all our talk about
compulsory education. Plainly a lesson about
powers of attorney should be included in the
fourth Reader.

M.L. Parliament recently underwent the
indignity of being cinematograplied in all the
glory of wig, bell topper, robe, bauble, and
bumbledom. It expressly got together before
noon, and, forming up in line, advanced past the
machine with the dignified solemnity of a pea-
cock. Ihe circus included the Speaker in full-
bottomed wig and robe ; the Chief Clerk and bis
assistant, in their petticoats; and the Sergeant-at-
Arms, in evening dress, staggering under the
Mace. Later in the day a second performance
was gone through. All members who could be
raked m (King Dick included) were marched past
the machine in twos and twos. The pictures are
destined for show in England.

There’s a little Bill that Deakin’s introducing-,
A gentle little Bill that’s all his own,
That will show that Young Australia’s fairly oozing
With a greasy, grand devotion to the Throne.
We must save the shillings that we might have toted,
And go thirsty for the heers that might have been, ’
That five-and-twenty thousand niay be voted
For a statue to the late lamented Queen.
And while our debts ar - heavy on our shoulder
(And to pay them is a job we bravely shirk);
While our I O U’s accumulate and moulder,
And our population’s often out of work
We shall spend the cash we owe (transaction shady !)
In building, on the planet’s farther side,
ine statue of a stolid'German lady
Who lived beyond the usual age—and died !

The new Westralian Government Auditor
has been trying to find out whether the State s
experiment in hotel-keeping pays or not. The
pub. is at Gwalia, away out in the wilderness,
where J. Bull owns a profitable gold-mine ; and
the local yarn is that the profit that comes up
through the beer-pump flows out in extra-special
accommodation provided for J. Bull’s jackeroo
and for the haw-haw visitor who goes up to see
whether the poppet-heads are sprouting gold yet
or not. The auditor doesn’t go into this branch
of the question ; in fact, he says that he can’t go
into any branch of the question very far, because
no proper accounts are kept; but, so far as he
can make out, the people’s pub. is paying close
upon 6 per cent. Still, it doesn't seem to him
that anything is being allowed for the chips
that are being knocked off the furniture, or the
crockery, or the building, or anything else ; so
the financial result of the experiment is still hazy,
whatever the other results may be.
* * * *
In reply to a woman-journalist (London
Referee) who commented on the humorlessness
of her sex, another woman wrote “ Personally
I am, thank goodness, gifted with a great
abundance of high spirits, and no less plentifully
with a humorous and witty sense. To play a
practical joke, or to be the recipient of one, is one
of my greatest delights. I receive an infinite
amount of fun from this sort of humor... I attribute
my success to my gift of high spirits.” It can’t
have been ’Arry who started the bank-holiday
pastime of exchanging ’ats with the lydies, and
shovin’ one another abowt. It was ’Arriett —
mirthful, humorous, witty, high-spirited ’Arriett.
She’s the mother of practical joking. Who in-
vented that brilliant pleasantry, the “apple-pie
bed”? Woman. And look at all the funny
things she commits for the purpose of tempting
man to chase her into a dark room and kiss her.
* * * *
Considering that Premier Bent poses as a
Tom-of-all-trades, it seems about time for him to
start a Government fish-hawking business in Mel-
bourne, with country-town branches. The public
at large would be all the healthier for an increased
fish diet, and all the richer if they got it at a con-
siderable reduction on the existing “ ring ’’ prices.
Fishermen complain that they are sinfully under-
paid for their produce, and have no inducement to
keep the market well stocked, whilst hawkers
complain of the tactics of the big dealers. A
Government fish agency, properly managed,
would benefit the producers, find employment for
new distributors, reduce the cost of food to con-
sumers who now suffer from excessive meat-eating
-—and the Stinking Fish party would admit that
Socialism had one virtue.

The Victorian Government is going to
have the reserve alongside Federal Parliament
House, where the Eight Hours monument stands.
“ beautified ” with flower-beds and grottoes. It is
a pity the monument can’t be beautified. The
tawdry apex of the thing, a gilt ball and three
tarnished B’s, is an eyesore to every Laborite with
a sense of beauty, and a laughing-stock to the few
Tories who can see a joke. If statues were any-
thing to go by, a stranger in Melbourne would
think Rupert Clarke’s late father was the most
important circumstance ever known in Victoria,
and the Eight Hours movement the least. A
beautiful monument by Bertram Mackennal cele-
brates Clarke’s alleged virtues. There is probably
no chance of getting rid of the Eight Hours
column (unless it can be traded off as a
tombstone to some wealthy widow whose hus-
band lived to the age of 888 years); but it would
be worth while for the Eight Hours people to con-
sider whether 190(3, their year of jubilee, will not
be a fit occasion for knocking off the ugly tinsel
top. If it is necessary to preserve the three B’s,
they could be buried under the monument, or
melted and poured over it.
* * *
“ A. 8.” : Vic. Premier Bent’s Bill to-pro-
hibit the smoking of cigarettes by boys should go
a step further and make the sale of cigarettes,
cigarette paper or cigarette machines to anybody,
irrespective of age, a criminal offence. I am not
an anti-tobacco fanatic. I smoke six pipes a day
regularly, and formerly was fool enough to smoke
cigarettes. Therefore, I know the effects of them.
The difference between a pipe and a cigarette is
that a pipe satisfies the craving for tobacco while
a cigarette only feeds it. No man was ever satis-
fied with cigarettes only, and if he smokes 10
packets straight oif the reel, his natural inclina-
tion, requiring a severe wrench to overcome, is to
start the eleventh. This, altogether apart from
the effects of the “drawback”—responsible for
weedy, weak-kneed, anaemic youths all the world
over—and the fact that a youngster of six can
smoke a cigarette without turning a hair, while it
requires a fairly mature stomach to take on a pipe
for the first time. The cigarette is a curse that
should be stamped out on national grounds. A
nation of drunkards or pipe smokers or libertines
may accomplish great things; but a nation of
cigarette smokers will lay down its rifle and trade
off its women to the invading scum of Asia.

Sachse’s intention to secure colored reproductions

each (B. 27/7/’O5). Writer of par. jeers at the
pictures as possible half-crown chromos. Con-
sidering that the beautiful three-color reproduc-
tionsin the “Nation’s Pictures” and “Private
Galleries ” series are procurable at the rate of four
for a shilling, or less, it is not likely that M.r.
Sachse will go in for chromos. The man who
wants better reproductions than good three-color
screen work gives is hard to please. State school-
children of earlier generations would ’ have been
K ‘“JX, ‘ »IteS“ r M±?' r B™wn brig wlttt
Millais Greiffenhagen, Abbey, or Fred Walker.
Of course, one won id like to see, in addi-
tion reproductions of the best work of
Australian artists. Excellent three-color pro-
cess work done locally shows that this is feas-
ible. And good black-and-white drawings should
not "be forgotten. The sole decorations of Vic-
torian class rooms, in my juvenile experience of
them, used to be ancient maps, looking like pic-

portrait of Queen Victoria, and a black-bordered

was dead or alive. I fancy some of these weeps
about Prince Leopold are still yellowing on the
walls of remote academies.

The Cobram (Vic.) honorariums lately
gaoled for three months an old battler of 87,
whose sole offence was that he seemed to be too
old to carry a swag any longer, and had no one to
look after him. He had never been m gaol before,
and he soon died. As Coroner Cole put it : “He
had lived in the open air all his life, and shutting
him up was like shutting up a child. Instead of
a benevolent asylum he was sent to gaol, and it
killed him.”
“E.”: The list of Bernard Hall’s pur-
chases for the National Gallery, under the terms
of the Felton Bequest, is at hand. He has done
better than any previous purchaser. He has
obtained 38 works for less than £5OOO, a rather
better result than one small uninteresting Alma-
Tadema for £4OOO. Among them are examples of
Turner (the greatest landscapist), Madox-Brown
and Holman Hunt (pre-Raphaelites), Pissarro (a
representative French impressionist), Rodin (the
greatest sculptor in the world), and Clausen,
Gilbert, Banes, David Cox, Charles Keene, &c.
All these works represent something—a school, a
period, a personality—and are not merely pretty
pictures, dome people would have preferred one
big canvas, with a big name, but better, for the
present, good small works till we get together a
collection that will represent the main currents of
modern art. And if Hall keeps on as he has
begun, Melbourne in time can’t help having one
of the best modern galleries in the world. '
THE OTHER SIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am busy sorting the morning’s post;
My husband puts down his cup to say
“ They’re hanging a man at the gaol to-day,”
And he helps himself to the buttered toast.
Through the lattice window there comes the call
Of sparrows, chirping along the rail,
A great magnolia blossom pale
Spreads its drowsy sweetnesses over all.
The grasses wave on the lawn unshorn ;
Wild bees cling close to the hearts of flowers,
A promise of warmer noontide hours
On the scented breeze from the north is borne.
With no mourning train and no church-bell’s toll,
Where the prison walls rise rough and brown
On the other side of the sunlit town
They are setting adrift a human soul.
My husband pushes the window wide,
I pin in' his coat a late-blown rose,
And out of the shady room he goes
To the amber glare of the world outside.
Now the ten drilled wealth of a vine astray
To the clasp of the lattice frame is caught. . .
I wish I could banish that haunting thought—
“ They’re hanging a man at the gaol to-day! ”
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 8, 1905.
My Dear Arini.—
A man—he doesn’t belong to the Amateur
Fishermen’s Association—told me that he counted
five hundred red hats in the crowd outside the
church where the Gaden-Hordern wedding was
being celebrated. Every woman in this violet-
filled city of ours has a red hat —except the woman
who hasn’t. The motor car that carried bride and
groom off on their honeymoon to Pennant Hills
broke down at Balmain and over an hour was spent
in repairs, so the happy pair—to coin anew
phrase-held a reception of some hundreds of
Balmainiacs. Bride’s brown dressing saved her
from distracting all attention from the repairing.
This same motor is the one that dashes nightly in
such style on the stage of the Royal (“ Midnight'
Mail ”), its blunt nose shining with pride and
polish. It takes eight men to lift it on. Then
Miss Ada Guildford, in a motor-costume of white
cloth with bright facings and veil, comes on,
and the real things—even to the toot and the
odorously-loud trail of redolescence—are all there.
A bride of this month will be Mrs. Jamieson,
who was one of the Misses Joseph, of Marratta
station. Her late husband was in the Gordon
Highlanders, and was killed in the Boer war.
When she married a Christian, she lost the large
fortune bequeathed her by her grandfather, but
regains it by marrying Mr. Reginald Davis (of the
firm of David INathan and Cos.). The Davises
will live in Wellington (M.L.) after the Wedding
March has been played. Do they play the Wed-
ding March in the Synagogue?
Certain names in the passenger lists of steamers
keep the eye a space longer than others. The
name of Mack Jost, a departer by the Macedonia,
for instance. Mr. Mack Jost is a Briton, and
I believe the most notable commercial representa-
tive from the Cold Country who takes Australia
in his periodical rounds. Rumor says his annual
income runs close to £20,000 (everyone is interested
in the fearfully vulgar question of what the other
fellow “makes”). Apart from the fact that he
has put more girdles round the earth than most
men, he is interesting as the disposer of a
chemically-manufactured ruby. It was a superb
stone in color, weight and size, so perfect an
imitation as to deceive experts. Mr. Mack
Jost made no secret of its having come from
a laboratory, and, as such, he sold it in India
to a native merchant for a big figure. Buyer
disposed of it at a profit, and after much trading
it fell into possession of an English lady visitor to
India at about £15,000. She, on returning to
England, heard for the first time of its chemical
compoundage, and. brought a suit against its last
seller for restitution of her money. She lost her
case. Cost of producing this artificial ruby was
so great that its maker did not continue the
industry.
The progressive Mack Jost has a clever wife,
who acts as his secretary and does his typing.
When here, at the Australia, she used to wear
the smartest of long, long red coats, with a high
collar and engraved gold buttons.
Miss Fanshawe—her father’s rank has been
raised during her absence, and he is now a
complete admiral—was, at last accounts, at
Rotorua, otherwise Heartsease Land. Before
much more wattle withereth she will be back here
admiring papa’s new flag. The whole population
of Sydney was wakened by cannon-fire when the
new bunting made its first bright, fresh flap.
Talking of Maoriland reminds me that Maggie
Papakura, the Maori guide, has been on a visit to
the Sees in their eyrie at Wentworth Falls.
The marine product again : Miss May Dalgarno,
a local girl, and a niece of a recent P.M.-G., left
by the Moldavia to be married in London. Her
“ future ” is one of the officers on the steamer on
which she travels. They met on a previous sea-
faring.
Miss Rose Musgrove is now staying at Eaton’s
Pennant Hills Hotel, that the hill air may aid her
slow recovery. Miss Musgrove is just able to get
about, and is still ' in care of a nurse. Before
leaving the hospital she gave the nurses a many-
eoursed and very floral dinner.
S.A. Governor Le Hunte’s new A.D.C., Hon.
Dudley Ryder, it appears, cannot dance, but is
now struggling hard to master the art at Adelaide
Government House. A lady teacher of dancing
attends the vice-regal residence on North Terrace
very frequently, and shows Dudley the goose and
other steps, whilst Lady Le Hunte plays the
piano. He will be one of the debutantes at the
Government House ball.
W. E. Sparke, property owner and wine mer-
chant, "who passed out last week, aged 73, died a
bachelor, but leaves a regiment of nephews and
nieces. The real-estate brigade will be surprised
if Mr. Sparke’sproperty doesn’t pay duty on nearer
a million than £500,000. ’Tis said that one
nephew falls in for nearly half-a-million.
In appearance, “Old Sparkie” was short and
stockily-built; wore an obvious wig, and looked a
plain—a very plain—edition of Mr. Justice Bar-
ton. He made most of his money in the good old
whisky days 40 .years ago. And he was the best
judge of liquor in the State, and an out-and-out
epicure in his food. For years he kept a cook
who was originally in the service of Earl Cowley
when British Plenipotentiary at Paris ; and a
good cook who feeds plenipotentiaries at Paris
generally knows how to boil water without making
it too hard. For the rest, the deceased was fond
of picnics, and dropped his aspirates. His great
pals were Messrs. Fitz Wentworth and “Billy”
Long.
Mr. “ Jack ” Want’s friends will be sorry to
hear that he is ill with congestion of the lungs.
Do you remember that immensely rich old man,
Silas Harding of Victoria, who married a young
second wife and died soon afterwards? The widow
who inherited all the wealth was killed in a vehicle
accident, and her brother and sister, Oldham by-
name, came in for the money. The brother and
his wife are now. living in Sydney. They have
bought Mr. Harry Rickards’ house, “Carthona,”
at Darling Point. Mr. Oldham’s determination
to secure the picture place was just a shade more
acute than the Tivoli man’s reluctance to sell it. It
finally changed owners for £12,000, some £6OOO
more than Mr. Rickards paid for it.
The mereness of man—as shown by advt. :
A LADY, leaving State, would like to meet another
Lady, who would take over nice COTTAGE, with
one gent, boarder, paying high price.
The male boarder has become a chattel to go with
the gas-stove, fixtures and fowls. What is man?
Echo answers, “Something perpendicular, without
feathers.”

ment tones : “ Kubelik’s twins / Another adver-
tisement for his violin-playing. The fuss made
