And she waves a photograph of her six young
Heermanns all seated in a row on a plank.
Mr. D.ui O’Connor was in the midst of one of
his usual rosy sky and flower-crowned speeches at
Miss Violet Mount’s benefit concert meeting. An
impatient gentleman in the gathering, who doesn’t
know a verse of poetry from a Brussels sprout,
scrawled a message to the chairman and sent it up
in the middle of Mr. Daniel’s most combustible
blaze of eloquence. “ I’ll take fifty of the highest
Ericed tickets.” Cheque was pinned to the scrawl.
oprano Violet goes to sing at Buenos Ayres and
other places with tender sounding names before
starting study for grand opera in Milan. Great
interest is being taken in her farewell concert, and
Messrs. Barton, Parry, and Merriman, the hon.
secs, of the three leading musical societies, are
acting for her.
That small, picturesque old lady, Mrs. Rose
Elizabeth Selwyn, who for so long has been
living with her niece, Miss Rose Scott, died
yesterday, aged 80 years. Deceased was the
widow of the late Dean Selwyn of Newcastle.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ A devoted reader of the Argus told me yes-
terday that, since reading that Edward VII. had
signified his royal leaning towards knee-breeches,
he had decided not to order anew dress suit 1 until
he sees if Australia will follow the kingly example. 1
I wanted him to plunge on his luck, but his faith
was hot strong enough. Then I suggested that he
should get an ordinary suit, and stipulate that, if
the blessed change came, the tailor would cut a
bit off it without extra charge, but even that idea
failed to satisfy him.
“ Basso Horace Stevens faces the matrimonial
rails this month, with piquant Miss Eleanor
Chapman. The singer is a dentist by profession,
also an oarsman and a wheat-rust specialist. He
has the elderly choir-boy look of pious melancholy,
but is cheerful for all that. His own hymeneal
chant will be chorused at St. Paul’s Cathedral at
the wedding.
“ Spring head-gear sprouted green and magenta
tones at the Caulfield meeting on Saturday. Miss
Muriel Cumming, the pick of a large, frolicsome
bunch, flourished a saucer-lidded hat having an
undergrowth of roses and mignonette close to the
thatch. Mrs. Clive Miller showed another spring
sample in a shady hat of lilac with floral illustra-
tions about the roof. Mrs. T. Tait heightened the
effect of her forget-me-not blue eyes by trimmings
of blue about her black garb. Mrs. Edward
Miller flourished her well-whaleboned form in dull
mole cloth and a roundish toque swarming with
magenta blossoms.
“ The frilly element descended in thousands on
the three dress shows of the week, and, as usual,
figured in a pitched battle’ round the hat
stands. The significant import label attached to
a head-piece is argument (in the shopper’s mind)
of its being the only specimen of its kind, and it
is scrambled for, unless the price be prohibitive.
The suburban pinner-up is the most comic sight at
a town spread of finery. She is out for points. Her
eyes are hawklike in their craving, and a surrep-
titious pencil dots down trifles that are to aid her
suburban notions. The amateur who runs up her
own blouses is battling too for points, twining the
dummies round, peering into the stitching, the
fastenings, the gussets, the belt pulleys and other
minutiae by which the novice knows how the
Paquins of the trade acquire their effects.
“A sequel to a rapid flirtation among mixed
couples has the same old moral in it. No. 1 wife
philandered with No. 2 husband, while No. 1
husband glared indignantly, and No. 2 wife went
into hysterics. When she (No. 2) recovered she
went abroad to shed her remaining tears on an
Atlantic liner. No. 1 husband bore his anger for
a long time, and then fled silently. In vain the
frivolous wife sought for a note of farewell or an
inky curse among the toilet mats or her bonnet
boxes. The pair of philanderers, being thus left
with a free field and lots of favor, are tolerating
each other with ill-concealed disgust. The attrac-
tion of married folks’ flirtations lies in the
obstacles. Remove the obstacles and the charm
has gone.
“ The Christian scientist is a guileless or very
naive person. One of her, drooping on the virgin
stalk, relates innocently how, returning from toil
the other afternoon, a feeling of heavy fatigue
tugged at her ‘ all-is-well ’ faith. The tiredness
would not be denied. Corns or bunions or chil-
blains cried out for recognition. ‘ I have no
corns, ’ she told her tired spirit. But the spirit
mocked her and called her feet to a halt.
She sought rest. Then in her own words:
‘ One of our most effective readers hap-
pened to look in. I jumped up and ran
to meet him. “In time for tea !” I told him,
and I made some, trying greatly to be brave and
cheerful. He took the tea, enjoyed it, and,
finishing, said : “Mychild, I’m going to prescribe
for you ! ” I said it was nonsense ; I was quite
bright. But hepersisted, and ordered me into my
easy chair, with my feet tucked up. And he read
to me. By degrees all fatigue, all pain, all irrita-
tion had vanished. A feeling of perfect peace
stole to my spirit. On and on he read. Pre-
sently I slept. When I woke, the reader was
gone, and I was as a giant refreshed. Oh, the
power of the word qf light! ’ She seemed quite
serious. It was impossible to make any close
inquiries about Mr. Reader. Of such is the
naivete of the Christian Scientist.
“ The Vic. artists went into masks and dominoes
for their annual fling at the Galleries on Saturday
night. It wasn’t a wild Bohemian Quartier Latin
revel exactly, the girls (burglar-masked) refusing
to be ‘ mashed ’ sans ceremony by any frisking
cavalier. The set that took the bath bun was
that of The Order of the Bath—men in pyjamas
and the girls in towel garb. Consul Pinschof’s
two jaunty daughters scored in their towel rigs.
One impressive dasher—Mrs. J. Brind—as a
Souter lady, with a white cat silhouetted on a
staff, and various other snowy purrers scrolled on
the tail of a wonderfully cut black velvet gown,
was a handsome compliment to the Bulletin
artist.
“ A dainty little grand-daughter of Oculist Gray
(Miss Molly Wilson) played a small part in
‘Trilby.’ Besides a few lines, she had a festive
leg to fling about and flung it to good artistic
account. A cousin (Miss Ruby Gray) drifted into
the grease-ranks for a while, and drifted out again
without making any splash.
“ Nice girls have nice new posts open to them
now as assistants to doctors and dentists. A
Yank, medico started the idea, and it catches on
sure. The fair help in a nurse’s uniform un-
skewer'* the rich ones hat, takes charge of her
hair, and hovers gently near during lance probings
or dental squirms. Sometimes she sympatheti-
cally holds the patient’s hand. The male kind of
patient finds this a delicate attention.
“ Any old sort of weather is considered season-
able for the golfer. Three inches of rain and
spacious mud-puddles do not interfere with the
slie-devotees here. Away they tramp in riding-
boots or goloshes with gaiter continuations ; oil-
skin coats cover three-quarters of their persons
and a deep kid border the rest, and a sou-wester
or rough-weather hat, is lashed on taut. Sand-
ringham sees them in all weathers. After a down-
pour they wring themselves out, squat by the
Club House fire a while, and then tramp home,
out of curl but seldom with colds.
“ A dame who looms high in the public and
social view was asked to adjudicate in a difficult
little matter a while ago. In a certain society,
over which the dame presides, two members had a
grievous falling-out. One stood to her ground
doggedly (which is something stronger and better
than cattishly), and the other cried for revenge.
The dame had a confab and a pick of a
chicken’s wing in private with each of the parties,
and peace was proclaimed. The confabs were
under the seal of secrecy, but one can easily guess
the trend of each little tete-a-tete. To agree in toto
with each, and ask for a large tolerance for the
other misguided wretch, was the only course that
could result in perfect peace. The one irresistible
appeal in all scratch skirmishes is, ‘ She’s not a
lady. That’s the trouble, poor thing.’ So Cat
No. lis mollified. Same thing judiciously served
to Cat 2 leaves her purring with amicable intent.
“ The beauty man of a certain theatrical co.
frequents a cafe for his scone, always with his
wife. Most girl onlookers do not know they are
a prosaic married pair, and spin fancies galore.
Most often the man is silent. His profile remains
in unbroken line, and a favorite pose is to lean his
noble bump of veneration on a rigid forefinger,
while his wife laps up her tea with relish.
Romantic girls have invented the yarn that
Adonis wilts because the lady of the appetite is
unconcerned. But really Adonis has a rebellious
digestion, and wifie a grand passion for muffins,
and away in the background is a baby that howls
healthfully for barley-sugar.
“It’s not every charmer who knows in the
course of a wooing when to skip away. An in-
teresting professional was just about to fall a
victim to the wiles of Phyllis, when the fair one
levanted to a farm for the cream and roses of
rusticity. Mamma keeps the swain posted with
rural notes to date, and maintains his anxiety on
the bubble. By the time the coy Phyllis returns
that ardent young man will be ripe for a proposal.
If not, all calculations will be out.
“All the Convent ‘old girls’ rushed to ‘The
Gondoliers ’ to note how their chums, Dolly
Castles and Aggie Thorn, shaped. They were
absorbed in the twinklings of the once bashful
Dolly. That young scorer foots it gaily, flashing
lingerie laces right in the R.C. eye, and philan-
dering with Gondolier Kenningham with the most
sprightly comic opera knowingness. The Convent
chicks looked on amazed ; Dolly had beenllearning
a few things on tour despite (or perhaps with the
help of) a sheep-dog. Aggie Thorn was found
to have advanced in pertness, pirouetting, and the
cosy-corner business. But she always evinced
possibilities that way. The other lady didn’t.
“ Miss Beatrice Day says the two eminent
actresses who charm her most are Duse and Mrs.
Pat Campbell, because both are deeply passionate
in their stage manner, and suggest rather than
flaunt tempest and fire. Bernhardt is inflammable
always, on wires always, still never. The intense
quiet of Duse and Mrs. Pat is a powerful force.
Both women have had tragic lives, winds up Miss
Day significantly. In that case it would seem that
from the wreck of happiness the artist nature
acquires an impressive repose. Moral to artists :
Avoid the capacity for devouring 12 hard-boiled
eggs at a sitting, like the abnormal Bernhardt.

“ The latest party notion is the ‘ Photograph
Tea.’ All the guests bring their earliest photos,
and a guessing, or recognition, competition sets in.
It is a difficult thing to recognise in a naked babe
making goo-goo eyes from a ‘ property ’ shell the
Titian-haired dazzler of the day. Some cats do
another kind of guessing. They know from the
occasional embroidery of the little baby clothes
what year ’twas and how many are to be reckoned
to the photographed one’s credit from that epoch.
‘Claudia must be 27,’ whispers one to t’other.
That’s the form much of the guessing takes.
“A smart little business woman took a novel
means of dunning a Johnnie Who was behind in
his payments. After sending earnest solicitations
for the settlement of the account a score of times,
she got herself up sprucely, and took the first train
after breakfast to the young man’s home. She
was conducted to the drawing-room. There she
waited with her demurest smile, while Suzette
took her business card to the tardy one. Excuses
of a headache, ‘would call at the lady’s shop
later,’ fell on obdurate ears. ‘l’ll wait,’ said the
early caller, with a gentle smile. Then the family
came in one by one, demanding to know the little
lady’s business. ‘ Just a bill,’ she explained. ‘ A
few pounds .merely, but one must live.’ Then one
of the family wept, and all the'others were angry,
and the account was settled on the: spot.

“The Ledinghams, well-known golfers, are back
from a globe-trot, the dame with hats, hair and
frocks up-to-date. Six months were found
sufficient to allow of a gad round Europe, and a
look at the Louvre, and a round of shopping. The
pair talk of the Victorian coterie in the Big
Smoke, and the condescension of ex-Governors to
the clan. Condescension isn’t the word applied,
but it suggests itself. ‘The Linlithgows are
kindness itself’ ! Of course, any globe-trotter
saying that scores nine points for herself and only
t’other goes to the Linlithgows. It’s an unwise
gadder who returns and gives out that the Brasseys,
or the Hopetouns, or the Clarkes, were snorty.
It argues a snub right away. ‘ I found all the ex-
Vice-Regals absolutely charming. So nice ! ’
That’s what the wise remark.
“ A Collins-street man complains that the confi-
dence extended by upholstered Beauty to the doc-
tor is not stretched to the dentist. Up to the final
tug at the tooth, the slie-patient contrives to keep
her transformation, if not exactly in place, some-
where on her head. ‘lt is ludicrous,’ one com-
plains, ‘ to see the distracted efforts of the women
to keep their hair on. What do we care if they
wear a whole cushion on their heads, as long as we
can collar the tooth. One day when a patient of
mine who wouldn’t run to the expense of gas, but
wanted a tough old molar removed without pain,
held to her wig through all preparations. I asked
her brutally to drop the hair or I would drop the
operation. The wig went, but her tears streamed
over my hands and filled me with regret. Ever
since I have let women hang on to their trans-
formations and other delusions.’
An Adelaide correspondent:—
Social Adelaide is this year complaining of a mayor
whose entertainments, so far, have been few and far
between, and scarcely lavish. In place of the usual
festive hop at the Exhibition Building, at which 3000 or
4000 citizens were wont to disport themselves, present
Worship invited 900 to a “ bread and butter ” dance at
the Town Hall. That meant limiting the cards. In one
family, where two daughters had always been invited,
only one received an invitation. Mamma thought it
was an accident, and accepted for Mr., Mrs. and the
Misses ; whereat she was honored with a brief note, re-
questing her to examine her invitation and correct her
error. In another case pa and ma only were invited, and
inquired if they might relinquish their invitations in favor
of their son and daughter. An intimation was received that
they might do nothing of the kind, as their space was
badly wanted. So there are gruriibles. But why should
the Mayor be called upon to “entertain ” at all? Wouldn’t
it be, on the whole, better to seek out a Mayor, not for his
hospitality, but, say, for his ability to get a good tram ser-
vice going? The Adelaide horse trams, by the way, are
responsible for anew iniquity—they have set in motion
the worst pun on record. “ The Norwegians are more
fortunate than we are ; they’ve got rid of their oss-car.”
Then the humorist laughs horribly.
Adelaide will see a big thing in weddings on 16th
August, when Gladys, younger daughter of Mr. Lewis
Cohen, and Mr. Ralph Krantz are to be married. Invitations
issued are so numerous (rumor puts them at from 500 to
as many thousand) that the Town Hall has been engaged
for the ceremony. Bride-elect is a friendly clinging
young thing of 19 or so, with a very charming manner,
and, being one of a family of smart dressers, should look
“sweet” on the great occasion. Bridegroom is not so
well known, but is said to be afflicted with money.
Now it’s my turn again :
Consul-General Pinard and Madame, when
they entertain their countrymen at Double Bay,
throw crow-feeting years behind them and become
as little children. Lately they had a large party,
all grown up, and they played oranges and lemons,
and ring-a-ring-a-rosy, hide-and-seek, and cat’s-
cradle, and if they didn’t come down to noughts
and crosses it was because they didn’t think of that
exciting pastime.

NERVES ON FIRE
Neuralgia for 17 Years.
Worst Case on Record Cured
by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“ TF I talked from now till the day of judg-
ment I couldn’t tell you one-half what I
suffered with Neuralgia,” said Mrs. Eliza
M. Bevan, Hill-St., Forbes. “It tortured
me for 17 long years. I was nearly out of my
mind with pain when I started Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People. After the second box
the Neuralgia began to ease up. But the pain
was stubborn, and it was weeks before the pills
were able to drive it clean out of my system. I
had to take seventeen boxes altogether. But then
I was cured for good. When I wrote to Sydney
and told Dr. Williams about my case, he said it
must have been the worst case of Neuralgia in all
Aqstralia, for he had never known another case
that needed anything like so many boxes of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills to cure it.
“Years ago my health began to break down. I
felt myself growing weaker every month. I could
not keep my strength up, for I lost .all appetite.
Some days I wouldn’t have a bite inside my
mouth. Other days, when I did eat a little, I
suffered agony with indigestion. Everything I
swallowed seemed to lie like a ton weight on my
chest. Eating gave me so much torture that 1
grew to hate the sight of food. I got thinner and
thinner. From one week’s end to another, I wai
too tired to move. I let myself run down to the
lowest ebb. Every scrap of strength left me. 1
was as wdkk as a baby. There wasn’t a month
passed without me having a fainting fit. If I sat
down to read for awhile, I used to feel tins faint-
ness stealing over me. Sometimes when speaking
to a friend in the street, I had to excuse myself
quickly so that 1 could get away and save myself
from dropping down in a dead swoon.
“When the Neuralgia was bad a hot, burning
rush of pain would shoot from my chin up to the
crown of my head every few minutes. The agony
was so frightful that I had often to cry out. I
couldn’t bear the slightest touch on my face.
Everj time I opened my mouth a pain that I
could never describe darted up through mv brain.
My head felt as if the top were lifting off. If any-
one came behind me and put their hands on my
chair, it sent a spasm of agony through my face
and head far worse than if I had been burnt with
a red-hot iron. No one ever had Neuralgia like I
had.
“When the doctor couldn’t cure my Neiira 1 -’ ,
I thought the terrible pain must come front my
teeth,” Mrs. Bevan added. “I must have one
fully twenty times to have them out —hi i the
dentist wouldn’t draw them. lam glad now (hat
he wouldn’t. I tried no end of liniments and
lotions, but I might as well have saved my money,
for all the good they did me. At last, I trie Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. I took two
boxes before I could see that they were doing m ;
the least good—but I would have gladly taken
dozen boxes to be one day free from the Neuralgia.,
By the time I had taken the seventeen boxes, I
was back in splendid health. Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills gave me an appetite such as I never hoped
to have. For three years I have not had a single
sign of my old Neuralgia—so it is plain that when
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured me, they cured me
for good.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured Mrs. Bevan
because they actually made new blood for her.
That one thing is all they do—but they do it well.
They don’t act on the bowels. They don’t bother
with mere symptoms. They go straight down to
the root of the matter in the blood, and cure that.
And from that one root, bad blood, spring half
the common ailments such as bloodlessness,
indigestion, biliousness, headaches, backaches,
lumbago, rheumatism, sciatica, neuralgia, ner-
vousness, St. Vitus’ dance, spinal complaint,
general weakness, and the special secret ailments
that attack women and growing girls, whenever
their blood supply becomes irregular or overtaxed.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure all these blood
troubles, because they actually make new blood.
If you are not quite sure whether your illness is
caused by bad blood, write for free medical ad vice
to Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney. From
the same address you can also order the genuine
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills by mail, 2s. 9d. a box;
u..; boxes 15s. 3d., post free.

Afine example of journalistic enterprise was
provided recently by a young Australian, brother
of the central figure of a sensational Northern
tragedy, who wrote by the first post after the
arrest, offering portraits of his relative to all the
illustrated papers at a price commensurate with
the seriousness of the case.
A statue in Sydney to the late P. N.
Russell is talked about. In private buildings or
private grounds statues have a meaning that only
concerns the proprietors of the premises, but before
being obtruded on public places the public should
have a say. The late ironfounder would be a
dreadful person for the public to gaze upon till
his image wore out.
Oliver Bainbridge, now off to China,
found Australians stone deaf to his alleged dis-
coveries about Black Jews. In the first place,
his theories were sawn out in a manner which, tor
dreary woodenness, was almost a record ; and in
the second place, many of his listeners knew more
about Papua than he did, and didn’t even find his
ignorance picturesque.
N. S. W. Arbitration Court has been sin-
gularly unlucky. One of its first members, Mr.
Cruickshank, is invalided. Now of the present
court, the President, Judge Heydon, is ill; the
employers’ representative, Mr. Wright, is suffering
from pneumonia ; and the employees representa-
tive, Mr. S. Smith, is also seriously ill, and just
minaged the other day to crawl down and adjourn
proceedings.

mercial M.L.A. He tried to understand things ;

thing he prided himself on his private business—
taller an’ ’ides.
E. W. Carey, the man who left a building
society lamenting in Melbourne, was sighted a
couple of months ago in New York by a former
friend. Carey was an artist in assuming the airs
of high respectability. One of his latest per-
formances was to urge the blackballing of a
candidate for a, certain club on the ground of
impecuniosity - and general failure to pay up.
Judge Gordon (S.A.), whose eldest son
d'ed of consumption contracted while on service
in South Africa, has just lost his only surviving
sin by a shooting accident. The deceased was
only 21 vears old, and reckoned the best amateur
rider, as well as the most daring, m Adelaide.
He was on horseback when he was shot, and his
dead body was dragged along till found with one
foot in the stirrup.
A few of the late Duncan Gillies’ Tory
friends —Speaker Frank Madden and the like—
are moving to perpetuate his memory with a bust
in Vic. Parliament House. There will be no need
of any memorial of Gillies so long as the unfortu-
nate State that he bossed for some years has to
pay interest on his wild-cat railway loans. Still,
if a memorial must be put up, there is-nothmg
like a bust. Gillies made the biggest public bust
Victoria ever saw.
The late W. E. Sparke, of Elizabeth Bay
(Sydney) made his early coin in wines and spirits.
He never married, and never received ladies of a,ny
‘Tade at his elegant home at the Bay, excepting
his immediate relatives. He was a Sydney native,
born in the old Australian Inn, Lower George-
street. Yet he imported from England every
article in use at “St. Monans.” Some, years ago
he met with a serious accident in his bath, through
the explosion of the heating apparatus, which
somewhat disfigured him; And, strtfnge to say, he
died in the bath. That same bath is a work of
art, hewn out of a solid block of Carrara marble—
and imported, of course.
Judge Heydon, just appointed by Attorney-
General Wade as acting judge of N.S. W. Arbitra-
tion Court with the status of a Supreme Court
iudge was given his post as District Court judge
bv the Lyne Ministry, in spite of the strong
opposition of W. R Crick ; and Wade was at the

antagonism to Judge Heydon is said to have had

undertaken by the Reid Ministry (of which
Carruthers was a member).
Thomas Bent became reminiscent when up
in Gippsland lately, and moralised on the vanity
of human achievements and the ingratitude of
democracies—especially that of Gippsland. W hen
he went up to Maffra years ago, as Minister ot
Railwavs, to open the so-far unpayable Maffra-to-
Stratford line, the whole countryside turned out
to greet him, and a triumphal arch notified that
“ Maffra welcomes Maffra’s choice.” But when,
a few years later, as a rejected politician and a
busteddand-boomer, he journeyed therein a small,
seedy way to sell some property he held m the
locality, no one met the train, no one knew him,
even the publican who had effusively welcomed
him before answered his call for a beer with the
stony stare of oblivion. Bent seems to be late m
discovering this regrettable trait of humanity, but
at least he has learnt it at last. Some men never
learn.
Geo. Miln’s wife, whom Australia knew as
Miss Louise Jordan (a most accentuated Amur-
rikan actress), published her first novel the other
week, a story about the familiar early married
woman, with the wrong husband beside her. and
the right man waiting for her in the future.
Louise Jordan Miln inherited a literary turn, and
her journalistic style is good, Her maternal
grandfather was Professor Wilson—“ Christopher
North,” editor of Blackvvood’s Magazine and
writer of “ Noctes Ambrosianse. 1 robably Mrs.
Miln’s mother was the Miss Wilson whose sweet-
heart hadn’t the pluck to “speak to papa. The
damsel carried his proposal to papa, and the old
man was agreeable. “But,” said he, if youi
suitor is so shamefaced, I had better write my
reply, and pin it to your back.” Miss Wilson
returned to her sighing swain (William Aytoun),
and looked away from him whilst he read the
note. It was addressed to him with the authors
compliments.
Ex-Premier Butler, of S.A., is a sad,
depressed, weepy, self-conscious man whom
nature intended to be a black horse dragging a
black hearse. He always has a grievance against
some one, and he doesn t fight over his grievance ;
he weeps. He has now written to the papers to
weep that the Price Government is m some
respects onlv carrying out his ideas, and he re-
gards that g,s a Bitter Wrong. But the House
didn't throw out Butler because he had these
ideas. It ejected him because it wasn’t sure that
he had them, because it didn’t believe lie would
carry them out if he had them, pfecause he
had other ideas it didn’t approve of, and
because he lacked some more ideas. Also
it threw him out because of his weeps. Ho
far as Parliament was concerned Butler re-
tained office as Premier for a less time than
Sudden Solomon. The weeper was made Premier
in recess, when Jenkins fled to England and the
Agent-Generalship, and the Assembly, by a large
majority, told him to go and weep somewhere else
within an hour of its meeting. Solomon and his
large whiskers were in office for a week during a
Parliamentary session, although it is true that he
was also shot out directly the House got a chance
to shoot.

Geo. Swinburne, the Minister who has
charge of Victoria’s splendid Water Bill, and is
struggling to secure for the people absolute control
of all water supply in the State, is chairman of
directors of the most prosperous private water
supply in Australia —the one the Broken riili
mines and town depend upon. But Swinburne
only came into the co. some time after it was
formed, and when its rights had been secuied,
and because of it he knows what a valuable thing
a water supply is to own. Swinburne’s specialties
are gas and water —he is probably the best-known
gas engineer in Australia.
WHEN THE GALL COMES.
What is Australia’s chance?
The alliterative thanks of Edwards, the
Vic. homicide, to press, public and police for their
kindness, courtesy and consideration lead one to
think that he should have been a pressman. His
descriptive article about Skinner s scattered skull
or the gurgling gore of Girrgaree would have been
a small fortune to a Sunday paper.
Recently passed out in Adelaide a harm-
less old “ looney ” known as “Dennis,” who used
to haunt the S.A. Treasury and State Bank
offices. The old chap reckoned that the whole
State rightfully belonged to him. He would
hobble into the Treasure, and in the mossiest
brogue request that his revenues be handed over.
The clerks, with profuse apologies, would regret
that a full statement had not yet been made up.
and would tender the monopolist threepence on
account and a bundle of old Gaze? ms, from
which he was told he could get some idea of
the aino int remaining due to him. Dennis was
always satisfied, and never complained in the
press, like some of his fellow capitalists, of the
dilatoriness of State officials.
There lately died at Newcastle (N.S.W.),
full of veirs and sanctity, the once notorious Poll
Cott whose life story was published a decade ago
under the title “ A Tale of a Termagant. ’ As a
giddv colleen in her teens, Mary Maguire was
transported hither from the Ould So t for the
weirin’ of the green, or some other frivol, and, by
the Governor’s permission, was soon allowed to
marry one Cott, a settler. The price of her matri-
monial stock went up far above rubies, and re-
mained there until the early death of her only
child, when it slumped below rum. She accused
the doctor who attended her infant of having
murdered it. For this alleged defamation of
character the bereaved mother got three months,
at the end of which period the doctor was sorry it
wasn t more, for she went for him personally,
wrecked his surgery, and scratched the beauty off
several of the six policemen who were necessary to
her re-arrest. Thenceforward the termagant let
herself go altogether, and for 40 years trod the
path of sin which leads to 257 convictions, chiefly
for drunkenness and police-assaults. At this
stage her emotions took a religious turn, and for
the last 20 years she was a local show exhibit
of the Salv’Army.
Of the Price Government (S.A.), two
(Peake and O’Loughlin) are natives of the State,
and the others (Price and Kirkpatrick) are English-
born. Price is a Methodist lay-preacher, like
almost everybody else in S.A., and a total
abstainer, like almost everybody else in b.A.
O’Loughlin is a cold-water man and a Koman
Catholic.
“ E.” on the lady barrister
Miss Grata Flos Greig was admitted to practice at the
Victorian Bar last week, and Chief Justice Madden ex-
pressed a polite hope that the honor of her “ noble ’’ pro-
fession —how these lawyers delight to butter themsel' es .
—would be as well preserved in her hands as in the hauler
and larger paws of her learned brethren. In the matter
of professional honor Miss Greig is quite likely to outsu.ne
her learned brethren. She may feel womanly scruples
about charging fees which she hasn t attempted to tarn.
She is practically certain to be sober when she conus into
court to plead for her client. She will not adopt a c< arse,
bullying tone to hostile witnesses. In short, she wdl do
everything that becomes a woman, and enneavor
to score on the strength of her sex. And that's where the
unfairness of the fair creature comes in. Miss Greig is
entitled by Act of Parliament to a professional advantage
which men barristers don’t enjoy as yet, and which will
in a measure tend to put wisdom and experience out of
Court if they ever do get it. She can address juries and
Judges, with whom her sexual charm will go for a little,
or go for much, whether they be influenced willingly or
otherwise. There can be no equality between two sexes-
of pleaders whilst the pleaded persons are of one sex.
The history of legal proceedings thusfar has bristled with
instances of male susceptibility to the arts and graces of
female defendants, plaintiffs and witnesses. The sex sense
is a good deal stronger than the moral sense of justice
when it has to decide between a fascinating woman and a
mere man. This has been the experience of human nature
where the administration has rested with the male animals.
And if women ran the judicial show, the sex sense would
have greater power for injustice in so far that a sweetly
pretty plaintiff would be terribly disadvantaged in com-
petition with an ordinary young man defendant ; the
man would get sympathy for his manhood, whilst the
woman excited something of suspicion, if not animosity,
for her good looks. These objections to the licensing of
Miss Flos Matilda Greig and any future Miss Greig are
as old as the most discredited doctrines of Toryism. The
writer is fully conscious of his incapacity to accept
advanced ideas as regards female barristers. But he
reckons that human nature hasn’t budged an inch since
God made it. As long as man and woman continue to
exercise a purely sex influence over one another the
admission of women pleaders among male pleaders in a
court composed of men only will be palpably injudicious.

Aboriginalities.
Fremantle (W.A.) Customs authorities
have been learning something of the ways of the
wily Indian immigrant. Nine months ago, Mood
Chand arrived per steamer at Fremantle, and
explained suavely that he was a member of the
Indian civil service visiting Australia to study
local official customs. On the strength of his
yarn, backed up by testimonials, he was granted
a domicile certificate, entitling him to remain in
the Commonwealth for six months. Next, the
Customs officials learned that the distinguished
visitor was grafting in Fremantle for another
colored gentleman, and inquiries followed. Mood,
being asked to explain certain points, blandly
stated that he hadn’t any convincing explanation
about him, but would find securities for his
departure if his certificate were extended for
another six months. Vexed at his cool cheek, the
authorities summoned Mood to appear at the
Customs office for a personal consultation. Mood
replied that he’d see them thugged first. Then the
authorities swooped down in wrath, and gave the
illustrious visitor a practical illustration of “local
official customs ” by bundling him on board the
Orontes and starting him back to India, with his
turban badly disarranged.

“Jim Pan.”: Re “ Gilrooney ” and the
bushman’s almost inexhaustible vocabulary of
occupation labels (B. 27/7/’O5) —he has omitted a
name which the angry hullock-driver calls himself
when he wants to heap contempt instead of
curses on lazy, troublesome bullocks—“cow-con-
ductor.” Of course, a bullock-pilot has to be
outrageously mad to use such awful language. A
boundarv-rider on Whycanya station told me that
the best hack he ever rode went and suicided, by
cutting its throat on the barbed wire round the
cultivation paddock, the very day after being
called a cow.

How the country cow-prodder clings to his
filth. The millc-and- butter folk of Port Fairy
district (Vic.) have risen in a unanimous body
and ordered their local member to violently
oppose the appointment of Government dairy
inspectors for their unclean shire. The spectacle
of an infuriated cOwist hanging on to his dirty
cow-bail to the last gasp, and being finally torn
away from it by the Law, is too painful for words.
This affection for filth on the part of the moo-
urging fraternity seems to suggest that there isn’t
much difference between the cow and the person
who drags the pale fluid from its baggv parts
Everybody who has studied the bull’s'aunt at
close range knows that it takes a fiendish delight
in wading through slime, and it seems to have
learned its dirty habits from its owner.

“ Nymsie”: “ 8.5.” cannot understand
how it is boomerang throwing is not adopted as a
branch of athletics (B. 17/7/’O5). Anyone who has
tried throwing the curved curse understands. In
the early sixties boomerang and spear throwing
were amongst the chosen outdoor amusements of
youth and brawn in the N.E. district of Victoria.
Possessing boomerangs as a craze beat swopping
colored meerschaums—then a habit much in vogue,
and throwing spears was an accomplishment every
white buck of note sought to excel in. A number
of young full-bloods from the tribe (or tribes) that
used to corroboree at the junction of the Ovens
and Iving rivers occasionally lingered round
Wangaratta and other townships, selling weapons,
and giving lessons to aspirants in the native arts.
As long as the dusky tutors were sober our limbs
were safe ; but when King Billy was full of beer
we had to scatter for our lives, as the erratic
skull-cracker came home to roost. Some of us got
fairly proficient at the game, but I have never
found a white thrower, and few blacks, who could
be relied on at “boomeranging” two days run-
ning. So after the blacks had gone off ’possuming
(in those days the craving for ’possum was stronger
at times with the healthy rovers than the desire
for rum), we used to oil our boomerangs, and pile
our spears in the drawing-room spare corners as
curiosities, awaiting the return of our mentors.
Their visits grew gradually less as they were called
to the happy ’possum-tree Up Above ; and, closer
settlement becoming the order of things, there were
no large enough spaces available for practice.
Boomeranging gets back on a man in the long run
worse than politics, and always leaves a scar, if
not a tombstone. Spear throwing is a fine cold
weather pastime, but where are the spears to
come from ?

“ Robert Hap ” :
“D.11.R.” is off again, and “Elmore” is the victim.
“D.” riddles with adjectives “E.’s” statement that
dingoes do not bark. (I wouldn’t be surprised to hear
that the native dog coo-ees to his mates—especially when
he hears David on the warpath.) In order to prove his
assertion that “the genuine wild dingo” barks, D.11.R.
(B. 3/8/’O5) cites instances where dingoes taken as puppies
have, on arriving at doghood, barked as truly as any
town canine. Well, a parrot in his native state will not
use profane language, but take him young and there are
possibilities in that direction. And if “Dave H.R.” owned
the dingoes that he writes about I would say that their
barking was due to environment. It is no use for
“ D.H.R.” or anybody else to answer this. I have obtained
permission from the Lands Commission to absent myself
for medical treatment, and am fleeing to Africa, where I
won’t see the paper.

“The Prooshan” : To poison the wiliest
dingo with certainty and despatch, fasten the
innards of a sheep to a rope, mount your pony,
and ride round a circle, with diameter of a quarter
of a mile, three times, always dragging the paunch,
&c. Next, take sheep’s liver, and slit the baits
round the edges, top and bottom, being careful to
avoid touching them with the hands ; then insert
strychnine and carbonate of soda alternately in
the slits, put all baits into a bag hung round the
body, mount the pony again, and pitch the baits
along the circuit at intervals of a hundred yards
or so, using a fork in sowing the dingo seed. Next
morning go round early and remove the dingoes.
Many advocate the use of clotted blood for bait
during winter, but nothing tempts dingo like liver.

“ Gip ” : Once met half-a-dozen wild busli-
men in Sydney—they had come in as witnesses in
a land squabble. Some of them
had never before left the sta-
tion where they were bred, ex-
cept for a jaunt to the nearest
post-office. On the day of
their arrival they huddled to-
gether in a room at the top of.
a lofty pub., frightened to go
out. Curiously enough, when
they did venture into the
streets, it wasn’t the shop-
windows or the crowds that in-
terested them, but the horses
in lemonade carts and cabs. I
saw them nearly come to blows
one day, near the Strand, over
a horse which one man thought
“ the dead spit o’ Jim Rorke’s
chestnut.” The others didn’t
agree with him. One old fellow
used to walk up and down the
cab-ranks, examining the gee-
gees’ mouths and feet. Another
used to tie his handkerchief to
a prominent post near his pub.,
“so he’d know it agen.” He
never could understand why
the wipe disappeared. The hurrying business
men mystihed them exceedingly. ” “ Was there
races on?” they ask, in the soft bush voice.
“ Where was they tearin’ to ? ” But Sydney was
a dull place to them.

“Pommery” : Stories of champagne sup-
pers and explosion of big cheques during the
rabbit boom in Wilcannia (N.S.W.) about 1887
are greatly exaggerated. I lived there—in fact, I
caught the pioneer rabbit on the south bank of
the Darling in 1882, and acquainted the local
newspaper of the fact, but did not get the fame I
deserved, the editor being afraid of injuring the
town’s reputation. The rabbit invasion certainly
caused a lot of money to circulate, but if any rab-
bi ter or any other person spent £5 in painting the
public-house of a sunset hue, it was always mag-
nified to £lo—perhaps £5O. Trappers did not
stay out long enough to earn big cheques—their
thirst was too great. Of course there were
isolated instances. I knew one man who made
£l2O in six weeks, but he got in early on a good
patch, where the bunny crop was "thick, and
received fid. per scalp. He spent none of his
cheque on chanipagne ; beer was good enough for
him, and he didn t beer much either. There was
far more money spent before the rabbit invasion
than after it.

is a child in disposition, and so

worse. Maoris have caste dis

They are divided into chiefs and

who are not chiefs or their rela-
tions. The distinctions are not
very strictly kept, but the chiefs
have certain dignities in the way
of feathers and carved green-
stone amulets that are now very
scarce and have always been very
valuable. A chiefs daughter to’d
me that at onetime a chief would
barter fifty subjects for one
greenstone ornament. She pro-
mised me one, but didn’t keep her pro-
mise. A Maori interpreter told me there would
have been trouble if she had.
Brisbane has now a genuine sundowner,
the real live article, hall-marked on every link.
He has camped upon a vacant piece of ground in
Boundary-street, in the heart of the city. The
fir it morning about 200 stopped and stared at the
wanderer, who was sleeping in. He usually sleeps
in. Each night lie rolls himself up in his blanket
and tent, and Brisbane finds him so when it is
going to work next day. The police have tried to
make him move on without avail. He warned
the force off in the sacred name of “Private
Property”, and “Private Property” being in
England, and his agent lost, stolen, or strayed, the
sundowner remains in possession. He is fast be-
coming one of the local institutions. Mothers
take their children for an outing to see him, just
as if he were a whole zoo. If he stays much
longer the Government will have to put' down a
light line of railway and run excursions to him.

“Jimmie Pannikin” : At present, owing
to seasonable rains, thousands of healthy young
desert peas are to be found on the green hills
round Broken Hill. For many years past none of
the peas growing wild have been allowed to seed,
so eagerly were the glorious blooms sought after ;
yet the present crop is peculiarly prolific. How
many years of Centralian baking can the seeds
endure before perishing ? By the way, good old
text books state that the pea will not bear trans-
planting. Hundreds are successfully transplanted
in Broken Hill, but few folk can grow the pea
from seed.

But a bloomin’ dog destroyed me—there the dreadful fact
remains!

queens and kings ;

togs;

Ali ! we never sees our folly till the day it is too late!

“ Mars ” : There must be some kind of
freemasonry amongst Hindu hawkers.
Here and there exist cockies who are ue-
nighted enough to harbor such vermin,
and when any hawker new to that
road comes along he never fails
to make straight for such a
haven. Being a white Aus-
tralian myself I never buy
from Hindus, and they, do
not trouble me ; but one ■
day, tempted by being ab-
solutely out of soap, I
fell. After that every
hawker who passed that
way called, and forcible
measures were needed for
several weeks to restore
my old state of immunity.

“ Aquila ” : An account appeared in print
a few days ago of a pair of eagles [aquila audax)
attacking a woman and child near Menno (Vic. .
This eagle is well-named, and cases like this are
not so very uncommon. I, when a child, was sent
up a spur of the Pyrenees, near Ararat, with my
sister, after goats (we .were eight and ten years
old). On our return trip, an eagle attacked us. i
had a long stick, and made desperate slashes at
the bird, and both, myself and my sister howled
vigorously for help. Fortunately we got into the
shelter of some thick undergrowth. With the
spread of settlement the eagle disappears, but he
is still plentiful enough all oyer Victoria. He is
a fine, noble bird, but one is apt to forget this
when attacked. At any rate, it is not the best
time to admire his marvellously muscular build,
his powerful beak, and Ins strong, sinewy toes
and claws.

‘‘ Dandalup ” : “ Belah ’ ’ (B. 13/7/’O5)
paints the Westralian nigger much bladmr than
he is and as to politeness, Belah, having been
a number of years amongst the blacks, must know
that different tribes have different ways. Some
are full of cunning and treachery, while others
are Quite harmless. Out prospecting once we
struck an aboriginal wlio was tlie soul of polite-
S’ and could speak three European languages
He was a wild, hairy-looking individual, and
when we asked him the way to a certain place,
he replied in very good French, ihen he jab-
bered some German, and finally condescended to
speak English. Turned out when a boy he had lived
in Perth with an old lady, who taught him the
lingos. But he could not resist.the longing to get
back to his natural state, and now he was dirty
and happy in all these languages.

“ Rav X.” : Be aboriginal head and other
b as hino- I have tried hard to find something to
admire'in the Centralian blacks, but hitherto
have only discovered a Spartan-like indifference
to pain, which, after all, is probably ab-
sence of feeling. One day at Alice
Springs arrived a nigger from somewhere
lower down the telegraph line. He came
in answer to some sort of pressure which
the blacks are able to bring to bear in
such cases —to settle a matrimonial or
racial squabble, probably the latter.
Several hundred blacks were collected
near the telegraph station. -twenty
separated themselves, and standing our
nigger, armed only with a shield, m front
of them at a distance of 30 paces, pro-
ceeded to throw spears at lnm, each in
his turn. Our friend, with the aid of Ins
shield, juggled with them like Cmque-
valli, dodging all bar the last, which
went through his leg. 1 hen followed
10 minutes’ palaver, after which our mg.
again stood forth and stiffened his back,
stooping slightly forward, and each of the
20 who had thrown spears came up singly,
and made a deep cut in his back with a
stone knife. Before the twentieth had
finished he looked like a walking
slaugliter-yard. One of the operators
who had just come up turned away to
be ill, while Mr. Bradshaw, Telegraph
Master and Protector of Aborigines, told
them through “Old King Charlie that
if thev didn’t stop he “bin git it no
more tucker.” After some yabber, during
which the gins playfully thumped each
other on the head with yam sticks, the
blows sounding like the crack of doom, a
final agreement was come to. Kach one
who had cut our friend picked up a hand-
ful of dirt and rubbed it into the wounds,
after which the nig., having pulled some
spear out of his leg, stalked away to the
westward. Mr. Bradshaw related a case
in which a native had come into the
station with a broken arm, .which had
mortified. It presented a sight which
would have brought a tear to the eye of
Cleopatra’s needle. Nothing remained but
«mnotation. They, therefore, ground up some
butchers’ knives—none other being available
and, with no previous surgical kno^
it off. The patient didn’t even wink, and after
the arm had been bound up, insisted on leaving
for his camp, some 50 miles distant.

plague of mice round Warracknabeal (Vic.), wneie
the wheat grows, is so bad that storekeepers have
had to put butter, cheese, flour, etc., in mice-proof
tins, and now find the rodents chewing their
tobacco. The beasties will presently be leaning
against the door-posts, expeptoratmg, and saying
“ blanky.”. # * *
“Ne’er-do-well”: Mackay man(B. 13/7/’O5)
is quite right as to the Chow wages man m the
cane fields. Slow is no word, for him. He is
absolutely dead. I saw shiploads of Chow im-
ported into Jamaica in the early 80 s. 1 hey usee
to turn out in the fields with a hoe in one hand
and a fan or parasol in the other, llien when the
sugar men grew tired and replaced them wit
coolies, the Chows set to work on their own and
grafted. It didn’t take them long to put money
by then.
* *
“Talk about thieves,” said the timber-
cutter, “yer can’t beat the blokes round ere.
They’d shake anything. When I first started
cutting they used to snavel my logs every night.
Oh, they were ’ot 1 One night me and my brother
Bill tried to catch ’em, and sat with a gun on each
end of the log. We watchedl until about! 2in the
mornin’, when Bill said, “I’m dead cold; I
have a nip.’’ He had a nip out of a flask, and
then fell asleep. I had no one to talk to and oft
I went to sleep too. I woke at daylight, and
there was Bill and me sitting on the bark. .While
we was snoring the blokes had sliov ed the 1( g

“PDF”’ Be finding of bees’ nests
(‘‘ Orthos,” B. 13/7/’O5). In ’9B I knew a man at
Archer field, near Brisbane, to successfully fine a
bee to its nest. He caught the bee at sunset; put
it into a tumbler with cream and sugar, and
covered it with perforated cardboard. He placed
a stake in the ground, and next morning, after
studying the temper of the bee, lie stood by the
stake and let it out. The bee flew straight up-
wards and then in the direction of its nest. Imme-
diately under the bee line, about twenty yarcs
from the stake, he placed a second stake, llalt-
way between the two he then placed a third, so
that all three were in a straight line. He th< n
shifted trie back stake to the'front, and continued
s i doing, keeping all three in a straight line unti,
after travelling a quarter of a mde, he found he
front stake a few feet to the right of the tree
containing the bees’ nest. A straighter line than
a bee’s couldn’t be made by a surveyor with the
use of his theodolite. [The Bulletin wonders if
a miserable old nest wasworth the troubJe.J
“X.”: Be bif Australian snakes. Leopold
F Sachs, formerly of Hermit Park, Townsville,
now of London, had in his possession the skin ot a
carpet snake over 23ft. long, shot somewhere m
tlie Far North.

“ H.J.L.” : Following is the Malay method
of catching the “tortoise-shell” (hawksbill) turtle
in Torres Straits. A lady-turtle is first caught
(during the breeding-season) and made fast by a
chain rove through the tail-plates and secured to a
stone or an anchor on a reef. Ihe Malays then
steal up in small craft, at high water, and spear
the gentleman-turtles, attracted by the lady s
frantic endeavors to slip her cable. [To which a
Bulletin man who has boon there: As tar b
my experience goes, you might as well try to spear
a brick wall as a swimming hawksbill. J

POLITICAL
POINTS
Wesfcralian Labor Congress accepted pretty
well as it stood the tentative general platform sub-
mitted to it, but stuck at the State lotteries plank,
and finally ruled it out.
Queensland immigration is at last going
ahead under the stimulus of a semi-Labor
Government. Last half-year the State gained
1000 by excess of arrivals over departures.
Before even the formal del /;e on the new
Australian Government's policy was over, Reid
was clamoring in the Foreign-tv. i'.e press to know
why Denikin had not settled the capital site
question, the Redistribution of Seats Bill, and
the Tariff Commission report.
The Commonwealth has promptly dis-
covered that there a Labor Cabinet in South
Australia, ft is possible now to get civil replies
from Adelaide on Australian matters. Jenkins
always replied as if the Commonwealth had been
asking for a free ticket to his circus.
This is the very moderate platform
adopted by the Queensland Sugar Workers’
Union :
Equitable wage in all branches of the industry ; only
white labor to be employed in milk, also, when available
and efficient, in the fields ; shorter working days ; a daily
and weekly wage in place of the pres nt contract system";
shearers’ system of dieting where practicable; better
accommodation.
THE HINDRANCE.
DEAKIN: “ You'd better yet out of the road before
Australia comes along with a gun!"
REID: “I don’t care! I'll obstruct the business
of the country with mg last breath!”
This was mentioned in the daily papers
last week :—-
The Federal Government is to be asked to consider the
question of obtaining- an obsolete man-of-war for the pur-
poses of a training ship.
Which is a good way to train up an obsolete crew.
Why should Australia always aim at being oh-
solete ?
Remark by an Age correspondent:—
If Australians think that the few hundreds of first-class
shots Australia boasts, with their verniers, black pencils,
binoculars, rest forks, blow throughs, orthoptics, Chinese
white and camel hair brushes, are going to be any bul-
wark, then Australians are living in a fool’s paradise. The
club system has produced some hundreds .of shots whose
accuracy under certain fixed conditions-is phenomenal.
What we want is half a million men, not one of whom
could miss a six-feet target at any ordinary range.
The Trade Marks Bill, which the House
of Reps, has on hand, contains provisions by
which trade unions may register marks to distin-
guish goods, the product of the labor of their
members. One of the healthiest and strongest of
influences against “sweating” is an educated
public opinion, and in no better way can the
public be guided to purchase fair-labor goods than
by the use of trade-union labels.
The High Commissioner Bill is now before
the Federal Parliament. Senator Higgs fore-
shadows an amendment providing for the appoint-
ment of the Commissioner by a joint vote of the
two Houses. If that idea is not adopted, the
appointment will probably not be made until the
House rises. Those mentioned most prominently
for the post are B. R. Wise, G. H. Reid, George
Turner, John Forrest, and Langdon Bonython.
Premier Carruthers’ proposal that, instead
of reforming the N.S.W. Lands Department him-
self, it should be handed over for reformation
purposes to Commissioners for a period of two
years, suggests that J. Carruthers has very great
doubts of his own ability as a reformer. Anyhow,
the head of a Reform Ministry has come up
against a question of Reform, and he has decided
tlmt his Reform Ministry isn’t equal to the Reform
job.
The opening ceremony of Banana land’s
Parliament saw the Philp-Leahy Opposition a
frozen-looking crowd of nine. The Ministerial
benches were so packed that there was little
room left for any more recruits. From appear-
ances the Ministry, on its present democratic
platform, will get through the session without
difficulty.
When is Premier Carruthers, of N.S.
Willis, going to reveal these things which he
darkly intimates he has discovered about Lyne in
the matter of the arrangements re the Governor-
General's residence ? Sydney Telegraph observed
gloomily quite a while ago that the papers had
been found mysteriously, and when the revelation
burst it would “bring matters to a head.” When
is it going to burst ?
The Secret Commissions Bill now before
the Australian Parliament is a drastic measure. To
offer to an agent, or accept as an agent, a secret
gift, rebate, or commission, or to render a false
account with a view to allowing - a secret commis-
sion, is made an offence. Penalty £5OO or two
years’ gaol. In civil or criminal proceedings
witnesses may be compelled to answer questions
and produce documents even though tile effect of
such is incriminatory under the Act, and it will
be no defence to allege a “ trade custom.”
The motion of Toutcher, M.L.A., to abol-
ish imported State Governors, was defeated
in Vic. Assembly by . 2fi votes to 21. It
would have been carried but for the defection of
the shifty Peacock and alleged Liberals like
Keast, Hunt, and the turncoat Gaunson. Bent,
Irvine, and M'Cutcheon talked a great deal about
“ disloyalty ” and “silken bonds,” and the like;
but if dispensing with an imported State Gover
nor is disloyalty, it is surely about time the
British navy bombarded Canada and brought that
country to its senses. Canada has been doing
this kind of disloyalty ever since it first federated.
Only a few days after Premier Bent intro-
duced his Secret Commissions Bill in the Victorian
Assembly, Attorney-General Isaacs followed in
the Australian Parliament with his Bill on almost
exactlj r similar lines, save that it only applies to
transactions between parties when both are not
residents of the same State. The blastiferous
doctrine of State Rights prevents the Aus-
tralian 'Parliament doing the whole busi-
ness, yet it is just as capable of legislating
between two Victorians as between a Vic-
torian and a Waler. There is much work
waiting to be done, and there is no time
for this foolish system of doing one job twice
over in fact, before secret commissions are
abolished all over Australia it will be a case of
doing the job seven times over.
A military genius who drags a long dis-
arrangement of the alpha/bet behind his name
looks on tlje renewal arid expansion
of the alliance between Britain
and Japan as the greatest
blessing that can come to
Australia. “ ’Twill save
the Commonwealth,” he
exclaims. “ Britain
may want all her
ships in the Mediter-
ranean, on the Bay of
Biscay or the North
Sea Australian
squadron and
all ; and where
is our Common-
wealth then?
Where is Sydney ?
Where’s Melbourne ?
That’s where the little
Jap savior will come in. Togo
or Kamimura will lie off the coast and pro-
tect Australia till the glorious motherland gets
out of its difficulties.” Ye-e-s ! And isn’t there a
legend that Hengist and Horsa and their Saxons
lay off England and protected it some 1450 years
ago? And when they had done laying off, about
all that was left of the British people were living-
on crows and snowballs in the bleak Welsh hills.
It is so unusual to find Australian Chamber
of Commerce leaders taking a patriotic view of
the colored immigration question that the utter-
ance of President George Wall, at the Sydney
Chamber of Commerce, the other night, is re-
freshing
In considering this problem, we should strip ourselves,
if possible, for a time of our selfish commercial interests,
and weigh evidence before us with a judicial mind. ... It
has been suggested that colored aliens should he per-
mitted m tropical parts of the Commonwealth to do
black man’s work under certain restrictions, and we have
been assured that this would mean increased prosperity
to the country generally, and larger employment for
whites. The idea is to convert what is now an unoccu-
pied wilderness into a prosperous garden—teeming with
population and superabounding in wealth. But is there
not a reverse side to this glorious picture of happy homes
and pastoral contentment? Imagine within our bor-
ders hordes of Chinese, Javanese, Mala.vmen, .Japanese
and all the mongrel races that abound to the north.
Draw on your fancy for one moment and think of the
race mixtures and the race antagonism that would breed
among us. Then as to ourselves—we whites would have
to dominate these aliens or be squeezed out bv them.
Yet even to-day we are not sufficiently numerous to
properly garrison our vast continent. Already we have
heard of these aliens becoming masters, and whites their
servants. Then what of the color line of which we have
heard so much ? Who is to keep back the tide ; or if,
after a while we begin to find that in giving up portion of
our territory we have erred, what power will transport
this heterogeneous mass of humanity back to their own
countries? We should shudder at the very thought of
race admixture with ourselves, yet there are but two
alternatives, absorption or constant race antagonism ; the
latter to increase in bitterness as the aliens became more
numerous and consequently more powerful. There is
another point, and I sweef) away for the moment all
patriotic ideas, and get the subject down to the com-
mercial basis. These aliens are Useless to us. They are
not consumers worthy of the name, and what they do
consume they purchase front their compatriots. Take
the case of the Chinamen in our midst. They accumulate
and remit. They are not good citizens, are not capable
of ruling'them selves, are impossible defenders of the flag,
and are profitless, unprogressive—in a word, undesirable.’

Dr. “Jimmy ” Graham is again before the
N.S. W. public in the role of Parliamentary candi-
date for everywhere and anywhere. He is being
boomed by the daily papers as just the man for
the Ash held vacancy.
Pensions in the upper grades are growing
unpopular. Wadded 'in N.S.W. Parliament
wants to abolish Judges’ retiring allowances, and
Watt in Vic. Parliament wants all recipients of
pensions from the State to resale within the State.
Watts idea would be retmnative justice upon
several haughty loafing absentees who live in
England and abuse the country which keeps them
in splendid pauperism.
It is up to the Australian Government to
take some strong measures about a matter recently
reported by Senator Millen. Coming from India,
Mr. Millen found the P. and O. boat on which he
sailed decorated with a notice to the effect that
passengers should hand over all their dutiable
goods before touching the first Australian- port,
and that to be found with even one cigar or cigar-
ette in their possession would be fegarded in this
oppressed and ill-governed country as evidence of
an attempt at smuggling and be heavily punish-
able. The notice practically represented the Aus-
tralian Government as an institution which cime
on board at the first port of call and stripped
every passenger for inspection. When the English-
man who lies about Australia is in his own country
he is naturally out of our reach ; but it should be
possible in some way to get at the English liar
who pastes his lie on our own coast, so to speak.
Touching the matter of closer settlement
in Victoria, and the policy of “ bursting up” big
estates with the mighty dollar, Thomas Bent
has been remarking
that innumerable
small selections are
now at the mercy of
the Government.
“A lot of people
have got little bits of
land without fulfill-
ing the conditions.”
IntheTolmie district
the selections are
large, and the selec-
tors doing nothing.
They have had the
gall to offer the
Government a resti-
tution of some of the
land, on. condition
that a railway is
built. It isn’t neces-
sary to travel away
from the old railway
tracks to see that the
bogus selector badly
needs to be evicted.
The lines run through
tn i 1 e s of country
which cry aloud for
prompt action by the
Lands Department.
Valuable selections
are allowed to remain
in the hands of men
and women who have forfeited their right
to keep them longer lying idle. Bent talks
largely of the land lie is repurchasing (at fancy
prices) for closer settlement, whilst the books of
the Department point to tens of thousands of
acres that might be resumed by the Government
without any expenditure whatever, simply because
the occupiers haven’t paid for it. Public land in
Victoria—and in Australia generally—seems to be
the only sort of property which a person can safely
acquire by false pretences, secure from the risk of
prosecution, but that is no reason why he shouldn’t
be compelled i&o return it to the lawful owner
when the fraud is proved.

75 members of the House of Representatives have
been Cabinet Ministers, and with the Speaker,
Chairman of Committees, and the Government
Whips added, over a third of the House has held
salaried positions.
About Law :
Justice Hodges, presiding in Melbourne Practice Court,
the other day, uttered a very positive snort against what
he was pleased to call an “unsatisfactory” decision by
the High Court in the case of Marshall v. Colonial Bank.
Having to deal with a similar case, he said he was bound
to be guided by the previous High Court judgment,
although he “had no doubt” it would be upset by the
Privy Council if an appeal were made. Justice Hodges
made further remarks to the effect that the conflict
between Australian High Court law and British Privy
C Kincil law was a painful thing for State Judges,
inasmuch as it sometimes depended on their judgment
whether the right man would have the power of appeal
to the Privy Council or the wrong man would
score by appealing to the High Court. Of course, the
notions as to right and wrong in such points of law as he
was thinking about are purely Hodgesian, and inasmuch
as it is a beloved legal axiom that “nobody knows the
law,” the alleged eccentricity of Australia’s supreme tri-
bunal can be merely eccentric from the point of view of
lawyers ywljo really don’t know. In its strictly legal
aspect the state of uncertainty which moves Hodges to
snort is somewhat deplorable, but his summing up of the
trouble “must appeal to the country’s common sense.
“The public,” he thinks, “ will find that it cannot be
satisfied with these alternative courts of appeal.” Of
that there can be no doubt. The more that High
Court law conflicts with Privy Council law the sooner
will Australia be content to cut off the foreign source of
appeal which is practically confined to the wealthiest
litigants. It may be a very fine source, abounding in im-
pressive sequences of judgments, but where is its right to
reverse High Court decisions if “ nobody knows the law”?
Every Court that can have its decisions reversed is bound
to have them reversed in some case. The Law is a flight
of steps, and on the top step a climbing litigant is sup-
posed to find justice. Australia’s top step at present is a
foreign piece of work—a relic of the pre-National period.
Remove it, and reduce the height and cost of the climb.

’ Legal Notice.
PURSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division of the
High Court of Justice made in the Matter of the
Estate of Edward John Ellerman deceased Hermitage v.
Ellerman 1902 E. 1156 the following enquiry is directed
that is to say (1) an enquiry whether Henry Ellerman one
of the residuary devisees and legatees under the Will of
the above named Edward John Ellerman deceased is living
or dead.
And if he be dead the following further enquiry is
directed that is to say (2) an enquiry when he died and
who are entitled and in what shares and proportions to
the real and personal estate devised and bequeathed to
the said Henry Ellerman by the Will of the said Edward
John Ellerman deceased.
Now the said Henry Ellerman and all persons claiming
under the second above mentioned enquiry are by their
Solicitors on or before the 30th day of November 1905 to
come in and prove their claims at the Chambers of Mr.
Justice Kekewich and Mr. Justice Joyce Royal Courts of
Justice England or in default thereof they will be
peremptorily excluded from the benefit of the said order.
The Bth day of December 1905 at 12.30 of the clock in
the afternoon at Room 689 in the said Chambers is
appointed for hearing and adjudging upon claims.
Dated this 24th day of J une 1905.
W. O. HEWLETT
Master.
NOTE. —The said Henry Ellerman was a nephew of the
Testator Edward John Ellerman and son of Horatio Cock-
burn Ellerman Clerk in Holy Orders who emigrated to
Australia and travelled up country from Melbourne. It is
not known whether the said Henry Ellerman married and
had issue or left a Will.

Rheumatism, Lumbago and Sciatica
Caused by Uric Acid in the Blood. Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills get at the
Cause. They Drive the Uric Acid Out
of the System.
These are three of the most common and painful
diseases in this country, especially prevalent in
the colder districts. These three ailments prac-
tically, arise from the same cause, and the names
only distinguish the location of the pains. If the
arms and legs are affected it is Rheumatism ; if in
the loins, Lumbago; and in the hips, Sciatica.
The attacks are generally brought on by chills
when the body is over-heated, or by exposure to
the damp or cold draughts, which act, not upon
the bones or muscles, but upon the Uric Acid in
the Blood, which is the cause of the diseases. The
pains are affected more or less by cold or heat. A
damp day will double you up, or the warmth of
the bed will intensify the pain. But these diseases
need not be of long duration if they are attended
to in the early stages. The first twinge should
be a warning not to be neglected. Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills get at the cause by purifying
the Blood of Uric Acid and all impurities. They
are a positive and permanent cure for all diseases
arising from a disordered Stomach, Liver and
Kidneys, and are an infallible remedy for Consti-
pation, Biliousness, Sick Headaches, Piles,
Pimples, Boils, Skin Diseases, and Female Ail-
ments. Packed in amber bottles, and sold
throughout Australasia.*
