SPORTING NOTIONS.
The fact that Barney Kieran won the English
quarter-mile “salt-water” in 5.22 1-5, and beat Briton
Billington by 25,vd. in 440 yd., indicates that the Sydney
boy is now in condition. The time of 5.22 1-5 in the open
sea is equal to, if not better than, his world’s record—
-5.19 in an enclosed Sydney bath. From now to the end
of September, when the English season closes, Kieran will
probably shake things up somewhat. On his 440\-ds.
showing the Half-mile (raced last Tuesday) should go to
him also. To-day (Thursday)he will attack the “ hundred ”
at Weston-super-mare. After that he will be free till the
500. yd. is raced at Leeds on August 2Sth ; and the 200 yd.
eventuates on September 26th. Both of these events
should fall to him, and then he’ll come back to Australia,
unless a duchess marries him, or some other equally good
billet offers.
* * * *
Sydney Rugby people did not display the
wisdom of the long animal without legs by continuing
their first-grade competition while a Queensland inter-
state team was being knocked out at Sydney Cricket
• Ground by a Cornstalk team. As it happened, it did not
•make any great difference in the gate, for the club
matches were badly attended —but it did give the im-
pression that the N.S.W. people were not wildly anxious
to help the hard-up Bananalanders any more than they
could help.
* * * *
The inter-State Rugby match was a great disas-
ter to the Bananaland team, which went under by 24
points to 9. The Cornstalk pack was immensely superior,
and the line kicking of the N.S.W. backs was excellent.
It neutralised every temporary advantage gained by the
Queenslanders, and cleared the way for successful
assaults on the Northerners’ goal line. Rig-lib through
the first spell the Queenslanders failed to score, and
although they pulled themselves together, and made
g-reat efforts in the second half, the Cornstalks piled up
the points almost as fast, and made eight while the others
registered nine. As a match it was too one-sided to be
really interesting.
* # » *
Owing, presumably, to the sudden rise in the
temperature, Sydney’s first-grade Rugby on Saturday
afternoon was, all round, a wretched display. With their
blood grown thick, and their guernseys bought specially
for the antarctic breezes of a few weeks back, the players
seemed, to imagine that they were dead and kicking dyna-
mite in Tophet. At the Sydney Cricket Ground, before
the inter-State match, South Sydney ag-ain got Glebe
down, and fell upon it to the tune of 6to 3. It was a
fearsome exhibition, and the more prominent features
were language, roughness, and a disregard for the regula-
tions that made even the land agents present blush.
At, the Agricultural Ground the University crowd
simply rubbed the head of North Sydney on the land.
If it hadn’t been blinded by its own perspiration,
it might have rubbed the whole animal. As it was, it felt
content to win by 14 points to nil. In No. 2 Cricket
Ground, Western Suburbs downed the Easterns by exactly
the same score. In this match damaged noses, torn
mouths and cut legs were in evidence, and created a
cheerful red atmosphere. Balmain, which began to
sparkle lately, resumed a rush-light sort of splendor, and
went down before Newtown by 16 points to nil. The
result of the scramble was to put South Sydney at the
head of the list with 19 points, Glebe falling back to share
second place with Western Suburbs —18 points each.
University and Newtown follow with 17 and 16 points
respectively, and the rest are nowhere.
* • * *
The N.S.W. team to meet Victoria next week
under Australian rules put up some remarkable kicking
at Wentworth Park (Sydney) on Saturday against the
next best 18. The team includes five "Y.M.O.A.
men, one Newtowner, one West Sydney, two North
Shore, two East Sydneys, four Sydneys, and two N,
Paddingtons. Without a doubt, it is a strong team
—it is doubtful if a stronger could have been picked
—although in some quarters there is an "impression
that merit has been passed over in the anxiety to
have all the first grade teams represented.
» * * *
With but four more games to play before the
semi-finals Victorian football excites keener speculation,
the question being whether Essendon or South Melbourne
will be the fourth competitor—Collingwood, Fitzro.v and
Carlton being assured of the other three places.
Saturday’s games were much interfered with by a stiff
wind —the Northerly Busters being far the most consistent
and steadiest team of the three on each ground. South’s
supporters, who flatter themselves they have a hope of the
premiership, went home from St. Kilda with something to
ponder over, the seasiders, who adorn the bottom of the
list, having scored 41 points to South’s 39. Geelong,
which had badly dished ’Roy three weeks before, went
down to Collingwood’s science and combination, scoring-
-36 to ’Wood’s 50 points. The most cheerful argument—
though is wasn’t football—was between ’Roy and
Essendon. They were trying a new umpire, once a
tip-top player, and spectators quickly learned that a master
footballer is by no means necessarily a master umpire. As
soon as the players found he was bounceable, tripping,
slinging, scragging, deliberate kicking out of bounds,
and similar irregularities became the rule, and the game
deteriorated into a rough scramble, enlivened by two or
three incipient “ scraps.” For pretty football the games
played by the Association clubs are better worth Watch-
ing since their committee impressed the necessity of
strictness and severity on their umpires, and passed a
rule prohibiting and penalising kicking in the ruck—a
“ruck” being constituted by four or more players
assembled together. At the ’Roy-Essendon match writer
saw many vicious and seemingly hopeless kicks made at
the ball when it was being bustled about among a closely-
packed mob, and sooner or later someone will get the top
of his head removed or his ear booted off, if that sort of
thing is allowed. West Melbourne and North still dwell
at the top of the tree, and look like settling matters
finally and more or less amicably in about seven weeks.
v * » *
The inter-State Rugby match did not interfere
with the attendance at the various “ Soccer ” fixtures in
Sydney on Saturday. By beating Sydney by 12 to 2 Pyr-
mont maintained its position at the head of the list with
18 points. Rozelle and Glebe each moved up a couple of
points without changing position in any way. Against
Rozelle, Granville had no hope, and was trampled under
foot by 7to nil; while Glebe served South Sydney up on
toast, sto 1. Balmain and Ultimo both had scratch teams
in the field, and Balmain won by 4 to 2.
* * * *
“Crank”: Might happen that those presenta-
tions of purses of sovereigns and money in other forms to
certain members of M.L. football team bound for England
may imperil their amateur status. According to English
and Welsh laws, such things constitute professionalism.
* * * *
The inter-State lacrosse match between N.S.W.
and Queensland took place at Sydney on Saturday. The
gate suffered from the competition of the Rugby teams
representing the same States, which were playing the
same time at Sydney Cricket Ground. Both teams were
very f%st, and the crosse work was said by experts to be
the’ best seen in Sydney up to date. Queensland drew
first blood, and scored a couple before the Cornstalks got
a look in. The Walers by the end of their first quarter
had got their second wind, and were leading by 4 to 3. At
half time the Cornstalks were leading by 6to 4. In the
third quarter the Cornstalks got in some very clever pass-
ing charges, and Queensland began to sink rapidly.
Something like a rot set in, and the end of the quarter
found N.S.W. six ahead—ll to 5. The last quarter saw
Queensland make great efforts to level things up ; but the
Cornstalk defence was firm as a rock, and the Banana-
landers managed to secure only a couple of goals, while
the Cornstalks made a counter attack and secured
another one. When the whistle blew with the final toot
of fate, Queensland had gone under by five goals—N.S.W-
-12, Queensland 7. #
In Adelaide the S.A. lacrossers played all
comers, and plaved to win. Victoria was beaten by 11
goals to 3, and a second team put Westralia through by
sto 2 Then the visiting State teams met, and Victoria
defeated Westralia by 11 to 6. The ’Gropers stayed on a
week, to take it out of the S. Australians, and never had a
hope. Sturt, the premier Adelaide club, hammered them
by 17 to 5, and then the S. Australian team proper fell
upon them on Saturday and crushed them by 22 to 3.
Altogether, it was a great and terrible fall.
* » * *
W. N. Willis’s average for the past three or four
seasons is about 10’7 runs—sheep-runs.
* * * *
If the magnates of cricket were not the hardest-
shelled Tories in any line of sport, the luck of the toss
would be reduced to a minimum in a series of test
matches. In a set of five matches only two tosses are
required—one for chqice of innings in the first game,
and another in the last. For the intermediate games the
pick of the wicket should be alternated between the
teams, so that each might have the advantage not less
than twice. When Maclaren captained a team in Aus-
tralia he proposed this fair-plav arrangement, but the
blessed authorities at this end would have none of it. It
was an innovation, and the dreary wisemen of “ the
game ” are enemies to innovations. Their great grand-
fathers were never struck by the notion of taking turn-
and-turn-about for choice of innings in English countv
cricket when return matches were fixed ; therefore York-
shire, in 1905, is allowed to win two successive tosses
against Lancashire, or vice versa, whilst N.S.W. and Vic-
toria continue to battle on the same unsportsmanlike
terms. The toss is a-purely gambling element in the
game, and it should be eliminated as far as possible. One
toss would suffice for a set of two matches. Two tosses,
or even one, would serve for a- set of three, five or
fifty-five matches. Why permit the possibility of one
side having first choice of innings every time 1 !? The reply
of the heavy fathers of cricket is the ancient Tory excla-
mation—“ Precedent.” Smother Precedent!
* » * *
“ A.8.C.” : Iheend of the Worcestershire
match saw Armstrong displaced from the head of the
averages by Noble, who has been performing consistently
all along, while Trumper moved up to third place. To
show the appalling failure with the bat of the majority of
the team, and how Noble and Armstrong have carried the
crowd on their backs: the aggregate of nine—Hopkins,
Gregory, Cotter, Kelly, M‘Leod, Gehrs, Laver, Newland,
and Howell—at the enc[ of the match totalled 3188 runs,
while Noble and Armstrong between them had scored
3309. Noble, with 1780, still stands a chance of putting
up the record aggregate of an Australian batsman in
England, a distinction at present held by Trumper.
* * * * *
V.R.10. reports that during the past year it gave
away added money to the amount of £35,525—£107 less
than previous year. But larger fields enabled, through
sweepstakes, the handing over to winners of £1167 more
than the previous twelvemonths, a total of £46,618.
Though some of the meetings were losing ventures, the
year ended with a profit of £3005. To swell this modest
sum it has been decided to reduce race-meetings within a
radius of 20 miles of Melbourne to 24 days per annum
from January next.
A GOOD AUSTRALIAN.
“The South Australian Premier (Hon. T. Price), when
he stands up in his place in the Assembly to declare his
policy speech, will be clothed from neck to toe in home-
made apparel. Shirt, underwear, suit, boots, collar, and
tie will all have on them the brand ‘Made in Australia.’”
—Adelaide paper.
The only part of “ Tom ” Price not manufactured
in Australia. Yet he is a better Australian than
many local products.
When York had run away with the Caulfield
G.N. Hurdle Race there was no more doubt as to his
having- been the best of good things for the correspond-
ing event at Flemington. York’s running with Marmont
in the Doutta Galla Hurdles, a month previously, seemed
to give the old champion a good chance, but last Satur-
day’s result disposed of that notion very decisively. The
S’Australian jumper looks like repeating some of the per-
formance of Redleap, who captured the Steeplechase at
Caulfield after winning the Flemington “ double.” He is
said to be equal to the big sticks. There are others —
notably Beilis, and perhaps' Airly. But if York stands
up, the time for the Steeple will probably be a record,
and only the racehorses in the field will have a show in
the last mile of the journey.
York is another smack in the eye for punters
apt to complain that a horse indulged with the lead for
the greater part of a long race has been “ made too much
use of.” The old complaint looks absurd when a good
stayer stays in front all the way. To have the lead in a
jumping race is to be spared the risk of coming to grief
through another horse’s blunder. To tire in the last half-
mile is to fail for vant of stamina; consequently, the
leader that rushes them along from end to end may be
said to win by testing the stamina of the pursuers, which
wouldn’t be fully tested if the best stayer were not made
so much use of until the others are getting tired. Towards
the finish it is only the jockey on the foremost that can
afford to ease his mount a little—for a final effort if
necessary. The pursuers have to be most bustled at the
time when they are most weary.
* * * *
The difference. Last year’s Caulfield Grand
National Hurdles went to Good Day (50 to 1 against), who
did Ikey a great turn. This year it is different, and there
is much lamentation in Israel. Saturday’s winner (York)
started first favorite and is coupled in doubles with every-
thing in the G.N. Steeple.
» * » *
“Old ’Un ” : Easy successes of crack wheat
State lepper York in V.R.C. and V.A.T.C. Grand National
Hurdles has set tongues wagging, and it is not unlikely
that V.R.C. committee will provide opportunity for those
concerned to explain. Just prior to winning the V.R.C.
event, York was beaten in the Williaiustown Hurdles, and
afterwards went under in Doutta Galla (at Flemington)
and Moonee Valley Hurdles. York’s appropriation of
V.R.C. and V.A.T.C. events equals achievements of
Rhesus (1382), The Agent (1885), and Marmont (1903). As
a yearling-, York was bought for 30 guineas—his breeding
is good enough for anything.
The Y.R.C.. in a praiseworthy attempt to cut
down the number of race meetings during the year, has
roused the suburban proprietary clubs, which are
threatened with serious financial loss, to concerted
action in defence. These bodies make a good
point by showing that, while they are allotted
one day per week among the lot, the unregis-
tered or “illegitimate” race clubs hold meetings
on 156 days, or on every other working day throughout
the year. The suburban proprietary clubs urge that a
curtailment of their privileges will only increase the
profits of the more undesirable unregistered people, who
will grab the vacant dates for themselves. But granting
all this, the remedy would seem to lie in an effort, Parlia-
mentary if necessary, to grapple with the illegitimate
racecourses. There are too many race meetings, both in
Sydney and Melbourne, and too much horse, and too
much turf and undersized jockey, and far too much
bookie.
* * * «
Horse-racing, above all things, begins to catch
the national spirit, and it is seriously proposed that there
shall be an Australian Derby and St. Leger, to be run in
each State in turn. Chairman S. Miller, of V.R.C., says
his committee discussed the matter with the A.J.C.
executive, and that the latter expressed approval. The
idea is that the Australian Derby and St. Leger should
supersede the State races of that name, the added money
to be £2OOO and £lOOO respectively.
» * '* *
Ikey Mo. gets considerable of what is going
both ways in connection with the horse-racing business.
Oxenham topped Australian money-winning list for year
just closed with £5382, and Sol Green’s £4319 followed
closely. J. Wren gathered £2BBO, and E. Kelly £1298.
Truly the bejewelled are the favored of fortune, and yet
it isn’t enough for them. They want the earth and the
fulness thereof.
* * * *
Veteran Queensland trainer J. Butler died at
Ipswich recently, aged 77. Butler was employed by late
Joshua Bell in the halcyon days of the Grange'stud, when
the old North Australian (Ipswich) Jockey Club’s course
existed on the Grange estate. It w'as there that Warhawk,
Whisker, Olivia, Wheatear, Waterloo, Sir Oliver, Lothair,
Le Grand, Elastic, Othello, Cadmus, Canary, Lilia, Lord
Clifden, and others were prepared.
Ikey Noses, now on strike in Sydney, has
become arrogant and nasty, and declares that he will
wait on the clubs no more. Meanwhile Canterbury and
Moorefield races have taken place w-ithout him," and
although he was numerically less, the blackleg—ahem !
“free bookmaker’’—supplied all the capital that was
required. One book (an ex-comp of a Sydney sporting
weekly) went to Canterbury “to give £2OO a chance,”
and won £3OO on one race, and £BOO on the day. Others
reported similar results—what an awful time the backers
must have had !—and the result is that the rush to fill the
places of the Noses on strike is like the rush to a free
banquet. Probably when the Noses declare the strike off
they will find that thev have been struck off.
* * * *
Some anti-gambler alleges that popular betting
on races only dates from about 1849. Before that it was
almost exclusively the bloated aristocracy which wasted
its substance on the precarious animal.
* * * *
“Snaffle ” : As little as 25 to 1 was taken “the
two Yorks” for V.A.T.C.’s G.N. Hurdles and ’Chase at
Caulfield last and next Saturdays, and York (with one leg
in) looks like having a big chance of landing the double,
especially as he has incurred no penalty and is no tyro
over “ country,” having tackled the big leps three or four
times. Published somewhere that Marmont (with 12.13)
can have no chance in Saturday’s Steeple. Bunkum !
Redleap, Daimio, Domino, and Colonel Shilinski had
weightier loads when they won.
* * * •
An English motor-cyclist recently ran a short
distance at the rate of 86 miles an hour. The man that
would do it would ride Satan himself if His Knibbs could
only be held and saddled.
* * * *
Sydney’s latest craze is to learn to drive a motor,
even if there is no chance of being able to buy one, and
a School of Motoring meets the demand by training
men to drive, repair, and clean the machines. Also,
when it has taught them, it will hire them cars, or get
them billets as motor-men (chaffeur is declared obsolete
by the English Automobile Club, whose word is law on
such matters). Last week writer saw a crowd of cars
careering out to Coogee in great style, and learned after-
wards that it was the School taking its pupils out for a
run, after which it would take part in the hill-climbing
contest. To induce a motor-car to climb a hill the
machine has to be coaxed and wheedled just as a horse
has, and a man who knows how to talk properly to a
machine can always get more out of it than one who
doesn’t. The hill-climbing was interesting, £15,000
worth of cars competing. It was a Swiss car which
carried off the honors, having slithered up a quarter of a
mile of Maroubra Hill—grade of from 1 in 6 to 1 in 12-
in Imin. 40sec.
* * * *
For weary years—from 1899, in fact, till the
present season—East Brisbane Harriers Club has mopped
up the Queensland cross-country championship. This
time the Thompson Estate burst in and grabbed the title.
Owing to having violated his amateur status, C. Saunder-
son, individual champion for six years, could not start
this season, and the honor was taken by T. Singer.
How the retired bikists run to pubs. Plugger
Bill Martin has served out liquor to thirsty Victorians
Relph, ex-champion of England, and Carpenter and
Parsons (Vic. wheelists) are also in the pub. line in Mel-
bourne. Now Pie-biter MacFarland threatens to throw
up the jigger and take to the pewter, also in Victoria
•**■*■*
The cross-country five-mile championship of
Bent’s State still remains with the East Melbourne Club
and G. Blake, of the same crowd, retains the individual
title. Runners up were Ballarat and East Melbourne.
* * * *
In the tediously-prolonged wrestling bout be-
tween Weber and Gunga Brabm, at Melbourne Athenasum,
the other night, the greasiness of the Hindu became a
great protection to him as time went on. His skin exuded
oil from every pore, and the trouble with Weber was to
hold the black man tightly enough to exert the force
necessary for levering him over upon his back. Gunga
didn’t slip from Weber’s grasp. It was the grasp that in-
variably slipped from Gunga what time he kept himself
huddled together on the mat. He avoided a fall for 100
minutes, but he never made a show of aggression. He
lay low and perspired for safety.
**’ * *
Weber, judging from the time he took in
scoring a pin-hold against Gunga, is at present no match
for Scott, the Maoriland champion. Scott is a mighty
big fellow and a versatile performer, and he did as he
pleased with the heavy-footed Harry Groth. The two
winners are to wrestle anon for the right of challenging
Buttan Singh, who may possibly retain his “ champion-
ship ” title by greasily dodging a pin-fall, but doesn’t look
very likely to overpower either of the big white men.
* * * *
Pigeon slayers are a bloodthirsty lot. N.S.W.
Gun Club wanted to give a “send off ” to one of its vice-
presidents and decided on a complimentary “shoot.” A
trophy was subscribed for ; the members of the club shot
off for the “honor” of presenting it, and before this high
distinction could be properly allotted 220 pigeons were
slaughtered. The winner of the competition thereupon
asked the president of the club to do the presentation,
and presumably that person rose, covered with blood and
feathers, and did it.
* * * *
“ Vaevictis ” is indeed the motto of the sports-
man. Stanbury, ever since that eventful Saturday after-
noon, has been a demi-god—and the English language
doesn’t hold oblivion enough to throw on defeated Towns.
And yet, Australian scullers owe Towns a debt of grati-
tude. Stanbury went abroad years ago and threw away,
or otherwise lost, the championship. And it was Towns
who went abroad to bring it back to the Parramatta, and
wasted much valuable time and hard cash to get it. He
had to hunt old Jake Gaudaur to his lair at Rat Portage,
in Canada, and consent to outrageous conditions, before
the Canuck would agree to row.
Stated by a Sunday paper that a man-o’-wars-
man, at present on the Australian station, will receive his
discharge in a few weeks and immediately challenge Jim
Stanbury to defend his title as champion sculler of the
world. This hunk of information was accompanied by a
vivid description of the hardness of the man in blue, and
other interesting particulars, but there was no mention
of his name—and even a beastly, common blue-jacket
has a name.
* * * *
A fight occurred the other night in a popular
bar in Swanston-street, Melbourne, which casts some
light on a recent discussion re the comparative punishing
powers of professional and amateur scrappers. A large
Victorian landlord, 6ft. 4in. in his skin, and stout enough
to carry away several farms, found himself drinking at
the same bar wdth a coon pugilist of no great size. The
man of acres began to make fierce remarks. The coon
looked hard in another direction, and tried to live it
down. The remarks grew 7 more personal and more
vigorous. The all-white w 7 as warned Hint, he was toying
with a dangerous animal, but he insisted that .he could
destroy two like the coon pugilist between meals, and he
followed this up with so choice an insult that the colored
“ genleman ” saw red. The fight lasted about 30 seconds,
and then the bulky amateur was carried into a cab. A
week later his face looked as if he had been stopping
tramcars with it.
* * * *
The Hanley-Raff scrap, at Sydney Gaiety, on
Saturday night, w 7 as a gruesome business. Like Bright’s
disease, Hanley went for the kidneys every time. While
he paid attention to that part of ’ Raff’s anatoniy where
the electric-belt is generally worn to infuse a warm invigo-
rating current, &e., Raff hit him a beauty on the eye.
Hanley left the kidneys alone and coiled up on the floor,
but managed to get up again inside time and stagger
round like a lame microbe. Raff tried desperately to
finish the object noth his fists, but Hanley managed to
weather it. Later on, he resumed his attentions to Raff’s
kidneys, and this writer, who used to shoot the kea birds
that eat the kidneys of the living sheep in Canterbury
(M.L.), felt that he must go out and buy a gun. Raff
smote and swiped, but Hanley went on tap, tapping at
his kidneys, till Raff began to sag. over and wear an ex-
pression of agony. Then Henley smote him suddenly on
the jaw, and Raff w 7 ent to his chair, where he
instructed his myrmidons ta wave the towel of defeat.
This kidney belting is quite legal, but it is utterly
repulsive.
* * * *
There was a flavor of ye olden tymqie about the
bare-knuckle fight that happened at Middle Harbor last
week. Bruisers from Waterloo district fought for £25
a-side, without referee, timekeeper, ring, or rules of any
kind. Fifteen rounds passed without any serious punish-
ment happening to either party—and then there was a
rush of police and a scatter of the crowd. The National
Sporting Club offered the warriors the “ gate ” to finish
the interrupted tenor of their scrap in its hall on Thurs-
day, under the M.Q. rules. The matter ended by one
man being knocked out in the third round, after being
very second best from the start.

(employed in Willis’ office), and Bernard Hoskins,
on a charge of conspiracy. M‘Nair and Hoskins
were promptly arrested. Willis will probably be
met with in S’Africa.

State Premier Carruthers asks Australian
Premier Deakin to go to law with him in the High
Court on 14 separate points in connection with the
fixing of the capital. The legal profession thinks
the idea a good one, but considers that it would be
still better to have 14 separate actions, and is of
opinion that each Government should engage a
strong Bar, so as to have the best legal assistance
in its time of trouble. Nothing makes the Bar so
sorry as to see two wealthy litigants struggling
along without the best legal assistance.

A bright Australian girl, in the desperate
endeavor to keep young, appeared for the fourth
time as a debutante at a recent hop. And if
nothing matrimonial happens she is quite capable
of becoming a young thing just from the nursery
several more times.
The position of the N.S.W. “Reform
Government in regard to the Lands scandals can be
put in a sentence : Whereas public duty demands
a keen prosecution of the inquiry, the Govern-
ment, in the words of its own press supporters,
has to be flogged to every step. Sydney Tele-
GB.APH suggests, as a result of the latest disclosures,
that Premier Carruthers should leave the Cabinet
for a while until the scandals have blown over.
But of what advantage would that be? If the
reason is that Mr. Carruthers’ open presence m
the Cabinet is undesirable, would not his influence
as a sort of temporarily-suspended Premier be just
as bad ? The Telegbaph’s suggestion is shock-
ingly foolish. Fancy the Constitutional position
of having the Premier under suspension ?
A coincidence occurred at Melb. Supreme
Court when Edwards, convicted of manslaughter-
ing Skinner at Waranga, was sentenced by Justice
Hood to 12 years’ hard labor. Many years ago a
’possum-trapper was killed near Strathmerton, and
his body was cut up similarly to Skinner’s. That
slayer was also found guilty and sentenced to 12
years. When the hunt was on for Edwards, the
Strathmerton homicide’s photo, was identified by
several as being a picture of Edwards, so much
alike were the two. And after Edwards had been
sentenced, almost the next business of the court
was to give the old duplicate man-slayer, who is
still wandering in evil courses, two years for
cattle-stealing.
A sorry tale was told at Sydney Quarter
Sessions, last week, when two men, both first
offenders, were charged with assault and robbery.
One of the pair, a seafaring man, stated that at
the time of his offence he was starving, and that
he saw the prosecutor counting his money and
wrenched it from him. He got £4 and divided it
with his mate, and then both went looking for
work. As a side issue, the robber had carried a -
portmanteau for the robbed, and the latter had
refused to give him a trifle for the labor. The
accused had at different times saved 27 lives, a,nd
held the English, N.S. Wales and Victorian
Humane Society’s medals for saving life and the
Lloyd medal for conspicuous bravery at sea.
Judge Docker could not see his way to bring the
case under the First Offenders Act and gave the
medalist 12 months.
A carpenter with the Young Ladies’
Journal name of Dorsamey Noel has had a
memorable “scrap” at law with uncle Cohen, to
whom he is indebted to the extent of £l3. The
carpenter having shown no anxiety to pay up,
uncle decided to appeal to the law, and with a
view to doing the thing cheaply voluntarily wiped
£3 off the debt, reducing it to £lO. This made it
possible .to sue the carpenter economically in
the Small Debts Court, where Cohen got a
verdict. But Dorsamey Noel appealed, and hired
an expensive barrister to prove to the Full Court
that he Owed uncle £3 more than uncle said he
did. He proved it so successfully that it was de-
cided that the Small Debts Court had no jurisdic-
tion, and uncle’s verdict was quashed. Also,
uncle had to pay costs as a reward for knock-
ing off £3, which is the sort of thing he will never
do again.
N.S.W. Lands Commission heard from a.
modest man in broad Scotch, the other day. He>
hadn’t been long in politics as Labor member lor
ex-Spealcer Abbott’s Lower Darling seat, when a.
flock of constituents came along with some land
business. He fixed them up all right,, and they
went away happy and without being asked to
leave behind even the price of a drink. The news
that a successful land agent had opened up busi-
ness on these terms flew over the Kiverina and
the Darling country like a duststorm, and every
train landed a new drove of applicants at bcobie s
door. Finally, when the representative of a rich
squatting co. came along, and sent him fossicking
in. out-of-the-way corners for advice, he screwed
uo his courage and asked for £5 to help pay his
boot-bill. When the business was through, the
squatting co. handed him a parcel. I said,
* There’s more than £5 here,’ ” the innocent new
chum told the Commission. But the squatter
said he wouldn’t have an easy conscience if he paid
less ; and later on, conscience being still unsatis-
fied, another £25 dropped in upon the new chum.
Just at that stage the man in broad Scotch began
to smell things, and when the next person with
land-hunger happened along he was passed on co
a lawyer. Lawyers are used to smells.

has approved of a measure which gives the London
County Council power to register and inspect all
labor employment agencies. The reform might
well be imitated in Australia.

Iii an afternoon tea-room on a recent day I sat
opposite a girl who blushed—blushed often and
vividly, like Sophia Western in Fielding's book
H hen so high an authority as John Oliver Hobbes
put lorn Jones” in the list of books that a
debutante should read I rose like one woman and
got the Great Work. Well, Sophia had cause to
blush ! But she didn’t blush at the Blue Causes,
tier brilliant color ” came at the mention of a
gentleman s name, or a chance remark of hor
maid, or at sight of a certain handwriting on an
envelope, or ataman’s glance. “Until a more
Beautiful color than vermilion is invented we will
say nothing of Sophia’s color on this occasion,”
said the novelist, that being the period when a
writer stood up alongside his book and personally
addressed ins characters, or pointed them out
to the public. Shade of all flamingoes! I
forget the “occasion,” but it was not a psycho-
logical one. She blushed because every virtuous
genteel girl of that time had infinite capacity for
changing color.” If she hadn’t, she was a minx
or a treak. 1 o return to the girl in the tea-room :
1 fe was about seventeen, her hair was done at
half-mast, and she wore the usual ninepenny
string of pearls round her pretty neck. An apple-
green blouse accentuated her fairness and helped
to show up the blushes. The red signals became
her wonderfully and I wondered if the stolid-
looking youth whose mumbled remarks brought
them out could appreciate such flattery.
A crowd of outdoor-sports girls trooped into the
room presently Those who were not talking or
laughing wore their mouths open or snapped-to.
Ti? .you the -glazed-looking lady in
hittle Dorrit, who advised the young ladies to
say i apa, potatoes, poultry, prunes, and prism ”
before entering a room, because that would give a
nice shape to the mouth ? I wonder what the
sportsg’r s would murmur. I would suggest :
Holf, bloque, afternoon-tea, blouse; rats.”
I he Adelaide damsel is much addicted to that
form of amusement known as the committee
dance. A dozen or twenty angels join forces, hire
a nail, and share two or three hundred invitations
among them. They give their dance a name,
generally that of a flower, and, when possible, the
decoration scheme accords with the name. Many
of these dances become annual affairs, the com-
mittees changing, of course, as time goes on. A
tew of them are the Butterfly, Waratah, Prim-
al: Cooeyana, Karaka, Geranium, Murillo, Bose,
Uaffodih Buttercup, Forget-me-not, and Taran-
tella. Occasionally the men rally up and give a
return dance to some of the damsels who have
entertained them but usually that scarce animal
man, is so overwhelmed with invitations that he
nas no time to invite anybody.

The newest proposal by which it is thought
Australia might earn a few coppers is through ex-
porting iron ore to Japan. Nippon hasn t
developed any ore of its own to speak of, and its
coal is of poor quality on the whole ; yet, under a
heavy tariff, and with other State help, it has
bu/c up a large iron industry, and makes its own
fighting weapons and its own warships. China
has been supplying most of the iron dirt out of
which the Monkey has built himself up so far ,
now it is suggested that Australia should supply
the dirt for the Monkey to work up into things to
subsequently hit us with. The idea of Australia
using its dirt to make things to protect itselt with
hasn’t, however, got much forrarder.
A Victorian correspondent : —
After two years of Canadian railway-man Tait in Vic-
toria the service is one seething mass of bosses. They are
like ants when you boil the ant-hill. Everybody is a boss.
As regards the Bolling Stock Branch (formerly more
appropriately named the Loco. Branch), dozens of officers
are falling over each other’s heaps of figures in the “ big
offices ” at Spencer-street in a wild endeavor to arrive at
the average amount of coal and oil used pel train mile.
Men who have never driven a locomotive, or anything
else but a pen or an oppressed office boy, fix an amount
of fuel, etc., to he used, and, if it be exceeded, reams of
official paper begin to blow around like leaves in Vallarn-
brosa. The oil consumption has been cut down so low
that it is said hundreds of pounds worth of machi-
nery, particularly valves and pistons, are being cut to
pieces before their time. Later on, when the official mind
grasps these facts, the pendulum will doubtless swing the
other way, and the saving will be shown in engine
renewals * and repairs, while oil will slosh round
like a cataract. But just now the fad is oil saving, lait
shows no desire for economy in his personal arrange-
ments. When he is on tour it is like a circus. Officials
pour along a few days ahead of the Commissioner s
special—" advance agents ” they jokingly call themselves
amongst their equals in the service. To inferiors they
assume a mighty haughty and frigid air, in fact the frigid
air pervades the service. It is hardly possible to speak to
a superior officer over the ’phone now without having to
thaw your nose at the gas-stove afterwards, so cold are
his remarks. The special is a gorgeous affair, run through
without a block or stop, while the trains with the com-
mon people who pay their fares drag along a day or so
behind. To a recent deputation of employees who waited
on him about anomalies which exist in the service, Tait
said : “ Yaas, I’ll admit there are some anomalies, and we
propose to go into them as soon as we get time, and 1
think we’ll get time vurra soon now. But, let me tell
you this, that dealing with anomalies will not mean a
rise in pay. There’ll be no increase in the average rate
of the pay roll. Let me tell 3 r ou that." And the men find
at every turn that therein lies the secret of Tait s
surplus : “ There’ll be no average increase in the pay
roll.”

Can You Look at It Five Minutes

If you wish to ascertain how susceptible you are to hypnotic influence, place yourself in an easy
sitting position and look steadily and intently at the black spot (pupil) in the centre of the eye above
for five minutes. While looking at the eye count very slowly to yourself, one, two, three, four, and so
on, to five hundred. Do not permit your eyes or mind to wander for one moment. At the end of five
minutes, if your eyelids feel heavy or tired, if you feel slightly drowsy, if you have a slight tingling
sensation in the arms or hands, if you have a peculiar sensation in the head, or if you feel during the
time an inclination to wink the eyelids, you are quite susceptible to hypnotic influence, and you should
at once learn the science for your self-protection.
i. The eye of a person who understands hypnotism is a thousand times more powerful than the cold,
lifeless picture given above. To tell bow susceptible you would be to a real hypnotic eye, multiply
the effect you perceived by 1000.

Coppabella shares—“copper-bellies,” as they
are known in the Stock Exchange ring—have furnished
nice profits to a hungering crowd of spec, sellers and
their jackals. Market bump, which shot shares in Mel-
bourne up to £24, started the game. It needed resolution
to face the bulls, as Adelaide had got into the swing of
the gamble. Still, one or two lion-hearted souls who
punch anything waded in hard. For two or three davs it
was a toss-up whc would win, but on Thursday and
kiiday the current of selling set with the 'bears,
-the gamble represents the usual fate of the public when
it rushes in to get rich on an unproved mine. A Queen-
street crowd that took up a big - block of shares because
it believed the show was worth prospecting did strenuous
bulling, but was overcome by weight of metal brought
to bear by its antagonists. It may not all be lost
ground, for some of the wise men" of Gotham mav
learn that mines, like ladies, are ticklish things to
handle. A bull market also needs a lot of propping
unless the scrip is locked up to prevent leakage
or unless indiscriminate profit-taking can be prevented!
there was plenty-of room for a big bear nip, but the
Hackenschnudt of the crowd was nervous. As for the

Wheal Dorey, Daylesford (Vic.), is one of the
shows , that wants cash, so Bull is to be applied to for it
probably because he has struck a div. or two in the
Victorian Cornish, floated by the Fields on the strength
of the mine s has-been record. Wheal Dorey’s chairman
softests that he should try to get Bull to plank down

ups it won’t matter so much ; but he would be
regarded as a bold man who ventured to ask Victorians
fori 'lO paid-ups in any co. to work the Wheal Dorey.

* * * *
Black Horse, Egerton (Vic.), has a splendid
history, a grand overdraft, and a splendid lot of call-pay-
mg shareholders. But- a few up-to-date ideas instilled
into the heads of the directors wouldn’t be out of place
hirst, it should get its surveys checked bv a geologist’-
next it should consider the propriety of testing its ground
by means of the diamond drill; and it should have its
Pi7u . ov erhauled by a first-class mechanical engineer
What is bothering co. is to find the Sister Rose lode lost
in the Egerton mine through heavy faulting. This is
where the geological surveyor should come in, and if his
work was backed by diamond-drill boring, a lot of costlv
driving and crosscutting might be saved.
* * * *
Morse’s Creek dredge, Bright (Vic.), has sur-
prised the locals by the productiveness of its operations
Last six months it put through 130,000 cub. yards for
782J0z., or roughly 2fgr. per cub. yard. Yet on this
average profits worked out at the rate of 54 per cent, per
annum-. Working expenses were £52 per week. Does
this include renewals and writing off for the heavy wear
and tear to which all dredges are subject ? Irrespective of
that, the record is a capital one.
* * * *
Rathscar, Maryborough (Vic.), is a little show
that is having a good deal of quiet puffing. Shoots of
stone make very small at first, then widen, and then pinch
out, leaving only a track. Driving on this, so far, has led
to other shoots being picked up. Like majority of Vic-
tonan little mines, a small trial lot of half-ounce quartz
and about 200 ft. of driving justifies the buying of a bat-
tery. Looked at critically, the property hasn’t shed its
milk teeth. It wants deeper sinking and more driving
before crushing can proceed on a decent scale.
* * * *
Great Boulder, Kalgoorlie, continues to look
well in south drive at 1900 ft. For 150 ft. lode has varied
from 10ft. to 12ft. wide, worth from 20dwt. to 47dwt.
This is one of the best developments in mine belowl2 level’
Hamilton shaft (north end) is now fully equipped but so
far work at 800 ft. and 1000 ft. has not disclosed any value
Co. has spent an enormous amount on this shaft, which
will . chiefly benefit Boulder No. 1 and West Boulder.
Sinking Hamilton shaft has been renewed to search for
the lode pierced by drill nearly two years ago, and which
assayed 25dwt. for 10ft. wide. This was at a vertical
depth of 1335 ft. Edwards shaft (south end) is also being
sunk below 1200 ft., at which depth it has been hung up
for years. Co. has strong ore reserves ; also 400,000 tons
of old tailings, valued at 3dwt.’ Its, treatment costs
pan out at 145., which is higher than those of some of its
neighbors’.
* * * *
North White Feather, Kanowna (W.A.), is in a
much improved position. At 500 ft. reef has already been
driven on about 280 ft. and averages 21dwt. for a width of
Sift. At 400 ft. shoot was 400 ft. long, 4ft. wide, and worth
25dwt., While at 300 ft. it was 250 ft. long, and worth only
12dwt. for lOin. wide. Nearer the surface it was very
poor. Above 300 ft. mine is worked out, but at 400 ft a
good block of ore. still remains, while at 500 ft. no stoping
.has been done. Co. has recently been making
■profits of between £l5OO and £2OOO monthly, and
.after paying off liabilities, is £6OOO to the good. Recent
I'eport said that mining and milling costs had been
reduced to 19s. 7d. per ton, but manager.’s figures pan out
.-at about 405., and this gives profits stated above. Co. is
in 400,000 shares, and is working same reef as W. F. Main
jßeefs; and, under circumstances, scrip cannot be
recommended, even on improved position.

* * * *
Golden Pole (W.A.) continues erratic. Mine
has always been a puzzle, as reef twists about in a most
peculiar way, and all others anywhere in vicinity have cut
out, usually at or about 400 ft. What has increased sus-
picion is that work at 400 ft. was suspended for months on
one plea or another. It is a cute crowd that controls the
concern. 00. is at present paying monthly divs. of 9d.
and, after September, when tailings treatment starts, Is!
is predicted. But at no time has it had much more than
12 months’ reserves in sight. Only thing in favor of co.
is that mine has always been open to inspection, but, till
lately, no inspection at 400 ft. was possible, and manager
says he has still 50ft. to go to reach reef. °

All manager Roberts’ predictions regard in «■ con-
tinuity of Oroya shoot in Associated ground have been
fulfilled, and length now proved is 950 ft. Shoot is as it
wa f “ other mines, a pipe ranging up to 70ft. in width
and 50ft. in depth. Values are given as Ito 3oz but
rich telluride veins run through it. Lode is trending'
south-west, and, opposite Tetley’s shaft, is about 250 ft
east. If it continues this trend it will make through
Australia lease and give Associated a very long stretch of
it. Possibilities of mine are very great.
Oroya-Brown Hill is drawing bulk of its ore from
Oroya South block, and sweetening- results from Oroya
North block. Old Brown Hill is practically depleted
which show's how cute Bewick, Moreing and Co. were
in bringing about amalgamation. At end of last year ore
reserves in Oroya shoot W'ere estimated at 78,000 tons
worth 40z., and of this a very considerable portion must
have since been treated. Great efforts are being made to
force output since H. A. Shipman took charge but
Shipman is booked for ’Murka in October. Since Asso-
ciated cut continuation of Oroya shoot, both Shipman and
Loring have come to conclusion that Oroya-Brown Hill
has been working- too far west at south end of property
and so got into a blank. Efforts are now being- made to*
Jocate shoot to the east, and, if located, life of mine as a
high-grade proposition will be prolonged.

While the Water Bill was before the Victorian
Assembly, Minister Swinburne threw some figures at the
House that ought, but for the hide of the average land-
owner, to settle the question out of hand. He°quoted
statistics of five trusts which have become so hopelessly
insolvent that three have been taken over by the State
and the others are in process of going—Loddon United!
Carrum, Marquis Hill, Cohuna and Macorna. Altogether
tnese cormorants had £231,750 advanced to them, and
£193,140 of principal and accumulated interest has since
been written off as hopeless. Notwithstanding this ease-
ment of their alleged burden they have got so far in
arrears again as to owe £16,813 for outstanding interest
and redemption. But some of them made a valiant effort
P° - honest. Cohuna paid £512 towards its total
indebtedness of £151,213. Carrum paid £5O and Marquis
Mill a whole £2. It must have caused a tremendous
wrench to put aside that £2 ! Marquis Hill Trust owed
£14,477, was forgiven £9076, and then let its interest bill
get m arrears £s933—but it once put aside a whole 40s
towards being honest. It is a wonderful record, that of
these anti-Socialistic farmers, but more than direct State
control is wanted. Cells and a long, steady course of
bread and water are also necessary.
* * * *
During the discussion, by Vic. Parliament, of
the supplementary estimates, Mr. D. O. M’Grath, the re-
presentative of Grenville, made a few' remarks that are
well worth noticing. M'Grath is, in spite of his name, a
silent member, and his speech only occupied four
minutes . In Ballarat East two leases held by Pearce
Brothers were taken up in 1890 for 15 years, and would
have expired this year... The North Woah Hawp mine
had paid Pearce and some other gentleman £19,000 in
royalty. The New Normanby had paid them £14,000.
When he made inquiries at the Mines Department he
found that those gentlemen had been clever enough to
surrender their leases two years ago, and got them
renewed. He (M'Grath) wished to know if anythin"-
couid be done, or whether this state of things was to be
suffered for another 13 years. He might also mention*
that the South Star had paid the Guiding Star £25,000
.royalty, and the Star of the East had paid them £50,000
royalty... In Ballarat East they had paid £200,000 in
royalty, not to landowners, but simply to leaseholders
who were purely shepherding.” A strong Minister of
Mines is needed to put in a full day in his shirt sleeves
cancelling misused leases.
Premier Bent proposed to do a lot of good work
with his surplus. Among other things, £147,000 is to go
towards replacing trust funds borrowed in previous years *
£lll,OOO to pay for old railway rolling stock and railway
stores bought out of loans, and worn out in bygone years
without the debt on them being paid off ; £22,000 for new
railway works ; £3OOO for a hospital for epileptics ; £4O 000
towards the much-needed new lunatic asylums and’in-
ebnates’ home ; £20,000 to replace money cadged from
the licensing fund by a previous Treasurer who wanted to
make a surplus ; £3OOO for hospitals ; £25,000 for the re-
pair of the dreadfully neglected State schools; £27,000
for land purchases and land reclamation ; and a multitude
of small sums for bridges, dredges, &c. If Bent can raise
ano *'., er , sur Pj us bke this one, and — having wiped off this
multitude of small arrears—start railway construction
again, but this time out of revenue, it will be a splendid
continuation of the good work he has commenced.

, *. n needless explanation:—“Fairplay” talks)
nrough his hat when he confers the title of “ musician ” |
on somebody who can’t play, sing, conduct, or compose— I
on some stale pedant who can but teach counterpoint,
ugue, and harmony (out of text-books), and give lec-
tmetteson Incidents in the Lives of Sober Musicians.”
t n a P er * on knows the “ construction of music ”so
profoundly that he can’t write it, or play it, or utilise it by
putting it into vulgar sound and common practice, he
niay be a Stand proper person to teach others not to do
likewise— i.e., though he is not a musician he may be an
excellent University Professor. And Petersen is simply
tne typical Prof.—an uninspired exponent of the plati-
.Under the Marshall Hall regime the music section
01 j * shop ’ was full of life and vigor and enthusiasm,
and students wanted to Do something, to hear and play
and talk and make music all day long. Now the Coii-
servatorium, wherein music is regarded as a shining
a istraction and a matter of the manual, has become as
auu and antediluvian as the geological museum. Peter-
sen doesn t “ roar ” or “bound”—he crushes you with a
slab of counterpoint, or tells you Mozart produced his
Dos “ C u S II 1 reen socks. Petersen makes music a dead
art .Marshall Hall makes it alive and kicking. One is a
Professor, the other a musician—that’s the main differ-
ence between them.
„ a , u Ya " a ! ) * : An air of mystery apparently envelops
the Modern Milo ” —if nothing eise does. Some music-
nan patrons imagine that she is a continental beauty who
goes about in draughty clothes to earn her salary ; others
tlunk she hails from the Land of Trusts ; and, again,
there are others who are under the impression that the
ever-so-lightly-clad lady is English. All are off the right
track. The living statue is an Australian girl, Pansv
Montague, and was one of George Musgrove’s show girls
during his last and not-over-sucoessful comic opera season
here. Speaking from memory, her first appearance dates
Dack eight years ago—perhaps longer—when she and two
sisters graced the footlights at the old Alexandra in
Melbourne, the famous Dan Barry being then lessee.
“0.K.”: Thoughtfully considered, the strength of the
situations in ‘ The Walls of Jericho ” appears to be all on
the surface, as in “ Magda ” and other plays'whfere'the
principal speaker is at war with the surrounding order of
things. You don’t get cause and effect as in “Mrs.
ianqueray.’ You get dramatic effect achieved bv arti-
hcial means. Accepting the two men from Queensland
as possible arnvals in the London “ smart set,” one might
call the situations realistic. But the men, not being
snobs, are impossible arrivals. The hero, Jack Frobisher,
could never have attached himself to a clique of utterly
uncongenial acquaintances in the first instance. He isn’t
built that way. As for his quiet-spoken friend, Hankey
Bannister, he comes into the “ set ” on the understanding-
that he is a guileless, middle-aged roamer with £40,000 a
year. Both men are haters of shams, and both of them
hawe deliberately chosen to hobnob with supercilious
johnnies and Gwendolines as though they had never
heard of any part of London outside of Mayfair where rich
men can go and get married. A pair of brainy, honest,
enterprising, matured bachelors suddenly transformed
into the silliest of parvenus—“ on their own’’—not
dragged into Society by their wives and daughters. Such
men only exist in fiction.-
Miss Unie Russell, playing juvenile lead with the
Knight-Jeffries co., talks of leaving the stage in a month
or two, when her engagement with Williamson terminates.
JLne work and the continual travelling- are too much for
her._ Tis a pity, for Unie shaped remarkably well for a
beginner, and her Stephanus, in “ The Sign of the Gross,”
was something of a little masterpiece. But, after all,
slight, fair girls of her type can never hope to star with
■my great success. The heroine of most plays stands sft.
bin. at least, and is usually a haughty damsel of com-
manding mien, at least once in every performance.
When a very small girl repudiates a very tall villain, the
gallery is apt to laugh.
1 lora Graupner is sometimes seen abroad in Melbourne
these times, pretty much her old self physically,
i- Nance H’Heil never before cut so fine a figure as
she did in the last act of “Marie Antoinette” at Her
Majesty s, Melbourne, the other week. As the fallen
queen, white-haired, pale-faced, dressed in a simple
cream frock, with a band of the same color across her
whited locks, she looked by far the most handsome
creature I have seen -on the Australian stage during
-.0 years. « Nance’s figure is now finely proportioned, and
her statuesque Marie was as nicely balanced as a sculptor’s
art could desire. Of the acting—aids,! If the star had a
say in selecting her people, she deserves to be judged bv
the company she keeps j and'she is.

Thus Prof. A. W. Ward : “ A peculiar danger beset our
earlier dramatists in consequence of the usage allowing
full license of comic extravagance to the clown, whose
ambition it was to say very much more than was set down
for him.” The clown is a musical-comedian nowadays,
and most of the later dramatists, resigned to his exuber-
ance of gags, leave him practically to find the words of his
part as well as speak them. Hence the scarcity of genuine
wit in musical plays since Gilbert ceased writing.

About Mummers.
“ Melb.” : I don’t object to Bland Holt keeping a natty
gig, but only to his driving with both hands,
Maud MacCarthy, the Australian violinist, was asked
by an English duchess, the other day, to her At Home,
to play a little, and “she would be glad to pay her ex-
penses, two guineas. n Maud did not go into that garden.
As showing that they understand things better in the
U.S.A. : Mr. J. M. Sears, of Boston, had the violinist to
play at his place and next day sent a letter of thanks and
500 dollars.
Bert Gilbert, remembered in Australia as the musical
comedy colleague (and husband) of Ada Reeve, is now a
“turn” at the variety halls. There isn’t much call
nowadays for frivollers who are not able to provide their
own little show.
Cuyler Hastings is now leading man with Mme. Bertha
Kulicb a New York tragedienne who made her name in
Yiddish plays. America possesses a number of theatres
where the plays are given in Yiddish, and most of the
popular dramas are turned into Ghetto language. Mme.
Kulich now speaks English on the stage, so the cool,
calm Cuyler need not learn to be imperturbable in Zang-
willese.
II slbourne Age’s London Jeames tells of Melba’s birth-
dav reception ; duchesses in the hall, marchionesses in
the doorway, and mere countesses out on the mat. The
reception followed a dinner party. . . . Madame Melba
disappointed her public at Covent Garden on the follow-
ing evening on the plea of indisposition. What I
“ There is not another place in the world where the
spirit of routine has such narrowness, strength and
tenacity as in the theatre.” —Fvctncisque Sarcey .
T.P.’s Weekly tells a Patti story. Stagno, a rival tenor
to Patti’s first husband, Nicolini, packed the theatre at
Barcelona in 1885 with persons paid to hiss Nicolini and
Patti The performance was spoiled, and Patti threatened
to leave the town next day. The astute manager asked a
society friend for all the visiting cards left at his house.
With the help of his secretary he wrote under the titled
names every kind of delicate message (the handwriting
varied in every case). “ Bird, do not fly away. The
scoundrels ! are they deaf? ” “To hiss Patti is to offend
God.” “It is enough to make one blush at being a
Spaniard.” These were left at intervals during the day
at the hotel of the singer. Late in the afternoon the
manager called, ostensibly to beg once more for a change
of decision. He was met by a radiant creature. My
dear, we stay, w.e stay.”
The -latest looping the loop business is done in
Paris by a girl in a motor-car. Only a question of time
when some one will try it on roller skates, or on foot—or
possibly on his head.
“ The Worst Woman in London ” and “ The Greatest
Scoundrel Living’’have found a suitable companion in
melodrama titles. At a London suburban theatre The
Worst of All Women” has been produced. Anderson
must give that a chance.
“B.” : I had an experience last week. I met an old
friend outside the theatre, and duly proceeded to the
nearest bar to discuss old times. Later on we adjourned
to the theatre, where the “ Scalded Back” was wending
its way. On our entrance hundreds of double-faced and
double-bodied people met my gaze, and two “leading
ladies,” dressed exactly alike, screeched at the same time
as two Paolo Macaris thrust forth two bright daggers. I
motioned my friend outside to explain. He suggested
“ bed.” Went to my room, and for fear of extraordinary
possibilities, sought my revolver. There in the drawer
lay two beautiful “ guns.” Examined them, and for fear
of accident, dropped one. They both fell. I took off
four boots that night, and then the two floors stood
up in front of me. Mext morning my friend’s first word 3
were, “ You were ‘ loaded’ last night.” I told him he was
a slanderer.
“ Yes,” said - the advance-agent, “I write my own
advance pars. It gets pretty stale after a few months, I
can tell you. You express things in all sorts of ways till
there’s no other way left, and then yer have ter lie, but
pretty soon yer run out o’ lies. T’ make uphes about
plays' being produced 2,000,000 nights and all that sort o
thing is easy ; so’s yarns about actors. But th rottenest
work I ever had was when I was ahead for a bioscope
show I did get stale. I stuck t’ the truth as long as I
could and then I lied about the cost of films, and the
expense in getting them, and so on. I soon ran out, and
I had to start on the operator. He was a timid sort o
chap and ’ad never been out of Australia in his
life but I said he had seen much active warrare,
and bore marks of wounds that he had received
when getting the pictures. One shot had gone right
through his left arm, I said, and he carried in his right
shoulder a bullet that doctors had never been able to
abstract. In the Boer war he had been a prisoner for five
months, had had enteric six times, and his life had been
despaired of on many occasions. I made him famous in
the rural districts, and when we arrived at a place near
the border of N.S.W. (on the Vic. side), the local paper
sent a man to interview him (for they had got wind of his
fame), and asked him to recount his experiences. He wa*
shv and backward (as I said before), and wasn’t very ready
at lying, and became terribly embarrassed ; but next week
the paper appeared with the interview, and he was spoken
of as an unassuming young man, who would not talk about
his own experiences. I’m advance-agent for a whiiligig
now, and that doesn’t need much newspaper comment.
“N.” : In the second part of “ Faust,” Wagner creates
the new man, the Homunculus, thus forestalling what
radium is coming to. There’s a time-honored song in
Ireland. A venerable woman, what the Scotch call a
howdy by profession, sings about Dan O’Connell

Find the folks go on the ould way.
Pacific Ocean Dramatic Co., doing the provinces (for as
much as it possibly could), put in a one-night season
at Two Forks. Its talented members were producing
“ Hans the Boatman” under an alias, probably because
they did not desire to attract the attention of the pro-
prietor of the Australian rights. “ Hans the Boatman ”is
a lugubrious dog drama, in which the blind “ Yarinan
hero is towed round by a tall tyke. Pacific Ocean co.’s
dog was an athletic animal of the color of fried liver, and
he was not to be trifled with. The blind hero was
endeavoring to get a demonstration of devotion out of the
dog but the insurrectionary beast resented his impor-
tunities, and, instead of fawning, grappled with flans,
and bit him in the arm. Hans broke out m coarse, Anglo-
Saxon oaths, and kicked the faithful dog. The dog
feinted, got into holts, and tore all the back part out of
the poor blind man’s unmentionables, and the shrieking
audience had one mad moment of delirious joy as Hans
fled the scene, with his devoted friend hanging on to the
tail end of things and hauling back all he knew. The
tug-of-war was won by the dog. He broke away with
half of the hero’s under-garment in his mouth just as Hans
reached the cover of the wings.
A musician, recently engaged to conduct an amateur
charity performance, was told by one of the lady-pro-
moters : “ We want, you know, to have nothing but really
qood music.” The'conductor thought on Beethoven,
Schubert, Wagner, and then on the resources of his
orchestra, with considerable misgivings. But the lady
relieved his anxiety by adding : “ Something out of The
Orchid,’ for instance, if you could manage it. He said
he could.
“J.”: Some of the effects of “Empire Days,” St.
George’s Societies,” and the like : I dropped in the other
night to see “Bill Adams,” and liked the show right
enough up to where May Beatty sings “Tommy, Hesa
Dandy ” and shows divers flags at the end of the
The “ Harp ” got a good reception ;so did the Thistle,
while the “ Union Jack ” bulged the premises with ap-
plause. Then came the Australian flag, and a dead damp
- silence in the pit, while from the stalls a few faint cheers
crawled upwards into the atmosphere and sneaked out
through the ventilators like shamed caterpillars. I was
puzzled as to the reason of this until a man leant over to
me and spoke : “ Say, mate, what was that bloonnn flag
they showed last ? You know, the one with the stars on
it.” You could have knocked me over with a traction
engine.
Alice Hollander, who went to England with a view to
putting Ada Orossley’s nose out of joint, is now a star
turn at the London Tivoli. “ The charming Australian
vocalist” has a 44 weeks engagement, and will sing 19
weeks at the Tivoli before going to another hall. Who
Wouldn’t be a singer on the upper-class variety stage f
The salary is liberal, and the work is light. Miss Hol-
lander is timed to appear at 9.55 p.m., and the following
turn comes on at 10.5. Ten minutes a night—one hour
per week. No travelling. No “frequent changes of
programme.” The same ballad, or two ballads, including
• the°encore, will do duty for a month at least, The life
of a first-class concert artist is full of toil and trouble, as
compared with that of a variety-hall vocalist of Alice
Hollander’s quality.
On the first night of “ Walls of Jericho,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, the long wait between the third and fourth
acts was relieved by a roar of laughter from the amphi-
theatre, suggesting that somebody had done something
funny. A pair of sweethearts were the unconscious per-
formers. They had seats in the front row, and their
mutual admiration was specially marked, even for lovers at
the play. For about four minutes the amphitheatre silently
watched the worshippers as they drank to one another
with their eyes, and thrilled their respective beings with
hand-squeezes and shoulder-contacts. Finally the feel-
ings of Adolphus compelled him to kiss the maiden, and
the shrieking audience demanded an encore. It is a curi-
osity in human nature this invariable ridiculing of real
love scenes in the auditorium even by women who have
come prepared to take a serious view of stage-kisses.
Feminine sentimentality has a sense of ridicule in witness-
ing any emotional display which isn’t tricked out with
theatrical effects. The art of love-making is histrionic.
“ Melb.” : I fancy that Brough’s greatest delight over
the production of the “ Walls of Jericho ” was in the real
doors of the wall interiors. Don’t we all know the stage
door, the canvas one ? First it won’t open, and then it
insists on remaining agape to show you the behind scene.
A certain Hamlet in this city could not have his real door,
but the grand feature of his performance was a real lock
and key.

’em on the list ” does not appear in the collected edition
of Gilbert’s plays? ' Didn’t Gilbert write the patter song,
or did he write it and afterwards come to regard it as a
pinchbeck patch spoiling the “ Mikado ” libretto ?

Tamarama Beach.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE waves are dancing in the sun,
A jewelled crown has each ;
Their raiment is of silver spun—
On Tamarama Beach.
Upon a far-off summer day
We sat on this grey stone;
Now you are half the world away,
And I sit here alone.
What made the pebbles jewels rare?
What turned the sands to gold ?
The Prince of Fantasy was there,
In those fine days of old.
The wild flowers bloomed in fashion brave,
The breeze made music sweet,
And Eros, on a crested wave,
Rode laughing to your feet.
Your hair was like a shining veil
Around your shoulders spread ;
Your proud young face was rosy-pale,
And, oh, your lips were red.
A wreath of smoke rose from the hill;
A sail shone far at sea;
And, in that scene so calm and still,
There were but you and me.
The sun made on the ocean floor
A pathway broad and bright;
The Future shone our eyes before.
Like that long lane of light.
Methought I saw, as we stood there,
The noon of your renown,
When you, as Queen of Song, would wear
A rose-and-laurel crown.
For you strange flags would be unfurled
In cities old and new,
And you would sing for all the world
The songs I wrote for you.
And while I saw that vision rare,
That would be truth in time,
I wove for you a garland .fair
Of many colored rhyme.
What glamor had come over me ?
What strange spell magical ?
It was the Prince of Fantasy
Who held me in his thrall.
I looked into your eyes of brown,
And I saw clearly there
That you, too, saw the Singer’s Crown
Which, some day, you should wear.
My vision was a Prophecy,
And part of it came true.
Your name is known from sea to sea—
And I am dead to you.
Your voice turns Winter into Spring,
They say ; so let it be.
I only know the songs you sing,
They were not made by me.
You are a Queen of Song sublime —
So I have often read —
While I am a poor Prince of Rhyme,
Long disinherited.
Enough ! who cares for song or rhyme ?—
I’ve had my share of each :
I knew you in your sweetest time
On Tamarama Beach.
• N.S.W.
CREEVE ROE.
“The Starvers.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A few months before the break-up of
the drought, I was riding in company
with several other “sweaters,” members
of a Melbourne club, down the Ferntree Gully
road. Tiring of persistent attempts to lure me
into a race, I turned off into a side road at
Scoresby, and biked slowly along, hardly disturb-
ing the thick, gray dust, and enjoying the abso-
lute peace of the scene.
I passed farms that nestled in ancient orchards,
modern cottage-villas, each with the original slab
habitation still lurking in the rear—even in its
sombre decay more suitable than the “ornate,”
pink-pine-wood, undersized upstart residence that
so barefacedly stood between its old gray homeli-
ness and the beauty-seeking eyes of the wayfarer.
Presently I was made aware of the fact that I was
now five miles from Ferntree Gully, thanks to the
remains of a L. V. W. board, which had apparently
served as a target to many a gay bucolic sportsman.
Looking towards Bayswater I caught a splendid
glimpse of the Dandenong Ranges, and far around
me Spread the green and prosperous farms.
Then I came on tracks of travelling sheep,
where they had entered the road by a lane; and,
topping a slight rise, I saw the dust cloud, and
heard the fretful, protesting sound of driven
animals. Nearer me, and following the flock, was
a tilted cart, the tilt soiled into a yellowish white
and patched with sacking. Tied on to the back-
board by the shafts, was an old and marvellously
mended American buggy, piled with, what ap-
peared to me, a wild, dirty and useless tangle of
rubbish. Old bags hung limply over the sides;
billies and buckets rattled emptily beneath. But in
the pile of bedding and wearing apparel, tumbling
and heaped upon the floor (there were no seats), re-
clined the saddest and strangest hospital I ever saw.
I had got off my machine and was walking, so I
had time to observe these details. There was a
sheep, Sitting on its haunches as far as I could
make out—old and hoary it looked, with its long,
nartow face set in the woolly frame, and the great
yellow glass-like eyes staring. Leaning against
its Side was a boy of about 12. Dark moist wisps
of hair stained his white forehead j below his eyes,
which were heavy with illness, his face was sun-
burnt to a bright copper color, and his neck was
the same, but the white of the brow was repeated
where the unfastened shirt fell open on his breast.
I heard a warning growl before I noticed the sharp
alert face of a sheep-dog, with angry eyes watch-
ing me It crouched against his knees as the boy
held one of its paws in his thm nerveless hand
pasture.
The other arm was thrown over the sheep s neck,
either for support or from*came running

back towards the cart, hmpmg, with tong

of heartsickness ran cold over me. Throug
deep grass paddocks, by running, shady stiearns,
in sunlit beauty and cool breeze this brute of
revolting aspect and hanging lip unload
drunkenly past in his harness, dragging his load
° f A U littlTfurther on a man drove before him a
few hundred sheep, not white hke happy, grass
land sheep, not even gray, but grimed with the
red dust of the famine country they bad just
quitted. One ran aside into the logs and brush-
wood. The dog saw it, and tried to run it out,
but it kept along the barbed wire fence,, having
long streamers of wool in its track, and I J. j*
to jump a log, fell and lay mutely where it fell.
The dog bit at it weakly. Both animate were
exhausted. The man came back to them, sent the
dog away to his work and lifted the sheep by l
fleece, set it on its feet, and pushed it gently a few
steps to try its strength. It seemed distressed,
baa’d feebly, and fell forward heavily. In a sud
den passion of anger he kicked at it savagely, an
a curse that was almost a cry broke from him.
Then I did an unexpected thing, I offered my
services. I had a holiday, I explained, and Icl
done a bit of droving, too, long ago. Starvers;
I asked, and the bearded man laughed nastily,
looking me up and down. I looked back at t e
approaching caravan, and he looked too, wistfully,
and thought of his sick. Then he called up the
dog and introduced me, and, without anothei
word, took the bicycle as I pushed it toivards him,
and waited for the carts while I went on to my
new duties,
After the first hour the task grew wearisome,
and more and more wearisome. The dog: ‘ loafed
on me,” running inside the fences and hedges and
lying in the deep grass, or running under the
bridges and culverts to cool himself in the puddles.
I hardly looked back. Now and aga.n a sheep
would fall, and, helped up, would struggle on. A
lamb or two I let go, and no objection was
made from the rear. Once or twice the carts
stopped, but I plodded on, allowing the sheep
to gather as much food as they could. Here and
there I yelled and ran and bullied, in the new
excitement of a corner or a broken fence.
Toivards evening a few vehicles passed me,
and once some happy picnickers screamed with
laughter at the sight of the broken-kneed old
horse dragging the two carts, and the sick sheep
sitting up in the bedding. They addressed many
humorous remarks to me, gave me the address of
an imaginary boiling-down establishment, and
otherwise cheered me on my way. I had a
camping-place in my mind’s eye, and determined
to push on to that before I stopped. As I began
to hurry on the flock of skeletons a feeling of
nightmare grew upon me. The backs of my legs
ached and my heart ached, and I Avas most deadly
hungry, and the long shadoAvs began to bar the
long, white road. Then came the top of
the hill I had been walking for, and by-and-
bye the gentle descent and the green grass
in the reserve, bordered by the thick, dark,
bushy tea-tree scrub. Plenty of wood, plenty of
feed ; the only thing necessary for perfect security
all night, a couple of rails across the bridge, a
couple across the road at the foot of the hill, and a
good night’s rest for all. The setting sun sim-
mered in soft haze on the horizon, tall white gums
drooped over lagoon and running water, reeds
rustled, birds twittered their final songs, the
flocks spi ead out resting and nibbling, the dog lay
on his side in a shallow pool, and the low-lying
bank was just the place for the old horse to drink.
I ran about like a boy, collecting sticks and fallen
strips of bark, and waited impatiently for the
others. A small bush boy drove his cows along
the road, and I captured him and promised him
fabulous prices if he ivould return as promptly as
possible with fresh milk. It was like a fairy tale,
and I was to be the good fairy.
Then they came, tottering slowly down the
slope, between straggling hedges and wattles, lhe
man held the horse’s head to help him, and
pushed back the shafts to ease the breeching. I
hurried towards him. His face was stern and
gray, the mouth fixed in a line that showed the
teeth were clenched within it. He pulled up and
began to unhitch the harness. I gave a nervous
glance round to see if anything had happened to
the rear cart, but the boy was rousing and begin-
ning to look interested. When. the horse drew
out of the shafts, the man got up into the cart and,
pulling the flap back, disappeared inside. Again
I heard the baby’s wailing cry, and then he hamded
a little bundle down to me. I laid it gently on
the ground and bent forward to assist, if need be,
its mother to dismount.
The m&n was kneeling on the floor, by the side
of a still figure, his hands closely clasping hers
over her breast. Her head was toward me, the
heap of loosened black hair pillowing it. It was
the face of the boy, only a mother face, sharpened
and white with suffering. The man looked up at
me; once'or twice he tried to speak, but the
words caught in his husky throat. Then he
moistened his cracking lips, and muttered with a
horrible difficulty : “Dead, my poor girl, at last
she’s dead, and so often she’s almost gone and has
come back to me.” He went on uttering dis-
jointed sentences. .
“She was alive when I passed, I said. I
heard her speak.”
“Yes, then she was. I gave her a drink after
I went back, and stayed with her a while, and let
her see the boy through the curtain, and »he spoke
to him, and —and all that, but she’s gone.”
That night the baby died too, and in the morn-
ing we went on to town with our sad load, while
the remnant of the flock fattened in a fresh
ROKE.
It is suggested as a means of checkmating
the devious Chow —who, it is said, is evading the
immigration laws by landing somewhere on the
coast between Port Darwin and the Gulf and
making across to the Queensland sugar country—
that legislation on the lines of a. recent Natal law
be passed. The Natal Act provides that Chinese
residents must present themselves each year to
t@,ke out a certificate and prove their claim to it.
The certificate, which may be demanded at any
time by the authorities, must always be carried by
its holders, and failure to produce it when called
for renders the Chinaman liable to deportation.
Transference of certificates is provided against by
a finger-print system.

press that the British Government stopped at the
Customs books by Boothby, Mrs. Henry Wood
and others because they were printed in Dutch ;
and that a resolute effort is being made to stamp
out the use of the Dutch language altogether—
after the manner of the Russians in Poland.

I believe that there are only two things that can save
us from presently having to take a back seat both as an
Imperial and a commercial Power. Those two things are
Protection and Conscription. Sugar-coat them if you like
to make them more palatable, but these are two of the
pills that will have to be swallowed before John Bull can
be restored to his former healthy condition.
