Dis-Missed.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HERE’S a rhyme that must be turned,
Ere the rhymer’s rest is earned,
And he turns him into bed to take his
wages.
But the midnight lamp is low,
And the bardlet’s pen runs slow
And unsteadily across the virgin pages.
There’s a thought that must be thunk,
On the sleepless side of bunk,
And the rhymelet must condense the thoughlet’s
vapor;
So the pen must cease to jerk,
And the brainlet get to work,
And precipitate a gemlet on the paper.
Oomes a thoughticule like this—
That the things we never miss
Are the things we all go missing, never knowing;
Thus, the smiles that die unborn,
And the flowers that fade forlorn,
When the world would be the brighter for their
blowing.
Then the friends we never meet,
Hidden gold beneath our feet,
Dews of morning that are mist before we miss
them;
And the light behind the stars,
And the mirth they have in Mars,
And the girls that grow to grandmas ere we kiss
them.
If we missed the smiles, the flowers,
And the friends that ne’er were ours,
And the nuggets and the dewdrops and the
kisses
That elude us day by day,
We should go our weary way
Inhabiting a hell of little misses.
When we miss them we shall know
That we’re dead and gone below,
And that all is over—bar what may be under.
Then we’ll see, without a glass,
All the things that used to pass,
And we 11 figure out the blame for every blunder.
Stop ! The thoughtlet will escape
Ere we’ve knocked it into shape.
Hurry, brainlet! for the flame grows dim and
dodgy.
Since we’re ready to declare
That there is a thinklet there,
Like the potter, we must thump it while it’s
stodgy.
How the bardlet’s soul is vext!
It has struck a musty text,
When it started for the sky—perdition take it!
And the pen it sticks like glue
In the moral, trite and true,
That the world is just as narrow as we make it.
N.S.W.
WAIF.
“The Cries of the Wounded.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
voice of Big Nose, the Yankee, is
| louder than a whisky still; it is full of bad
stories and strange catalogues of sound.
But there is a certain dignity about his voluminous
nose that disarms criticism. As a sapper and line-
surveyor he saw service with the U.S. army in the
Philippines. He admits that shell-fire at close
range made his teeth fall out, while the sight of a
dead comrade standing upright in a rifle-pit at
El Caney permanently affected the color of his
hair.
“Guess the Merikin and British war corres-
pondents know how to handle thatyer ‘ cry of the
wounded’ song. ‘All night we heard it in the
hills.’ Nuff to give a soldier man dry feet. Still,
sonny, a nickel bullet planted in the waistcoat
will tune a fat man up to C-sharp in one second.
You don’t know how many nerves a fat corporal
has until the lead strikes him.
“ I like good shootin’ from the other side. Bad
shootin’ hits the harmless mule and the parson,
and the red-faced tenderfoot when he’s carrying
the beer to the boys in the firing-line. No civilised
infantryman will shoot grog.
“Good shootin’. Waal, sonny, Pve seen a
scrap of smoke break right on the sky-line, then a
thin red poker of flame lick out at us. ‘ Right on
the eye,’ sez the corporal. Hoosh ! You can’t
duck from a shell. It’s a great treat. Splosh !
Milk cans and flying horse shoes. Whoop !
“ ‘Who spoke last?’ sez the corporal, looking
over us. Big MGinty lying in the wet looked
queer. He was smiling, holding a fistful of mud
in each hand. ‘ln me left side, corporal. Doesn’t
hurt a pinch,’ he said.
“ Thing like an oyster shell sticking out of his
ribs. ‘No pain,’ he says; ‘jest a holy calm.’
Then, sonny, I sees the blue of his eye turn grey,
a streak of pain seemed to rip across his face.
For a second and a half he held himself, then we
got our first man-scream, and it made us sick as
children. A regiment of howling women wouldn’t
hurt, but the whining of full-grown men is hell in
your ears.
“Yes, sonny, wounded men cry and howl; it’s
the top-note in a bombardment. I’ve hung by the
breech of a firing gun to try and lose a particular
howl. Some howls get hold of you. One man
with a bayonet-rip will dog-yelp all night; another
will smother up and croon a packet of nursery
rhymes. But men don’t weep. You can divide
it into a grunt and a scream. And they kick each
other like horses.
“ There’s the hero-man, who isn’t supposed to
grunt--the man with the golden brow and the high
hair. The band isn't playing when tfie bullet gets
him; and the bullet knocks the pose away, and
leaves him a clawing mass of twopenny yells.
There’s one lone and sorrowful thing about a dead
man ; you can’t offer him any of your breakfast.
“Get your wou..ded out of the way. They
destroy your aim and they turn your liver to ice.
Gueoo the Baltic men knew something when they
slung their bad cases to the deep.
“Gahdedam! The Yurrupean Tommy is the
best howler in mankind. His stomach is soft; he
is moist at the neck. The Yankee soldier is dry
behind the ears. You bring a man to me and say,
‘ This yer man is a Spartan. ’ I say, ‘ Yessir ;l .et
me feel his neck.’
“When they first put you on the firing-scratch
you breathe Sheol and a bullet in your first 10
breaths. Then your heart sits up like a woman,
and waits for the bit of lead to come and smash
her window.
“Tell you what, sonny—but don’t say I’m an
American citizen—the boys don’t like the wounded.
In hospital, when the long-haired reporter is look-
ing for copy, the wounded lie on pillows and every-
body plays tenderfoot, and sings ‘ Carry Me Back
to Dixie.’ But on firing scratch, it’s different.
Jt ou lie on your chest, waiting for the first rush of
the nigger insurrectionary. They are moving
through the cane towards you—moving, moving.
1 hero s a patter of black feet in your heart, a smell
in the air. Two things will happen. First, a bit
of cane will bend politely, then a long machete
wipes down at .Toe, and over he goes. The cane
bows again, straightens, and all is quiet. You
can hear that quietness ; you can almost taste the
blood licking the grass.
“ Now, if Joe is dead, he is a good sort of a lad,
but, if he is going to wake up and talk about his
wound and ask for drinks, the company wants to
poison him right away. There are men who
would be glad to kill him with a gun-butt. The
man who sits up after being hit and makes dog-
music over his cut is a blight on the army. I’ve
seen a company wiped out because it let a wounded
man howl, and his noise smothered the ‘creep
U P ( °f 500 Filipinos through the cane.
Now, sonny, this hand of mine is shaking:
put a coin in it. There’s a white face staring at
me from a rifle pit at El Caney. Whiskv is the
am Pr V nl^'on kid that dead man with.
. “ Thanks, sonny, but don’t think that the fight-
ing man is no good. He likes to think of death as
a white angel, quiet and peaceful; but when he
counts the crescendos that Hog Mullins arrives at
before turning it up he feels that death is only a
barnyard opera. Good night.”
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
Fly Monte.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE total stranger lay down in the doubt-
ful shade of the scratching board and fought
the flies. After ten minutes he admitted
himself beaten, and sauntered over to where I sat,
accompanied by a flock of the energetic creatures.
They seemed to have taken a fancy to him.
“ Flies pretty bad ? ” I ventured.
“Naw ! ” said he contemptuously. “ This ain’t
nuthin’ to wot they was up my way—Kennilwilla.
Why, at Brumby’s pub, they was that bad that at
meal-times y’d ’ave ter cut yer way in an’ then ’old
outer yer tucker with one hand, an’ fight ’em with
t other. Funny thing about ’em was that they
never come near but at meal-times. Then, d’reckly
ther feed-bell sounded, y’d ’ear a whirr o’ wing's,
an’ big, black clouds ’ud come up from all sides’
till sometimes it got so dark with ’em we’d ’ave
ter light ther lamp at mid-day. Brumby once
tried not ringin’ ther bell, an’ it fooled ’em fer
alf a day, but when they did fall to it
they wuz that mad they near ate ther township
Next we tried puttin’ the clocks on a hour
or two, but it were no good ; them there critters
just timed it by the sun, an’ turned up pungshal
as a shearer on pay-day. Say, mate, if you’s goin’
ter shout, come over to Donn’ly’s booth ; they sell
pisen over ’ere.”
Ihe “shouting” was entirely his suggestion,
but I allowed myself to be led off to the adjoining
booth, where the stranger called for a “rum-an’-
pep’mmt.” Then he led the way back to our
former seats, and, having chased out the flies,
which had usurped the position (he did it with
much picturesque language and a red bandana), he
resumed his seat and his doubtful narration.
“ Now this ’ere ’orse-racin’ is excitin’ enuf, no
doubt, but ’ave y’ ever played ‘Fly Monte’ ? No l
Well, I’m th’ inventor. The most excitin’est game
as ever was ! Y’ must know that Brumby got up a
case o’ flypapers an’ uster make a curtin of ’em,
an’ ’ang it in the doorway. I tell y’, fren’, it kept
six on us goin’ all the time changin’ them curtins
an’ draggm’ ’em out to the bongfire at the back.
The screams of the flies wot got themselves caught
was dreadful to ear. Ihe way the game was
played was this: —We’d git in the dinin’-room an’
drive all ther flies out, an’ block up ther chimney
an’ winder an’ door an’ cracks an’ key-’ole. Then
we’d sit round ther table, each with a fly-paper in
fronter ’im, an’ the referee ’ud loose six selected
flies outer a match-box, an’ ther man wat got one
on ’is paper fust ’ud take ther pool. Talk about
excitement! A fly ’ud come ’overin’ round’ yer
paper tryin’ to make up ’is mind where to land,
an’ then maybe ’e’d change ’is mind an’ light out
acrost the room an’ fool round coquettish like,
pertendin’ ’e wuz goin’ to settle, till we was near
dead with suspense.
..“.We'd bin playin’ the game ’bout a month with
various success, when Brumby begun to ’ave a
marvell’us run o’ luck. Miraculous it were—agin
natur’ so ter speak. Well, we watched fer ’im
pretty close, an then one time, when the referee
sed ‘ Go, ’ we noticed Brumby opin ’is fist caushus-
like under the table an’ out ’ops a big, black fly.
It ’overed roun’ an’ ioun’ till Brumby give a little
whistle, when down it swoops straight on to ’is
paper. Then Brumby ’e collected an’ left the
room, takm’ ’is fly-paper along with ’im. I crept
arter ’im very quiet and there, at the back, I seed
’im tenderly washin’ that fly orfer the paper with
warm water.
“ Well, I squashed ther animal wi’ me paw—the
fly I mean—an’, I ’sure y’, Brumby mourned over
that there insect wusser nor ’e did wat time ’is
missis died.
“ We let the little incident pass, so ter speak,
an’ ‘ Fly Monte ’ went on as usual. Imajine ’ow
we felt when, arter a while, Brumby begun to win
agin ! Couldn’t go wrong no ’ow. Now Brumby
was a large size in Irishmen, ’sides bein’ th’ on’y
publican within a ’undred mile, so we daren’t
cTend him, so ter speak. But I tell y’ we watched
an’ watched till we near strained our eyesight,
but couldn’t see nuthin’ crook no ’ow. All this
time Brumby jist kept on winnin’ an’ winnin’
We tried leavin’ orf the game, but if y’d ever
played it, y’d know ’ow ’opeless that were— like
leavin’ orf ther drink.
“ Then one night we cotched ’im. ’Twas the
las’ pool o’ th’ ev’nin’ with a big stake, an’ excite-
ment was ’igh. The flies was very shy, an’ kept
themselves to themselves, so ter speak. We
could see Brumby was lookin’ anxious an’ sur-
prised, when we ’ears a terrible buzzin’ an’ hulla-
buloom’ from th’ ceilin’. We all looked up an’
there, caught in a spider’s web, was one of’’em
little coekshaver beetles. At that moment ’e
broke loose, stopped ’is cursing, an’ noiseless as a
shadder stieaked acrost to th* lamp, roun J which
the six flies wuz congregated, so ter speak. ’E
